O, my children, now I have completed this book for you, which is the last of the medical sciences. By its completion, by what it contains, and by its explanations and clarifications, in my view, I believe I have fulfilled your love for this treatise, which pertains to the practice that lies with me, namely surgery.
For the practice that lies with me is almost entirely deprived in our region and at our time, except that there is a slight trace of its science, and its appearance remains. We have nothing but small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which mislead people into error and hesitation regarding its principles and weaken its strength and the art itself.
And it seems to me that I should revive this through the order of this treatise according to the way of exposition and explanation and abbreviation; and so that it comes in the forms of cautery tools and other instruments used in the work as indicated by the explanation, and for its preparation, which is a necessary cause.
But the reason why a good practitioner is not found in surgery at this time is that the art of medicine is extensive, and one must be well versed in anatomy before being practiced, which Galen described as understanding the functions of the limbs and their forms, and the connection and separation of them; having knowledge of the bones, nerves, and muscles, and their numbers and distribution; and of the pulsating veins and the stationary ones, and the places where they are found.
And for this reason Hippocrates said that in name it is much, but in practice little, and especially in the art of surgery.
This we have already said at the beginning of this book; for whoever is not well versed in what we have said about anatomy will inevitably fall into error through which men may die, as I have seen too many of them, for some are formed in this science and get themselves out of that without knowledge and experience.
For I saw a foolish doctor who had incised an abscess on the neck of a woman, and he cut some arteries in the neck so that much blood flowed out, causing the woman to die quickly.
And I saw another doctor who thought he would remove a stone from a man who was suffering from kidney stones, and the stone was large. He came to him and pulled out that stone along with a piece of the bladder, whereupon that man died on the third day.
And I was called to remove that stone and saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I knew for certain that he would die.
And I saw another doctor who was being paid by a duke of our land to perform surgery on a black castrated man who had a fracture in his thigh. Due to the doctor's ignorance, he exposed the fracture over the wound with splints and bandages in a strong structure but did not allow the wound to breathe.
And he untied it according to his desires and then bandaged it again. After some days, he commanded that we loosen the binding; and thus, between loosening and reapplying it, the thigh and foot became abscessed, leading to his ruin.
And I was called to attend him, and I prepared to loosen the bandage. Once it was loosened, he found relief from his pain and tranquility.
However, once the corruption was confirmed in the member, since it could not be restrained, and it did not cease to spread the corruption throughout the member until the patient was in danger.
And I saw another surgeon who lanced a cancerous abscess, and it became ulcerated after about ten days, until the malignancy worsened greatly under his lord's supervision.
The cause of which is that the cancerous part is free from melancholic humor; therefore, it does not need to reach all parts of the body, but rather remains in the member that has suffered and hosted it.
And for this reason, my children, it is necessary for you that the operation be planned in two aspects: one is an operation associated with health, and the other is a procedure involving significant risks.
And I have already stated this in every place in this book where an operation involves both error and fear; therefore, it is necessary for you to heed this and avoid that, so that the pages do not reproach me for speaking and leading you astray.
And thus take up your weapons with diligence and the protection of God, and treat your patients with skill and firmness.
And use the best path by God's grace to lead to health and praiseworthy success, and avoid terrible diseases that are difficult to heal, and remain armed against what causes you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and name does not evade you: for it is better to remain in your glory, and more so in this world and ultimately for your sanctity.
Wound treatment in some monastic surgeries: Do not treat illness badly, for thus bad doctors are not named.
And I have dedicated this book according to three chapters.
The division of the book begins, and first of the first part. The first chapter is ordered concerning the head and its treatments, in the forms of the instruments and in the tools of cautery, and of what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, concerning bleeding and punctures, and of cupping, and of plasters, and of extracting arrows and similar objects, fully chaptered and ordered regarding the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, concerning restoration and dislocation, and the treatment of fractures and similar conditions; a chapter ordered regarding the head and its treatments, and of the forms of the instruments.
I shall speak of the quality of the movements of that cautery and of its precautions, and in what condition cautery should be prohibited.
Therefore, I say that the discourse on the quality of the movements of cautery and its precautions is long and extensive, subtle by science, and hidden as a secret; many wise men have spoken about it, and they are often at odds with each other, but for me a little of their discourses suffices to avoid prolongation.
Therefore, I say that cauteries apply universally to all formations, either material or immaterial, with the exception of two formations which are tail-like and immaterial.
In the tail-like and dry formation that is material, it dries out; for some say that cautery is helpful in such cases, while others say the opposite, that cautery is not appropriate for a disease that is due to heat and dryness, since the nature of fire is heat and dryness, and it is inappropriate to treat a tail-like and dry condition with something that is hot and dry.
And those who say the opposite are wrong, because cautery is beneficial in all tail-like and dry diseases that occur in human bodies. When you compare the condition of the human body and its humidity to the nature of fire, you find that the human body is fragile.
And I say according to the words of that person, that experience has already shown this to me on several occasions.
But I do not intend that it should be upon this for one who has already been trained and exercised in the chapter of cautery by previous practice, and who has known the diversity of human formations and the disposition of diseases in their seasons, and the causes thereof, and their accidents, and the duration of time.
But in other formations there is no fear for you, especially in fragile and humid diseases, since all physicians are now in agreement about those, and they do not disagree on the benefit of cautery in such cases.
And you know, children, that the secret of healing through cautery with fire and its effectiveness is that fire is simple, and its action affects only the part being cauterized and does not harm other parts nearby except for minor damage.
Here is the English translation:
May the fire, by its sublimity and goodness of substance, do nothing but superfluify. And it is known to us through experiment and length of service in studying the art about the truth of causes; and by this I excuse myself from the length of speech. And if not that it were read in my book here paused for you secrets hidden with fire, and the quality of operation thereof in the bodies and expulsion thereof by which expels diseases, philosophical discourse demonstrative, which would be sharp in your possessions.
And remember, children, that they who neglect the time in which it is proper to perform cautery lose the best time of spring.
And I say that cautery is convenient at all times, for the harm caused by accidental time is submerged in the benefit of what is attracted by the cautery, and especially if the cautery is for pains or necessary to relieve pain, strength and inflammation, which cannot endure delay because it is feared that from those might come malice, which is worse than a small injury entering through the wrong timing.
And this does not concern your possessions, or, children, what is valued only by the common people and foolish doctors, is that for the illness which is cured by cautery, it is not remediable at all times, and they delay that consequence.
And the reason is not just as they think, for cautery is merely a substitute for medicine that alters the complexion and dries the humidity which is the cause of pain.
But may the fire, however, be used sparingly in medicine due to the speed of its value and the virtue of its operation and the greatness of its power.
It is possible that his illness may return at some time according to the condition of the patient and the location of the illness, and its nature, and what has been prepared in his body by the accumulation of superfluities in him, and what has been acquired in him through food and similar causes; but if the illness for which cautery is administered is a subtle one in a member with little superfluity and moisture, such as the cautery of a tooth for pain and similar cases, it is possible that this pain will not return, but this applies to minor cases.
And the common saying is that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, and it is said by those who specialize in it, not because those who understand it hold this view, for I believe they consider there to be no cure that benefits more than cautery.
And the cause is contrary to that; and the understanding that cautery is the last remedy of medicine is only when we administer methods of treatment for certain illnesses, and those remedies are ineffective, and at the end of this cause, cautery is administered, and it proves effective. And at this point it is said that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, not according to the understanding which I hold regarding the common misconceptions and excessive practices of foolish physicians.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except for the equality of gold and the excellence of its substance.
And they said that the location for cautery does not matter, which is not absolutely true, for I have already experienced this and have found that it works well only in certain bodies and not with others, and cautery with gold is good and better than with iron as they say; but when you perform cautery with fire using gold, it is not clearly defined to you, because the hardness of the gold changes according to the quantity you want by the heat of the fire, and because it prepares itself to receive friction; and if you adjust over it with fire, the gold melts due to the heat, and if you place some sheets in the fire for the craftsman to shape them during that process.
And thus with water is made the cautery of iron which you should apply gently and gradually according to the correctness of the procedure.
And I have already arranged this chapter on cautery after the chapters, and I have arranged those of the head among the parts, so that I relieve the inquirer asking for what they want.
The first chapter.
This cautery is applied to the cold and moisture that causes an accidental inflammation of the head, and these two causes are also the cause of lethargy and a multitude of rheumatic fluxes called corsica, from the head to the limbs and ears with fluid discharge, and a multitude of sleepiness, toothache, and gout, and generally all diseases that come from cold, such as paralysis, stroke, and similar conditions in those diseases.
The form of this cautery is as follows: first, order the patient to be evacuated with laxative medicine that cleanses the head, for three or four nights, according to what you require based on the strength of the patient and their age and habits.
And then command him to shave his head with a razor, and then place him sitting between two chairs and have him rest his elbows on his knees.
And then place the palm of your hand near the bridge of his nose between his eyebrows. But when the temperature of the middle finger has been felt, mark that place with ink, and then apply the olive wood cautery, which is made as follows:
And then pause there at the marked spot with the needle, in such a way that it descends, by which the skin may be gradually stretched, and you turn it.
And then move your hand gently and look at the place, and if you see that some part of the bone is uncovered, as much as the amount of grain in one's palm, now raise your hand, and if not return your hand to that tool or another like it, if it has cooled, until you feel the bone as I have told you.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it on the spot and let it be for three days.
And then pause on the inner edge and let it be inside until the burn scab is destroyed. And then treat the place with a quadruple ointment until it is healed.
And when it is said that if the wound heals covered, then remove the bandage, for it is better and more helpful.
And some of them say that the wound should be cauterized down to the bone, and the cautery should be held until it adheres to some part of the bone.
And then scrape what is adhered to the bone, and then treat it.
And others say that with the cautery reach to the deepest part, until it presses strongly on the bone until the bone melts, and this is the way of alpechati (cauterizing slightly). And they estimate that from that place the vapors of the head emanate.
And let the wound be dressed for a long time until it is healed and has become firm.
I have not seen these two types of cauterization on everyone, but only on some people due to a certain way of fearing; and leave that one alone, as far as I am concerned, it is better for the health when it is left alone; because the head is delicate in terms of maintaining its continuity as we have testified with other parts of the body, and even more so when the patient's constitution is cold by nature.
And the first type of cauterization is safer and better in my opinion; and from that one, health is more likely to follow.
Chapter Second.
About cauterization of the head.
When chronic and long-lasting pain occurs throughout the head and this is prolonged in the patient, even after using plasters, poultices, compresses, emplastres, ointments, and other remedies, especially if it has already been cauterized with a cautery that we have described but has not benefited from any of those causes, then consider whether the constitution of the patient's head is strong by nature; and if it is not cold and if there is no great danger, at that time one can use another type of cauterization after that one. And then cauterize it above the crown of their head with a cautery until the pain is relieved. Incise the area of pain and expose the amount of bone that we have described, then cauterize it with a cautery at the back of the head in the place called "vas," which is where the temporal artery is located. And handle it thusly in this manner, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will find great pain from that different from the pain of all other cauteries of the head.
And I shall describe this cautery in its place, for it is more delicate to use the cautery which is used for burning the horns of the head and the occiput than that with which one cauterizes the middle part of the head; and this is the form of it.
Chapter Three.
On Cauterization of Recent Abscesses.

But if you perform cauterization with a combustible medicine, take a piece of amber and scrape it, then hold the scraped ends between your fingers. And then cut the area of the pain on the temple with a scalpel until it reaches the proper location just below the skin, so that it is hidden.
And then apply pressure to it with a cloth for a continuous period, and leave it like that for three hours; then remove the cloth and place the amber on it, and leave the wound covered for two or three days; afterwards, place a poultice soaked in butter over it until the area becomes warm.
Let him be treated with those remedies which will heal him.

Chapter four.
On the cauterization of ancient ulcers.
When you treat an ulcer as we have said in the previous treatment and in the divisions of diseases, and if it does not improve, and sometimes due to the disease what has been said about the first cautery with medicine or the cautery with fire is insufficient, then you should ignite the ulnar cautery until it becomes white-hot, and place it on the site of the pain along a line that is half the length of your finger or slightly longer; and then raise your hand once and quickly apply pressure so as not to injure the artery and reach the bone.
However, you must guard against the continuation of the jawbone, which is necessary for chewing, and ensure that the tendon is not adhered and the nerves that control it are intact, so that spasm does not occur.
And if it is about the care and caution of the flow of blood from the artery that is being tied off, because there is danger in doing so, especially for one who does not know what they are doing and is not careful and experienced; and leave the operation at that point at the beginning.

Chapter X.
On the cautery for epilepsy.
An epileptic is not cauterized except when the epilepsy is due to phlegm.
And therefore, you should cleanse the brain at the beginning with decoctions and the other treatments we have described in the section when the patient is robust and can tolerate the exception of medications.
And if it is a child who cannot tolerate the medicines, then administer gargles and powder mixtures which are purified, before that for several days, with purification of the foods.

Chapter XVI.
On Cauterization of the Eyelid When the Hairs Turn Inside and Scratch the Eye.
When the hairs of the eyelid turn inward and scratch the eye, cauterization should be done in two ways: either by performing it or using a burning medication.
If you use cauterization with fire, instruct the patient beforehand to cut their hairs as those of people in the world are cut, so that they are extended and even; and if they scratch from their origin, then bandage the eyes so that it does not happen again until the hairs grow back.
Therefore, when the wound is new and inflamed, place the tip of a lancet on the head of the injury in its proper position, and then mark around the eyelids with it, making a sign like the shape of a willow leaf, and let the beginning of the sign be near the hair.
And then place a probe deeply in the eye socket or in the mucus of the eyelid.
And then apply the cautery, which has this shape.
And then cauterize over the figure you have marked, little by little, in small strokes, until the surface of the wound, which is like a myrtle leaf, is burned properly.
And the sign of health in this work is that you see the eyelid already contracted and the hair raised around the perforation of the eye.
And then raise your hand and lay it still for three days, and then place the probe around the border until the scar is eradicated. And then care for it with medication until it is healed.
But if any cause returns the hair to its original state and the eyelid is softened, then perform the cautery again on that area just as you did initially.
But if the hair remains in the eyelid downward, then cauterize that area until it is raised to its natural place and is healed so that the hair does not enter the eye.
But the cautery with a combustible medicine is one that you command the patient to pull out the hair until it grows long and healed, and then make the shape of a myrtle leaf from paper or cloth.
Take soap that is known and living oil in ounce i, or what water you have, and mix those causes well, and do it with speed so that it does not become chilled; then spread that over the sheet which is made like the shape of a myrtle leaf, and put one side on both; and place underneath the poultice completely wrapped in white cloth, and the head of the patient should be toward you.
And place the hand on the medicine and press it a little so that the patient feels the burning of the medicine, for there is a burning in it like fire.
And when the burning pain persists, then remove the medicine and apply it with your hand; when the burning stops, then remove the medicine and wash the area with water; now check if the eyelid is already lifted as it has been washed, you should then use constriction by fire or incision, for now it is good; but if not, return to applying that of the medicine on the place where you have not applied the medicine and do not let it darken until your operation is equalized and the area is constricted.
Then place on it around the border until the edge of the adjustment is eradicated, and then cure that with anointing or another method until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you to guard with diligent vigilance that no dust of the medicine falls on the wound, and if the eyelid after some days becomes softened and any cause of hair growth in the wound, then repeat your medicine on the place where the eyelid has become softened, and properly as you did at the beginning, and then around it until it is healed.
This is the form of the myrtle leaf.
And you should know that the leaves of men are diverse in magnitude and smallness, and therefore according to this your contraction is made.
And the way to rectitude will not be hidden from one who studies in this art.
Chapter XVII.
On cauterization of a fistula that is in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula as we have said in the divisions of diseases and do not confer with your treatment, then when you cauterize it according to this method: instruct the patient to place his head between your knees, and let someone assist by holding the patient's head steady between your hands in such a way that it is neither too tight nor agitated.
And then place a cotton pad soaked in egg white or in a paste of flour over his eye.
And then light the cautery, which has this form:
which is made of copper, as in the shape of a reed or an eagle's feather, with one end being the cautery.
And if you wish, it can be inserted into the other end. And if you want it to be as thin as a thread.
But the one that is curved is better for your work.
If you approach the treatment of the fistula when it is swollen, and then drain the pus and dry up that area. And if it is not swollen, then open that area and extract the pus from it.
And then proceed with cautery using one that is well-heated, and hold it close but not too much so as to avoid burning your hand or disturbing the patient, and then place the cautery on the flesh around the area and cauterize it.
And if at the beginning of your cautery you penetrate to the bone, that is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice if necessary; and leave it like that for three days, and then proceed by placing a poultice around the area and apply a drying agent until it is healed.
But if after those days it has not healed, then proceed by applying sharp and corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed, and act according to what is appropriate in that case.
And there is another method of cautery for a fistula which some earlier practitioners described: approach the location of the fistula and open it, then place in that fissure a thin piece of lead, about one ounce in quantity; hold your hand close but carefully so that the patient does not move, lest the molten lead reach the flesh; and ensure that you apply to the wound an egg white or water poultice, because the lead adheres to the location of the fistula and heals it miraculously.
And if the fistula is healed as we have described with the cautery and treatment, that is good; but if not, then it is necessary to administer nasal irrigation and reduce the fistula to its proper place in the nose.

Chapter XXVIII.
On the incision of an abscess of the testicle with a cautery.

And then return to explain to the patient and show them how to identify the rupture near the pubic bone, marking the extremity of which appears similar to half a circle at the lower parts of the body.
And then prepare the cautery, which has this form:
so that it is round and emits sparks. And then reduce the intestine or wound to its normal position, and then place your hand over the area so that the intestine does not slip out again.
And you have already placed pillows between the patient's body parts and let them lie on one pillow, with other pillows under their thighs, so that they remain lying down.
And then place the cautery over that mark with your hand on top of the cautery, and do so until you feel the pubic bone in the middle; repeat this several times if you did not succeed on the first attempt to locate it.
Observe wisely the condition of the bowel when you are about to cauterize it, that you do not burn that bowel so that death or other great harm does not come upon the patient.
And be aware that when you use the cautery, do not touch the bone, do not proceed with your operation.
And it is necessary that the cautery for children be gentle according to their age and size.
And take care of the place where you cauterized for three days, bandaging it so that the burn scar heals.
And take care of it with other ointments until it is healed.
And let the patient lie on their back for forty days until the place is sealed and the wound healed.
And it is necessary to restrict the patient's diet according to the length of the healing time for which the belly is inflamed, and that the bowel does not protrude due to the effort of healing.
And when you want to remove it after about forty days, administer it carefully and properly, and keep that in place for another forty days.
And let them be restricted from work and overeating food and drink, and from speaking loudly, for when they follow this regimen of complete healing, they will be healed.
And I will describe the treatment of ruptures in other parts of the abdomen, and at the beginning, if you do not want them to spread, then cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size, and let this be about one-third of its length; and afterward treat it as we have said, and it will heal.

Chapter XLVI.
On the Cauterization of Dislocations.
When pain occurs in some member due to falling or striking and persists for a long time without being relieved by medicine, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And the cautery for each member should be according to its size and smallness, its strength and weakness, and the location of the dislocation and pain.
If it heals with the first cautery, that is good; but if not, then repeat the cautery. For it is customary for such pains to be punctured in the member closest to where the pain is, so you must achieve this with the cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter XLVII.
On the Cauterization of Leprosy.
Some cases of leprosy are helped greatly by cauterization, but rarely when the type of leprosy is caused by the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And so when you wish to cauterize that person, consider whether the leprosy is commencing and can be cured by what we have said in the division, and if you fear for the patient that the corruption will encompass the whole body and its entire composition, then cauterize it on the head with five cauteries: one at the middle part of the head, and another further down, near the forehead; two on the temples; and one at the back, above the neck or nape.
And last of all your cautery must be applied a little inside where it presses upon the bone and makes a slight impression, so that the scales may thereby be displaced, whereby the emission of gross vapors is facilitated; and cauterize over the mucus as needed.
And if leprosy has already spread over the body and is manifestly apparent, then you should cauterize in those places with those cauteries which we have mentioned at the beginning; and one cautery on the tip of the nose, two cauteries on each cheek, two on each vertebra of the neck, six on the vertebrae of the back, a large one near the buttocks, another on the buttock itself, two on each thigh, one on each leg, two on each instep and one on each heel; and cauterize that over all the joints of his arms and feet, and on each sole of his feet, and on the creases of their hands.
And be careful of the nerves which lie above the heel so that you do not burn them.
And in some cases it is cauterized with one cautery on the bone of the little finger and another on the mouth of the stomach, and another on the liver.
And know that the more closely you apply the cauteries, the more beneficial and effective they are; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery as others do, because his body is already numb.
And it is appropriate that the cauteries be large or small according to the members and joints as required by the nature of the instruments.

Chapter XLVIII.
On cauterizing for numbness.
When a limb is numb or asleep and is treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and does not get better, then you should cauterize that numb limb with cautery according to the size of the limb or its smallness.
And let the heat of the cautery enter the depth of the skin a little. And then treat that with an ointment so that it may be healed.
And sometimes numbness is cauterized not only in the affected area but also on the bones and vertebrae of the back and on the points where the nerves that control that limb originate, and the numbness is thus alleviated.
But do not proceed with this unless you are skilled in the anatomy of the limb and the emergence of nerves throughout the moving body.
Chapter XLIX.
On cauterizing for ulcers that are necrotic.
When an old ulcer does not respond to any of the medicinal ointments, then you should cauterize over it with a cautery for a short time according to the quantity and specificity of the skin depth until the whiteness and color of it change, and its color is altered.
And then treat that with flour of eggs and lentils mixed with rose oil and arnica leaves, and the blood of a pigeon or swallow, each in part and all mixed together, and then apply it on a cloth and place it over the area until it is healed.
Chapter I.
On cauterizing ulcers.
When cancer begins and you want to remove it, then cauterize that part with a cauterizing agent in a circular manner around it as it appears.
And the wise ones say that it should be cauterized with the last cauterization in the middle part; but to me this has not been seen, for I fear that it may become ulcerated, and I have already seen this happen in some cases.
It is more prudent to cauterize it around the part in a circular manner as we have said, or rather use cautery around it.
Chapter II.
On cauterizing hemorrhoids.
When hemorrhoids are delayed in maturing due to excess moisture in that area, or because of the patient's condition, such as being old and debilitated with little blood, or simply due to time, and you want to hasten their maturation; then cauterize around them with small punctures using a cautery. After this, they will mature more quickly.
But if you want to perforate it with the cautery, then take the cautery which has this shape and place it in the middle part of the hemorrhoid until it penetrates the tissue.
And if the cautery is applied to that area further back, so that the arm remains undisturbed; then care for it until it heals.
Chapter LII.
On Cauterization with Corrosives.
A corrosive is a wandering corruption in the limb, which corrodes just as fire consumes dry wood.
Therefore, when you see the corrosive in a place where it can withstand cautery with fire, then you ignite and apply small points of cautery, and larger ones as is appropriate in the place where the corrosion is.
And proceed to cauterize it thoroughly from all sides until you eradicate the entire corrosion and leave no cause for its recurrence.
And then wash it for three days, and apply dressings over the cauterized areas with oil mixed in until all the scars are eradicated and all the corruption is gone.
And then treat it with substances that promote flesh growth.
But if after three days you see that healthy flesh is growing where there is no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cautery on what remains in the corrupted areas.
And some corrosives are treated with sharp medicines, for those are a means instead of cautery. But cautery with fire is more rapid in bringing about healing.
And I have already mentioned the treatment of it with sharp medicines in the division, and therefore use that when you find it necessary.
Chapter LIII.
On cauterization of wounds and other similar conditions.
Most times, when treating wounds in the lower part of the thigh known as "clau," which is a serious condition involving thick and deep tissue damage, one should proceed with cautery using two methods: fire cautery and hot water cautery.
The fire cautery is performed by heating the cautery instrument, which should resemble a circular shape similar to a wheel, made of iron in accordance with the size of the wound. It should have fine edges.
And then place the heated instrument on the wound, and immediately rotate your hand with the cautery around the perimeter of the wound until the cautery reaches the depth of the wound. And let it be for three days until it begins to scab over. And then apply a plaster made of crushed wild mallow mixed with salt to it, and let the plaster remain on it for one night, for this will help remove it from its roots.
And then treat the area with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
And if it does not convert, and often happens on the surface of the body, especially in the thighs and buttocks, then you should administer hot water cautery to them. This is done by taking a small tube made of iron or copper or resembling a wheel, and place it on the wound; then pour a small amount of hot water into the tube and hold it tightly while rotating it thoroughly with compression from your hand, applying the edge of the tube to the root of the wound; as the hot water flows through, wait for about an hour for the effect of the water's heat on the wound, and then remove it, for the wound along with its roots will be eradicated.
And thus place them one close to the other until you come above all those which by nature are in the body.
And apply treatment to those abscesses after the roots of the nails have been removed with the instruments which cause flesh to grow.
Chapter LIII.
On the cautery for trembling.
When you find that someone has a tremor due to friction in the nerves or from fever causing friction, or for some other similar cause, it is then necessary for you to cauterize that area with four cauteries or five on the vertebrae of the back, between each vertebra one cauterization; and at the feet of the patient, as well as over the stomach, use an olive-shaped cauterization.
And the trembling of the patient subsides and hastens the recovery from the illness caused by friction.
Chapter LIV.
On the cautery for accidental injuries to the body.
When in the body there appear accidental swellings and pus-filled wounds that are caused by thick, corrupted substances; therefore, at the beginning when they first appear, it is necessary to cauterize over the entire length of the fistula with a fine cautery made from myrtle wood, the end of which is heated over fire; or with long radish seeds; or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes it is necessary to cauterize additionally at the beginning of its expulsion according to this method; and if not done so, the excess matter causing those issues will be disrupted, and they will heal from them.
However, it is necessary that this be done immediately after evacuation and phlebotomy.
Chapter LX.
On cauterization of blood flow due to accidental cutting of an artery.
If the flow of blood from an artery that has been cut is prolonged due to the disease that caused it in the first place, or due to the beginning of abscesses or cauterization of the limb, and similar conditions, then it is difficult to remedy that.
Therefore, when such a situation occurs, quickly apply your finger to the opening of the artery, and press on it until the bleeding stops and no more blood escapes.
Then place the olive wood cauteries, both small and large, in the fire in great number, and blow on them until they are ignited.
Then take one of those cauteries, either small or large depending on the location and the condition in which the artery is severed, and apply it quickly after you have removed your finger, so that the bleeding is stopped and restrained.
And if the blood flows out of the opening of the artery when you remove your finger and take away the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from those that are prepared in the fire, and continue to do this one after another until the bleeding is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is here, and ensure it does not cause other harm.
Know that when blood flows from an artery, it is not possible to restrain the blood at that moment, especially when the artery is large, unless one uses one of four methods: either use cauterization as mentioned before, or apply extraction, because when extraction is applied, the extremities are burned and thus the blood is restrained; or wrap it with a bandage made of strong material, or place it on medicines whose virtue is to restrict the blood; and strengthen it with a pillow and appropriate constriction.
He who administers appropriate constriction with bandages and by wrapping in cloths or placing near burning substances and similar methods should do so slowly.
If anyone attempts this and the physician is not present or there are no medicines available, then gently place the index finger on the opening of that vein as we have said, and feel for the strong constriction inside where the blood has stopped from the upper part of the wound, and above the artery the finger should not be moved; and sprinkle it with cold water to cause intense cold within until the blood is congealed and thickened; and then proceed with cauterization or necessary medication.

Chapter 5.
On the treatment of phlegm through catharsis and the expulsion of fluids from the inner parts of the head.
When you encounter sudden and continuous bleeding for catharsis, and sometimes the fluids of the patient are exhausted and scarce, and their condition shows debility, with the eyelids ulcerated and in their place hair loss, and at the temples there is sharp pain and subsequent emaciation, then from these symptoms you should understand that the matter and catharsis do not come but from small veins within the inner parts.
And the best treatment in such cases is this: that you command the patient to shave their forehead, and then make a small incision at the midpoint of the forehead (beginning from the temple on the left side towards the right temple; and cut through to the bone; extend your hand over the temples which are swollen due to chewing), until the bone is exposed.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or dry cloth and then fill between the two lips of the incision with boiled cheese or linen strips. And then apply powder soaked in wine and oil so that it does not become a cauterized abscess.
And when you remove this and see that the abscess has already diminished, then scrape the bone where it begins to grow new flesh, and afterward treat it with a drying regimen that promotes the growth of new flesh. For example, take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, mix them together, and make a plaster from this mixture to be applied to these wounds until new flesh grows.
Chapter VI.
On the treatment of what affects the ears.
Any cause that affects the ears is one of four types: either it is a mineral stone or something resembling a stone, such as iron or wood; or it is from a plant, such as chaff or beans; or it is fluid-like, such as water and sour wine and their like; or it is some animal.
And when a stone affects the ear or any other cause similar to those that do not adhere or clot in the ear, place the ear towards the sun, and if you see the stone, then at that time drop into it some substance of violet oil or cumin, and immediately administer its extraction, and make them sneeze with certain conditions, and plug the nose before the occurrence of a sneeze. And immediately place around the ear some pieces of cloth or wool, and extend the ear to the upper parts, as this often helps with these causes.

If it is not forcefully pulled in this manner, now take a thin thread or a piece of equipment with which birds are caught a small quantity, and place that at the end of the vein, and then wrap around that point properly placed. And then insert it into the ear canal with ease, and then dry the ear of moisture.
And if it is not forcefully pulled due to all the causes that we have recounted, now you should consider cutting that area which has become inflamed or spasmodic.

Chapter VII.
On the treatment of blocked ears.
Sometimes some children are born from their mothers' wombs with unopened ears, and this also happens in some adults, where the ear canal is blocked by infection or flesh growing in it; and this blockage sometimes occurs at the entrance of the ear canal which cannot be seen clearly, and sometimes it is in the parts outside.
And the one that occurs at the entrance of the ear canal, which cannot be seen clearly, is in the most difficult conditions to treat.
And in the one where you can see clearly, it is necessary to expose the ear of the patient to the sun and observe it. And if you see an obstruction that is visible, open it with a fine spatula, which has the following shape:
If the end of it where the width is small, with one side sharp and the other not; and the spatula should be double-sided so as not to injure the ear.
And if the blockage is due to hidden flesh in the ear or in the canal, then use fine scissors and carefully cut away all the flesh inside.
And if there is an obstruction in the ear canal, then take a thin reed and heat it slightly over fire, and then insert that into the ear.
And therefore, if you find an obstruction with what the patient identifies as causing difficulty, due to hearing impairment, apply pressure gently on it; and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
And then place in the ear a piece of lint in the quantity appropriate to the size of the wound you have wrapped with Egyptian cloth; and do this for several days until you are sure that the swelling has consolidated; or take something and soak it in water and sprinkle it with finely ground powder and use it; and if you see the ear already inflamed, then you should extract what you have used and replace it with another piece wrapped in cerate made with olive oil until the inflammation subsides; and thereafter treat it until it is healed.
But if there is a bleeding, then soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use another treatment that stops the flow of blood, which is described in the chapter on distillation.
Chapter VIII.
On the treatment of eyelid inflammation that occurs in the eyelids.
Inflammation of the eyelids sometimes occurs with moisture, and at other times it is dry.
Therefore, you should have these at hand or in small containers, and mix them with the ashes of their roots, and then apply them as finely powdered dust to the affected areas.
If pain and bleeding come from a vein, then at that moment you should cauterize those parts with a fine cautery.
And the best cauterization is near the incisions and wounds return when there remains some cause for them; and when they are cauterized with fire, those causes are burned up and do not return.
Chapter IX.
Treatment for swelling that comes to the eyelids.
Wounds that remain in the eyelids have a cause similar to swelling in their strength and hardness, and thus they are called swelling.
And it is an accumulation of thick moisture in the eyelid both above and below.
Therefore, the procedure in such cases is to consider at that moment whether the swelling has arisen within what appears on the eyelid and is spread throughout; and the cause of this is light.
Therefore, apply something over that area along the length of the fissure, and then carefully remove it from all parts with ease until it is clean, and then suspend it with an ointment and bandage it.
And if the swelling is large, then you should adjust it with stitches; and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, no harm will come from it because the heat of the treatment will restore and heal it.
And if the swelling is inclining toward the inner parts of the eyelid, you should press the surface inside, then lift the eyelid and suspend the swelling with a needle, which you need for incision, and press it from all sides, and if the eyelid because of incision is elongated, this you do not note as any cause.
And after the incision, wash the wound with salt water; and treat the area with something that heals so that it may be healed.
Chapter 10.
On the treatment of abscesses above the eye on the eyelid.
Abscesses are more common in the layers above the eyelid, especially in children, and they worsen the condition of their eyes, leading to a worsening of their catarrh; and they cannot look at the brightness of the sun because tears collect for them.
And for this reason you see them lying on their faces all the time, and they sleep with their faces down; and thus their lower eyelids are covered by moist eyebrows, which have become inflamed, and the likelihood of an abscess is apparent in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel tenderness there.
And one method of operation is when the patient rests his head in your lap, and then you place a cloth over him and make a circular incision according to the quantity that contains pus from all sides, and then place that on it and press with your fingers all around so that an abscess forms in the middle of the circle, and then drain the moisture in the middle using a spatula inserted through a small incision, which should not be larger than the incision made in flaubotomy.
In the main incision cut through the wound completely until you reach the periosteum, for in major dislocations proceed to the periosteum by means of a fissure according to the form of the white line.
Then extract the bandages which you have wrapped around the said limbs at the parts from the inside, and twist them between your right and left hand until they are disentangled.
And be careful not to probe in the fissure so that you do not reach the bone and damage it.
But if the artery does not appear to you at the beginning of the fissure, then probe in the fissure gently until you find the artery; and then extract it as we have said; and afterwards immerse the bandages in warm water and vinegar and place them over the site and sprinkle those with powder.
And some people who sprinkle salt and place it in the depth of the fissure to absorb any remaining moisture.
And then take care of it until it is healed.
But if an abscess occurs in the area, now treat it with soothing plasters until it is healed.
Chapter XI.
On the methods of palpating the bone.
When in the eyelids of the bone, there grow hairs beneath the natural ones and when these are plucked, then those harm the bone and cause various illnesses such as frequent tears and softening of the eyelids and membrane, which is called palpating the bone, and it is treated in four ways: either by cauterization with fire; or with acute medicine as mentioned earlier in the chapter on cauterization; or it is done by incision and suturing; or by canals as I will explain.
Therefore, when you place the clamp on the wound in its place; quickly turn the upper eyelid with your left hand, and if it turns well, that is good; but if not, insert a needle into where there is pus underneath the eyelid, and let the needle penetrate to the base above, near the edge of the eyelid from which hair grows, near the hair, and draw out the pus towards the upper parts under the eyelid, and turn it with the ray.
And then insert into the inner part of the eyelid below the hair adjacent to the lower lacrimal passage entering into the smaller lacrimal; and then extract the pus and place below the eyelid small particles of cork or cloth, and then press on the eyelid with a probe, and it should be as shown in the figure of a myrtle leaf.
However, the figure should depend on the amount of swelling of the eyelid, for this varies in people, as some require it to be incised from the eyelid in great quantity according to the extent to which the eyelid is softened.
And then insert into the space along the two lines that you have marked, starting from the lower lacrimal passage entering into the smaller lacrimal. And if your incision is near the natural hair line as thick as the king's [royal standard or as the king's usual practice].
And then place the incision at one of the two angles of the jaw, and then excoriate that entirely, and then approximate the wound edges with fine thread or wool floss, and cleanse the blood, and make adhere what excesses of pus remain on two eyebrows for some viscous causes, if you want to do this, and otherwise, do not worry.
About four days after sewing up the intestines, begin treatment with this.
And if you wish to treat the wound without sewing, use something that dries and constricts, since the eyelid is raised due to the sealing of the wound and the accumulation of fluid; but sewing is better. This is what the ancients recommended.
However, one should be careful about the condition of the wound, and it is good practice, not done out of fear.
And there is another method for treating the eyelid, which involves shaping it like a myrtle leaf, and then lifting the eyelid by three inches, separated and brought together in this form:
But if it is not possible to do this, hold the stitches, and if they are uneven, now take a needle with thread and place it in the middle of the shape, and position the other side near the larger tear duct, and the second side near the smaller tear duct, and adjust between the said threads for equality, and then lift your hand evenly to raise the entire marked area as we have recommended.
And then align the two edges of the wound with the suture, and treat it until it is healed.
But if an abscess occurs due to your incision or the wound, treat that abscess with cerate and something similar, and frequently.
And sometimes you may need to turn the eyelid so that its side can be incised; then proceed as we have described for incision. But sewing is more artificial in such cases.
To make a needle, follow this form: And so you convert the eyelid and divide the figure that is inside as I have recounted; and then by art make two needles or two slender rods whose length shall be according to the length of the eyelid and whose width shall be slighter than the width of the pupil. And you have already cut into the extremities of each section where the threads are located.
Then apply each needle with subtlety over what is superfluous of the eyelid, and constrain the needles in each section with a strong ligature, and leave it for several days, because the excess will die due to the constriction and will be rejected so that it may fall off by itself.
But if it is delayed, then cut the needle with scissors, and afterward treat the wound until it is healed.
When it is solidified, the eyelid is raised and the hairs do not sting.
And this is the form of two needles that you shall make according to this form: and the quantity of the length and width should neither be more nor less.
Chapter XII.
On the elevation of stinging hairs in the eye using an awl when the hair is hard.
When there are unnatural hairs that are hard, rough, and sting the eyes, then the operation is that you take a fine awl and place it in the soft fiber of each hair, and afterward adjust the ends of them carefully and gently. And let the length of the fields be one palm in quantity.
And below that, prepare another fine field briefly, and use the extremities of it if you wish.
And then place the malate [affected area] in your lap in the proper position, and let it be close to the sun so that your work is clear, for it is a subtle and delicate operation.
And then insert the needle into the root of the hair that requires removal, and let it penetrate the eyelid quickly before you extract it from above the natural hair.
And then pull the hair in from above and insert it into another area, and let there be one or two or three hairs and no more.
And then pull your hand away so that the hair is moved to another area on the eyelid with the natural hair.
But if it is pulled to another area and the hair does not move, pull again to the parts below near the field you have prepared with that, so that it is moved to another sufficient place.
And place that in the sight of the eye, for now you will move it to another area and the field, and sew together each part and close the eye with stitches, and leave the stitches for two or three days so that the hair adheres to its place and grows over that good flesh.
And if the hair is shorter, now you should attach a long hair from the natural hairs to that one, or leave it so that it grows longer over some space, and then lift it.
Chapter XIII.
On the treatment of an abscess that occurs in the upper eyelid.
When an abscess occurs in the eye, and it is contracted when the eyelid is closed, either to the right or left side, due to poor healing of a wound, incision, or cautery, it is called leporine. This abscess may be either natural or accidental, caused by improper healing of wounds, incisions, or cauterizations, or similar malpractices.
The method of the operation is such that you should divide that consolidation and separate its lips, place between them a piece of linen cloth, and bind it until it heals.
And it is not advisable to use treatments for it that dry out or constrain it; for if you do this, the abscess will return worse than before and never heal properly.
Use treatments that soften it, such as fenugreek; boil the seeds and leaves in water, or use myrrh dissolved in some oil. Then soak a piece of cloth in this mixture and apply it for treatment.
The essence of your treatment is that you should pierce any part that has not healed in its proper form from the beginning.
And this operation that we have described is simply a correction of an abscess that does not return to its original form as it was before.
Chapter XIV.
On the treatment of an abscess that occurs in the lower eyelid.
This ulcer which is made in the lower eyelid, which is properly called an ulcer; and it is of two kinds, one natural, the other accidental.
The accidental kind is caused by plague or by cutting or by cauterization or by other similar means.
And the way of the operation is that you take a needle with which to make double thread, and place it in the flesh, and that you make it pass from the left lacrimal bone into the right lacrimal bone, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
And then stretch the flesh in the parts above the needle and cut along it with a broad probe. And then if you return the shape of the eyelid as needed, it is good; and if not, take a thread and place that in the incision, and with that convert the eyelid, and close both openings at the lower part of the eyelid.
And let the ends of the two openings meet at two corners of the incision, which you have made until they converge, and let there be their formation such that when they come together, a proper shape is formed.
And this figure is the Greek letter: lambda.
And then remove the flesh there in the amount of the width below it, which stiffens the eyelid.
And then adjust the separated parts and sew them together with wool thread; and then treat those with what we have said about softening medicines and poultices until it is healed.
And if through cutting or by means of a wound or cauterization it is necessary for you to make an incision, then you should make a shallow cut along the skin of the eyelid as has been described previously. And then separate the membrane between the two lips as we have said.
In the summary of the speech on the treatment of incisions when they are above or below, it should proceed according to the operation, as has been prepared for the form of the incision, and that is in a variety of shapes.
And the skilled artisans always prepare in every possible way that is possible within their means to restore the figure to its natural form or close to it, and they adapt according to the needs of the operation and the instruments in the best arrangements.
Chapter XV.
On the adherence of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this consolidation occurs in certain individuals such that the eyelid is adhered above to the sclera, and its movement and natural functions are restricted, then you should introduce a probe below the eyelid, lift it up with it, or extend it to the root.
And then you should cut the adherence with a fine probe, which should not be sharp enough to cause cuts, keeping it blunt so that the patient is not disturbed by the procedure, and move the probe gently so as not to scratch the eye with its sharpness.
And your incision should be such that it easily separates the adherence so that the eyelid can return to its natural form, and ensure that the entire adherence is healed.
And you put in the wound salt water with which you wash; or distill in that of rose dissolved in the water.
And you separate between the eyelid and the wound with a probe, and apply on the wound linen infused in egg white, and after the third day use consolidating remedies until it is healed.
Chapter XVI.
On the formation of swelling and elevation of flesh in the area of the tear ducts.
Know that swelling occurs in two ways: either it is nervous, and resembles a hard but subtle knot; or it is non-nervous, resembling white frozen moisture, which when touched with a finger does not allow the finger to sink into it, it is separated and the finger does not enter it.
And both kinds do not begin except from the major tear duct gradually spreading to cover the eye and preventing light and movement of the eye.
But the method of working on the swelling is that the patient places their head in your lap, and then you open their eyes, lift the eyelid with your hand, and collect the swelling with a probe by slightly folding it back to the parts above. And then take a needle and insert into it a horse hair or ox hair or cowhair, or something similar, or strong thread, and fold the tip of the needle slightly and place it in the center of the swelling, and penetrate it with the needle, and tie with thread the swelling; and extend it to the parts above, and irritate with the hair the edge of the swelling which is near the pupil. And thus you should pull the hair together at the rear.
And then you should incise the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a fine spatula or small forceps, and leave the conjunctiva of the tear duct intact so that by this incision there does not result a continuous flow of tears.
And sometimes the nail is removed with the sun on it extended or with glue, as we have said, and then it should be extracted with a lion-shaped spatula, of which this is the form:
And be careful that with the spatula you do not touch the corneal membrane and cause a rupture, and that the pupil is not perforated.
After the incision, place a small amount of powdered salt or dry paste made from myrrh in it, and leave that for another day; then treat it with water as needed until it is healed.
And if the nail is not tender and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor is it attached to the flesh underneath, then this particular kind of nail is treated with medications, and it heals as I have said and recommended in the division.
And if you wish to incise that area, then you open the wound of the malady and take a fine, sharp spatula in the shape described: with delicacy. And if it happens that the area is inflamed and swollen and needs treatment at the root, then apply a distilled solution from your own saliva or a dry paste made of myrrh or powdered salt to the wound, and bandage it for another day.
Then proceed with the operation until everything that is there has been removed, except for the opposite abscess which lies in the belly, because at that time you leave it alone and treat the beginning of the abscess until it is healed.
Then proceed with your operation on that one for the same reason until it is healed.
If the lacrimal flesh becomes swollen and painful in the eye, suspend it for a period of time; then incise part of it, but do not proceed with the incision to such an extent that you cut off the flow of tears.
Then apply a dilution of rose petals and sinah (a type of plant) to the lacrimal area until it is healed.
Chapter XVII.
On the incision of styes and of what is born of flesh tightly attached to the eye.
In the eyes of some people, small pieces of reddish flesh appear and grow until they cover the pupil, or approach it or protrude over the eyelids, causing the eyelids to be pushed apart in certain areas and resembling a balcony railing.
The operation in such cases is that the patient rests his head on a pillow; then open the eyes of the affected area and remove all that flesh at appropriate times, using forceps or scissors; then incise the flesh at the beginning until it is completely removed with the scalpel, and be careful not to damage the eye during your operation.
And perform your incision with the scalpel that you use to incise these areas or with the spatula that we have recommended for nail incisions, or with small forceps with which hair is removed, depending on the form of the condition as described in this chapter.
Thus, when your incision is complete and the flesh around it has been thoroughly cleaned, now fill the wound with finely ground salt or sprinkle on red powder similar to corrosive medicines, and cover the wound with a white cloth so that it is protected from abscesses and cautery.
And if any reason arises to guard against infection and the wound requires cautery, and you fear an abscess from cautery, then leave the wound open and treat that abscess with whatever remedy cures it, and subsequently proceed with the operation once it is healed, and similarly handle the flesh around such a wound as described.
However, you must be careful to remove all healing from the wounded area either naturally or accidentally by iron, and do not use your hand in any of these operations until you see that the member can withstand the operation.
Chapter XVIII.
On the Collection of Sutures for Wounds.
Apply red sutures to the veins that are visible on the wound and prevent their operation by dressing, and leave the wound to heal over several days.
At the beginning, you must consider whether the wound in which there is a stitch is strong and if there is no other illness but the stitch; then you should collect that stitch.
And you must command the patient to place their head in your lap, and then suspend those veins with a thread or two as per your discretion; and let that thread be of subtle double thickness according to this form:
or use two threads gradually in one piece according to this form:
And thus bind those veins with fine ligatures, and let the blood little by little, until you see those veins become flattened and turned empty.
And be careful that you do not lead the blood to the extremities of the body; and if your operation is in the morning before the sun, and if you are firm in your operation so as not to incise except those veins.
And when you have completed it, then you should apply distilled vinegar or rue to the blood because of its acidity to corrode what is left of the wound.
But if it is not possible for you to do this all at once, then apply a plaster that relieves the pain, and let it be for several days until the pain subsides and is assured of being free from abscess.
And then repeat the same method until he is healed.

Chapter XIX.
On the reduction of nasal fractures.
When you treat fractures with cauterization or with acute medicinal remedies as described and not healed, then do not let them generate in the wound but cut into the abscess when it is ripe, and remove all the humidity that is within until the bone is exposed.
And when the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then you should scrape it with an instrument of this form:
He is called the rough-headed one, and it is made of iron, and its head is round just like this which is made with small pricks by lime or steel points by fine pricking (as in the following form).
Place that over the corrupted part of the bone, and then rotate it between the said tissues, and press your hand gently little by little until you know that the corruption has been scraped away.
And this should be done several times, and then the area should be treated with drying and styptic agents.
When the area is consolidated and good flesh has grown in it, and the core of the pus has been removed and remains for forty days without being renewed and without forming an abscess and without any incident occurring, then you know that it has healed.
And if not, now there is no other way but to reduce the fracture to the shape of the nose, and according to this method: first expose the bone with an instrument or with sharp medicine, and when the bone is exposed, then you apply three strips in this manner:
Let the end of the instrument be triangular, and the shaft of it should be gradually tapered towards the end as you see here.
And then place that on the bone, and let it be near the lacrimal; extend your hand a little bit, and then rotate your hand with the instrument until it penetrates all the bone, and you will feel the discomfort because of what it finds venting out when you move your hand.
Now, treat the area with styptic and drying medicines as you have been instructed.
Thus when the flesh and the place are hardened, at that time the matter which was flowing to the parts outside returns to the nose, therefore this is easier on the patient.
Chapter XX.
On the reduction of the prominence of the nose.
When the nose is prominent in its entirety and no harm or reduction occurs upon touching it, then you should allow the patient to rest, and subsequently cover the neck with windy poultices without scarification and suction with good cupping; then apply a liniment made of aloes, sesame, and sarcocolla over the nose, and finally apply a plaster on the prominent part of the nose using powdered substances.
And the method for making the powders is to take linen cloth strips, and each strip should be folded into four parts; then make the powder from these strips.
Then apply those one over the other according to the amount the nose requires. Then apply a hard cloth with tight bandaging over them; and repeatedly use the wind poultices with suction without scarification; then remove the poultices and let the nose be tightly wrapped day and night.
But if the bandage becomes loose during this time, then it should be made tight again.
Then remove the bandage as we have said, both day and night.
And if you see that the nose has already been reduced, that is good; but if not, then repeat the plaster with the powders and tight bandaging and the wind poultices until it is healed.
Chapter XXI.
On the incision of the prominence of the uveal tissue.
When you find disruption in the uveal tissue, and it protrudes prominently outside the eyelids like a grain of barley, shaped like a human figure because of this, and you wish to incise it, then during the operation proceed as we have recounted: insert the needle at the edge of the uveal tissue from below and above, and immediately place another needle where the double suture thread is located near the lacrimal area, allowing the first needle to follow its natural position; then incise the site of the double suture and lift that part of the uveal tissue around the needle, strengthening this movement.
Then withdraw the needle and pause over the woolen thread infused in egg white, allowing it to adhere to the uveal tissue.
Then treat the wound with what will comfort it until the injury is healed.
And in some cases, apply from all sides a circular piece of cloth around the wound to cover the large prominence, and then puncture it with a fine spatula so that it adheres to the wound, allowing the albuminous moisture to flow, and soon the wound will subside.
Then strengthen it until it is healed.

On the treatment of contusions.

Chapter XXII.

Chapter XXIV.
On the treatment of flesh wounds on the nose.
When on the nose there arise various flesh growths attached, some of which are caused by a condition that resembles a spider web made of thick threads, or a water polyp; and this illness is called polypus because it resembles that aquatic growth. And it is cancerous flesh, ulcerated, of dark color; and there is flesh like a lion's but not of a dark color.
Therefore, for the one who has these lion-like flesh growths that are not fraudulent or cancerous, cover it so that the illness feels between your hands and place it in the sunlight, and starve the nose of it, and apply poultices occasionally to those flesh growths. And then extract it from the parts inside, and then cut out what you have identified of those with a sharp subtle spatula until you know that all the flesh has been removed; if you leave any part for any reason where the excision is possible, then remove it with one of the fine instruments easily so that no part remains for any reason. If the master decides to treat an abscess or boil with cautery, then proceed according to what is necessary; but if it is one of the malignant boils, then incise and cauterize that part which allows the blood to drain and all the cavities to be exposed.
And if it is necessary to cut near the nose close to the insertion, use either water or vinegar. But if the nose is swollen and the process of moisture extends to the palate, then you will know that it has already healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as necessary, then you will know that inside there is flesh still attached to the excess bone of the nose to which abscessification has not reached.
Then you should introduce some thick substance into the area where there is swelling, and use small nuts, placing an amount equal to the width of a finger or less between each nut, and generate the pus, then insert one end of the substance into the nose either through the ray or for another reason that is possible.
And once this has been done, cause the smell to penetrate until it reaches the channels of the nose, and let it sit over the affected area, for diligent observation of such actions is how children are taught in schools, and it is a quick way to achieve the desired effect.
And then place the two ends of the substance, one end extending out over the nose and the other remaining inside the nose, and then apply a layer of flesh around the nuts that are in the substance, and do this until you know that the flesh is covering the nuts of the substance.
Here is the translation:
