And if you prefer, make a closed tube like a reed, and the tube of the instrument used for nasal irrigation should be flat and open; to which attach a handle at the back, with which you can hold it when heating in oil or water what you want from juices and substances that need to be heated.
Chapter XXV.
Of swellings in the nostrils.
Swellings often occur in the nostrils, and they are treated with applications and ointments for several days until it becomes clear what course of treatment should be followed.
And for this reason, you should insert that at the beginning of its appearance and treat it entirely. Here is the translation:
If the incision is delayed until it becomes hardened, then consider whether it has become pus-filled, hard, dark in color, and with little sensation; at this point do not touch that part with a sharp instrument as it is chronic abscesses; for many times those to whom you incise this abscess will suffer great harm because of that.
And if the abscess is painful to touch and not dark in color, and there are times when an incision is possible throughout that area, then evacuate the pus and incise it without fear, and treat the site with substances that dry and constrict until it is healed.
Chapter XXVI.
On sewing up the nose and lips and ear when the continuity is disrupted by plague or similar causes.
Know that when there is a disruption of continuity in one of these cartilages, few will be suitable for this operation except in some individuals.
Therefore, when there is any cause for one of these conditions, consider whether it is a fresh wound or the result of blood, and then you should approximate the two edges of the wound with stitches, and subsequently treat it until it is healed.
And if the edges of the disrupted continuity are already separated and each is clean and healthy, then you should scrape each part of its visible edge until blood comes out. And then approximate the two edges with stitches and strengthen them, and on top of those apply powder from dragon's blood and sprinkle it, and place over the powder a piece of mastic plaster or another consolidating plaster, and leave that for two days or three, and then remove it and change the plaster and continue treating with an ointment until it is healed.
This is the method of suture: you add the solution of continuity with needles as we have recounted in the suture of the abdomen, or you add the solution of continuity with silk as you know.
Chapter XXVII.
On the extraction of those causes that occur between the two lips in the private parts.
Often in some men, in the private parts between their lips, there appear hard swellings similar to the gristle of birds, and some of them are smaller and some larger.
Therefore, you should turn the lips and split each of those hardnesses from all sides, and then when you make the incision, fill the place with finely shredded wool until the blood is constricted, and then wash it with cold water and treat the area with something astringent until it is healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the incision of flesh that is adhered to the gums.
Often there arises on the gum flesh that is adhered to, which the ancients called "albulus," then you should suspend that with a thread or what you have at hand and incise it down to its base, and let the pus or blood run out.
And then apply to the area finely shredded wool or other astringent powders or substances that promote drying.
And if that flesh returns, then incise it again, and cauterize it, for after the cautery it will not return.
Chapter XXIX.
On the Reason for Pulling Teeth with Iron.
When on the surface of the teeth in both the inner and outer parts, as well as between the gums, there are sharp, rough spots, discolorations, some areas are blackened or have become greenish or brown, causing corruption to the gum, and thus the teeth become discolored.
And therefore, it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands and place his head so that he leans forward, and then remove the teeth in which these rough spots appear and cause sand-like substances to form inside, so that none of them remain, thus removing the blackness and greenish discolorations and other such issues so that they may be cleaned.
If that is what remains as stated at the beginning of the reason, it is good; otherwise, repeat the procedure on those teeth another day and on the second and third days until you reach the final stage as you desire.
And know that the teeth have the need for various tools of different shapes and sizes according to how you prepare your operation. And therefore, the razor used to remove the part inside the tooth is different from the one used to scrape between the teeth according to other forms, and you should prepare these various shapes of razors as you will see shortly:
Chapter XXX.
On the Extraction of Teeth.
When you treat the tooth of its pain with all care and delay in its complete extraction, because there is no reason to spare it when it needs to be extracted, as it is a substance that requires removal.
When the patient is forced to have a tooth extracted, you must be certain which tooth is causing pain, for often the pain deceives the patient and they believe it to be in a healthy tooth, and if you extract that one, the pain does not stop until the diseased and painful tooth has been removed, as we have seen from experience with barbers.
Therefore, when you must be certain which tooth is causing pain, and once you know for sure, now it is necessary to incise around the diseased tooth with a spatula that has some virtue until it dissolves from the gum completely, and then use your said tools or fine forceps gradually little by little until it comes out.
And then hold firmly with large forceps for good leverage or a strong grip, and place the patient's head between your knees so that you have control without discomfort to them, and then pull the tooth straight in such a way that you do not break it, nor cause greater pain to the patient.
And if it is not loose, now take one of those instruments and place it under the tooth from all sides and endeavor to loosen it as was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is broken or decayed, now it is necessary to fill that break and reinforce it with a thin wire so that it does not break further during your constriction on it with forceps.
And you must work carefully when incising around the entire circumference of the gum and be cautious with your strength so that you do not break it or leave any part damaged, and return to the patient for the sake of avoiding further harm, as it is better than causing more pain than necessary.
And guard yourself from doing what ignorant barbers do in their rashness and audacity regarding the removal of [conditions], since they do not administer what we have recommended; for many times they bring about great illnesses upon people, which are more difficult to cure, and the roots of these remain unremoved entirely, or some of them, or that you extract it along with some bones of the jaw as we have testified many times.
And after the removal let the mouth be washed with warm water or salt water.
And if there is bleeding due to this, as often happens, then apply some quantity of sugar and sprinkle it over the wound, and if that does not suffice, cauterize.
The shape of the forceps and holding them skillfully with which the tooth is extracted at the beginning should be long, narrow, and thick, so that they do not bend when you are working with them.
And they should be like handles of thick material so that when you hold them, they do not bend; their extremities should be short.
And they should be made of Indian iron with straight extremities, and at the extremities of those there should be teeth, some of which fit into others and hold by strong constriction; sometimes the extremities of these are in the shape of a bird's beak called a ciconia, which is a kite, and they are very strong in their grip.

Chapter XXXII.
On the care of prominent teeth over other teeth.
When prominent teeth do not follow their natural course and the form is weak, especially when dealing with women, and particularly with those who are respected; therefore, you should consider: if a tooth is close to another tooth and it is impossible to extract or treat it properly, then root out that one; but ensure it is not attached to another tooth, so use this instrument of which the form is described; it resembles a pig's snout and is made of Indian steel with an extremely sharp point.
And so let your incision be such that you cut it after too many days because of the hardness of the tooth, and let it not be joined by another of the teeth.
And if it is a prominent tooth to be filed, then file that with an Indian iron file, which has the following form:
And let it be entirely of Indian iron and grind it accordingly; let it have small pointed teeth, just like the file with which needles are made, with which the tooth is filed little by little over several days with ease, so that another tooth is not joined and damaged. And afterwards line it at the end, and rasp it with some razors.
And if it is a tooth of which a part is broken and you examine the tongue, then you should file down the inside where the sharp edge of that fragment is, so that it is smooth and rounded off, and fill it in so that the tongue does not cut itself and the speech is not impaired.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the restoration of damaged teeth with gold or silver filling.
When it happens that the front teeth are loosened or knocked out by impact or accident, and the patient cannot eat properly because of this, and you have treated them with astringent medicines, but your treatment has not been effective, then to prevent further damage, you should strengthen those teeth with gold filling and not silver, for silver will corrode over time, whereas gold never changes.
And let the filling be in such subtlety and thickness according to the quantity that can fit between the teeth; the shape of the restoration is that you take the gold and place twice as much of it between two healthy teeth, and then build up with twists of the filling between one or more damaged teeth, until it reaches the adjacent healthy tooth on the other side. From there proceed with the tissue into the part from which you began, and stretch your hand with ease and skill so that it is not harmed.
And let your thread be such that when you pull the needle with the thread of the teeth, it should not slip.
And then cut off the excess part of the needle using forceps or scissors, and attach those parts at each place with stitches, and convert them and conceal them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth, so that it does not injure the tongue. And then sew it as tightly as a Roman stitch.
But if it comes loose or breaks, pull that or those parts with another needle in such a way throughout its entire length.
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the correction of two healthy teeth and of two damaged teeth, as you see them tied:
And when it happens that you return one tooth or two back to their proper places, and they grow back as we have described, and they are restored.
And only a skilled and wise artisan can do this.
And sometimes a piece of cow bone is used for a certain purpose, and it is shaped like a tooth, and placed in the location where the tooth is missing, and it is sewn as we have said, and remains and serves there for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the incision of the membrane that is in the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament, which is mentioned above the tongue, can be either natural, with which one is born, or it can be accidental and occurs due to a disease that has already become consolidated.
And the operation is that of opening the mouth of the patient, and their head being positioned accordingly; then lift their tongue, and proceed to incise that nerve ligament horizontally until the tongue is sufficiently freed from its attachment.
But if there is any hardness or swelling in that area, and this has become consolidated due to disease, then you should cut carefully and divide it horizontally until you have incised the ligament and dissolved the swelling.
And be careful not to make a cut in the upper part of the flesh so that you do not divide the arteries and cause bleeding.
And then wash the remnants of the incision with salt water or vinegar or fresh water; and afterwards place a linen pad below the tongue. This is to be done so that the patient keeps something under their tongue each night to prevent it from healing too quickly in another manner.
But if bleeding occurs, place over the area vitriol powder; but if the blood flows continuously, then cauterize the location with a hot cautery that is appropriate for this. And then proceed with the treatment using other methods until it is healed.
Chapter XXXV.
On the Extraction of a Ranula Underneath the Tongue.
At times, an abscess similar to a small swelling appears underneath the tongue, preventing it from performing its natural functions; and gradually grows larger until it fills the mouth.
In the operation, place the patient sixty paces from the sun and examine the abscess.
And if you see it of dark or black color and hard, to which the patient has no sensation, then do not touch that which is gangrenous.
And if it is inclined towards a whiteness, in which there is moisture, then you put the incision into it, and cut it with a fine scalpel, and cut it from all sides; and if blood flows during the time of your operation, then pause and apply vitriol above three times, until the bleeding is stopped.
And then return to your work until you have treated it properly, until it is washed with vinegar and salt; and treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter XXXVI.
On the extraction of abscesses from both ears and those in the throat and other abscesses.
When dealing with abscesses in the throat area, which are glands that are somewhat assembled together and appear as if they are ears, when you treat them as we have said in the division and they do not heal, then consider whether the abscess is hard, dark in color, and with little sensation; at this point, do not touch that which is gangrenous with a tool.
And if it is of red color and the root is large, then do not touch that with an instrument due to the flow of blood; instead, leave it until it has matured, and either you will remove it, or it will burst on its own.
And if it is of white color, hard, and the layer beneath it is thin, then at that time it should be cut open.
And the operation on it is what you consider before your operation, whether the abscess has already rested from the heat or if it has been reduced in any way; and then make the patient lie facing the sun, and his head towards you, and open the mouth of the patient, and the assistant takes between their hands the tongue of the patient downwards with an instrument of noble form, which is shaped as follows:
And it should be made of silver or iron, and it should be thin like a knife.
Then when you press with that the tongue and the abscess is shown to you, then apply your instrument carefully over it, take some of it out, and place it in a container, and pull it out to the sides as much as you can. Or take something from the tunics, and then cut it with an instrument of which this is the form:
Or take something from the tunics, and then cut it with an instrument of which this is the form: and it resembles forceps, but the ends of it are curved, so that the opening of each one is opposite the other, sharp, made of iron.
But if this instrument is not available to you, then cut it with a scalpel of which this is the shape: It should be sharp on one side and not sharp on the other side.
And after you have cut one slit, cut the other in the same manner of cutting, and then the patient should rinse with water.
If blood flows, guard yourself with water from the decoction of thorns of mulberry or leaves of myrtle and similar to those of styptic causes until the flow of blood is restrained, and then cure it once it is healed.
And sometimes pus appears in the armpit or as abscesses in those two ears, and incise them according to what is said about the incision of two breasts similarly.
And I had already treated a woman with an abscess which was born in her armpit, the abscess tending to a dark color, with little sensation, which had closed her arm pit, and the woman breathed very narrowly, and had prohibited her from eating and drinking water, and she would have reached death if may not have been for one or two days.
And the abscess had burst so much that it made two channels through which it discharged by two tracts.
Then I carefully examined one of the channels and pulled it out, removing its large portion, and then I incised that which included the other tract, and then I closed the opening, and covered it with a dressing, and then I placed the channel in that abscess.
And then I removed a large part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Then the armpit of that woman was completely open, and she began to drink water immediately, and soon after started eating food. I did not stop incising that abscess multiple times over a long period, and an abscess formed in place of it, which needed to be incised to prolong its healing.
Thus I applied a cautery to the wound and dressed it properly; then I walked around not knowing what God had prepared for me in that regard.
Chapter XXXVII.
On the incision of an abscessed wound.
When the discharge from the wound is directed downward and it is abscessed and elongated, it is then called perpendicular; and if it is thick and round at the lower part, it is then called a boil.
But when it is cured with what we have described in the division and does not conform to your treatment, and you often see that although it has been cauterized, it persists subtly, then you should incise those that are aggregated and round. And if they extend along a certain length and are bloody, of dark color or black, and do not respond to this method, then you should attempt the incision because there is concern about potential harm.
And when you see it as I have described by its whiteness and length, make the patient lie down and press your tongue against it with an instrument that conforms to its shape and then place the thumb below, pull it downward, and incise it with one of two instruments which are mentioned in the double incision procedure.
And you should not incise it more than what is appropriate according to the natural term without excess, for if you incise too much, you will harm the patient.
And after the incision, use what we have recommended for the double incision procedure, and care for it until it is healed.
If the patient is terrified by the incision, use an instrument in the cauterization of him without fear, and the method of the cautery on him is nothing but sharp water, and that you place the head of the patient on your lap, and then press the tongue of him with the instrument which we have said, and then take the water which we have mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and confirm it on that cause alive, and place that not thick nor thin, and with that fill the concavity of the instrument whose form is this:
Let the extremity of him who is cauterized be where the medicine is placed just as the cauterization of a ladle of reed, and place the instrument with the medicine on the wound, and the patient lies on his back so that saliva or mucus from the mouth of that person does not fall due to the intrinsic medicine, so that it does not descend from any cause onto him and contaminate that.
And then take your hand with the medicine and apply that to the uvula for a short while until you see it become blackened and numb due to the bite of the medicine.
And if you want, take a piece of cloth and wrap it around the end of the reed, and dip the cloth in the medicine, and insert the reed with the cloth into a tube so that the cloth adheres to that tube, and do this many times until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the uvula, and then leave it as it dries and falls off after three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, do so, and after the cauterization clean around the uvula with a cloth soaked in vinegar, and modify it with what is around it of the medicine, and wash it with rinsing water, and treat it from outside with embrocation, and heal it with remedies until it is healed.
And this kind of treatment is safer than incision and less lengthy from fear.
In some cases, the uvula is treated with something that is milder than cauterization and incision, for it is cured by subfumigation in this manner: take a poultice, hyssop, mint, rue, Arabian saffron, chamomile, and similar herbs; mix all these ingredients or some of them in a pot, and steep them in hot vinegar, let them boil; the pot should be heated moderately with coals, and place on it a tray upon which rests an instrument made of straw according to this method; it is made of silver or straw.
And insert the extremity into the mouth of the patient so that the steam reaches the uvula, until the uvula is sufficiently treated and becomes clear.
And repeat this procedure several times until it is dry, and be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of the onset of abscesses, as it would be too harsh for the abscess, and you should not do this; instead, you must wait for the formation of an abscess before cauterization.
But if this instrument is not available to you, take a cane and attach a hollow tube at its end, so that the mouth of the patient is protected, for the tube prevents the steam from entering the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter XXXVIII.
On the extraction of spines and what adheres in wounds and other causes.
Move gently around the belly or spine of the patient or for some other reason.
Then look for what is visible among those things, upon which your eyes focus, and then press the tongue with an instrument in sunlight so that it may be made clear to you what is in the throat.

And take an eel and bind it with thread, then place it inside and gradually extract it; or take a probe and gradually insert and withdraw it.
And if it is not expelled by the methods we have mentioned, then use an instrument made of lead in this form:

Chapter xxxix.
On the Removal of a Leech Attached to the Throat.
When treating a leech with what we have mentioned in the division of treatment with medicines and compresses, consider the position of the patient's throat relative to the sun, after you have pushed up the patient’s tongue with the instrument that we have recommended.
Then if you see the leech, remove it gently with a small forceps or with a fine instrument carefully.
And if it is not possible for you to remove it with that, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat, and immediately place a heated iron in the concavity of the cannula. Do this as many times (until the leech is dead and falls off) and the patient must abstain from water for the entire day.
And then take a jug full of warm water and pour it into the patient’s mouth, and wash the mouth with it; and do not let any cause remain in the mouth, and have the patient swallow the water for an hour afterward with his hand, because as soon as the leech feels the water it will catch.
However, if it is not expelled with what we have recommended, then熏use smoke to fumigate the patient with camphor or asafetida using the instrument that we have recommended for fumigating the uvula.
In this way, then in which position it lies.
And there is another method of operation by fumigation, which is that you take a jar in which are burning coals and the jar is covered with a cloth in the middle of which is a hole; and fit the end of the instrument into this hole; and place it above the smoke; and have the patient put his mouth on the end of the tube, and close his mouth so that the fumes do not escape until you know that the fumigation has reached the blood-letter, for the blood-letter will immediately fall.
And if it does not work, then you repeat the fumigation as many times as necessary, and abstain the patient from drinking, and give dry and salty foods, and do not allow water, and likewise it is necessary that this regimen be followed.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the blood-letter is treated when it is seen in the groin; and it resembles forceps as you see them, but with some curvature: which is placed in the groin, and the end of it resembles the beak of a stork, which is sharpened with a file, so that when it compresses something, it does not slip.
Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the speech on the preparation for abscesses and their incision.
Abscesses are many species, of many kinds, as will be recalled for each one in this book.
And there are various methods of puncturing and incision for them in two ways, one of which is according to the nature of the abscess itself; and by what they obtain from the humors.
And the second method is appropriate for the locations where they occur in the body, because an abscess that occurs in the head is different from one that occurs in the buttocks; and an abscess that occurs in a fleshy location is different from one that occurs at a joint; and each has its own treatment through surgery.
And there are some abscesses that should not be punctured but only after the pus has properly matured or in their completion.
There are other abscesses that should be drawn out when they are crosswise and have not matured according to their completion, just as abscesses that are near joints; because when an abscess approaches a joint, its nature is prolonged within what surrounds it, and if the tendons or nerves of that joint become corrupted, it can cause contraction of that limb. Or if it is close to a principal member, and you delay puncturing it until it matures, it will endanger the principal member; or if it is near the buttocks, then puncture it before it has matured; but do not puncture it raw, without proper digestion, except for one near the buttocks so that the pus does not spread to other parts inside the buttocks and form a fistula, or be done in such a way that it cannot heal. But it is important that you know the right time to puncture the abscess, and that is when it has matured.
And you should know that it is mature when there is relief from pain and the cessation of fever, along with a reduction in hardness and pulse, and the sharpness at the top of the abscess, as well as other signs.
And you should know that the puncture should be made in the place where the pus is below the abscess and coming from the swelling.
And the puncture should always be made along the length of the flesh or body if the abscesses are in the limbs or the chest and in places such as ligaments, tendons, nerves, arteries; and finally, in all other areas where there is no reflection; for in those that reflect, use a puncture according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy area, it is better to delay the puncture until the second stage of digestion is confirmed, as we have said; for if you puncture it before that, it will prolong the illness of the patient and cause great foulness, and make the lips of the abscess harden and deepen.
But some abscesses should be punctured across the width of the flesh or body due to necessity, or according to what the member requires.
And you should administer a small puncture, and in large abscesses, a large puncture or multiple sections according to the variety of the size of the abscesses.
And other abscesses in which the incision should be hollowed out and opened up, when it is made as if by bread dough and is done at a time when it has already hardened, just like those that occur with too much swelling and boils.
And of those that are incised in a section having three corners; and of those that are incised like the shape of a myrtle leaf, just like the abscesses of the armpits.
And there are also those in which a circular incision and a crescent-shaped incision are made, similar to those.
But for one who does not have such swelling as abscesses or superficial tumors, you should pierce it with a shallow puncture, or just plane it out.
And when the abscess is large and has already collected pus, you should pierce it but do not hesitate to extract all the pus at that moment; having extracted one portion from it, on another day scrape the abscess internally, and then extract a larger portion, and continue doing this in several stages according to its degree until it is healed.
And especially if the patient is a young man, or a pregnant woman, or an infant, or an elderly person in debility, because their vital spirit is very weak and depends much on the evacuation of pus, and the patient easily dies, so you should operate on them.
Therefore, guard this chapter with great caution, and when you make the puncture in which you pierce these abscesses, make sure to clean the plaques and then consider whether the plaque of the abscess is small or if it requires a flat incision; at that time, administer some of it or use cotton. If the abscess is large and the wounds from the puncture are many, then you should place a plaster on each wound until they heal.
And if in the abscess a part of the cord is severed or you have concave it around the circumference, then you should fill it with old linen or thread made from cloth, as when you treat pustules or boils without moisture; and strengthen that on the third day; then proceed to care for it according to what is needed from ointments until it is healed.
If blood flows during the time of your operation, then you should prepare water and vinegar in which to dip a cloth, and place that over the spot where the blood is flowing for several times.
If the flow of blood persists, then you should administer treatment and care as well as powders which I have recommended in various places of this our book and division.
If it is in the time of winter, and the location of the abscess is near many nerves, then you should infuse powders in vinegar and burnt oil, and place those on the locations.
And if it is in summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you should place powders soaked in water and oil or in vinegar and oil, and let all of this be rubbed into the area for three days, as we have said. Then you should open the abscess, cleanse it, and administer to its cure what is appropriate until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses generally, but according to the method of distinction I have already mentioned for each type of abscess in what manner is the way of its cure separately.
Chapter 41.
On the sectioning of abscesses that occur in the neck.
In the neck of the head, small abscesses may occur, along with various types of nodes that contain membranes; and these types vary in many ways.
And they are soft, and those contain moisture similar to lime. Of those among which I contain humidity similar to flour, and resembling them; and of those that are stony, hard.
And in the section of all, and in the extraction of those there is no fear, as the extraction of those does not oppose the structure.
And the operation in the section of those is that you express them with an instrument whose form approaches this, which is called an entrobes, until you know what it contains.
For if what it contains is humidity, then you should divide that with a longitudinal section according to a superficial section in this manner:
And begin in the section of the line from .B. to the line of .G. Therefore, when the humidity is evacuated, you should excise that skin which contains that humidity and leave none of it remaining, for if any part is left behind, it may return as a cause of illness.
And then immerse the piece in Egyptian ointment if it is available, and if not, in water and salt; and fill that wound with this mixture and leave it for another day, for in this way you will dissolve everything that remains of that skin.
And then apply the Egyptian ointment to that piece again, secondly and thirdly if necessary, until you are confident that there is no remaining cause of that skin; now treat the wound with the ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains hard nodules, then you should incise that according to this method: with a section having the shape of a cross, and place stitches between the two lips of the incision and explore it thoroughly, and exert your skill in extracting the membrane that contains it.
If your arrow wounds are in opposition to what I have described first, then do as I have recounted subsequently.
And similarly treat the suppurated abscess with regard to its section and cure as we have told you; and the section of a suppurated abscess is easier, because it contains little blood and less moisture.
And sometimes there are abscesses in the head of certain people who do not have much moisture throughout their body; the reason for this is that I once incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and found it as hard as a stone, rough, white, and unable to be squeezed, such that if someone were to strike it, they would cause pain.
However, in other abscesses that occur in the head, this is not the case for this chapter; similarly, for abscesses that appear on the heads of children and behind the ears, the section should be a superficial incision.
And pause the puncture of those from the lower part so that the contents of the abscess can drain into the lower parts.
And then treat those according to what is appropriate for that condition.
Chapter XLII.
On the incision of scrofulas that occur in the neck.
These abscesses often occur in the neck, armpits, and groin; and sometimes they appear in other parts of the body.
And those that occur in the neck often develop between the muscles, and every scrofula among them is within its own proper layer, just as in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have recounted.
The types of these scrofulas are spots, for only some of them are stony, and of those only some contain moisture, and of those only the malignant ones which do not respond to treatment.
Then in that one which you find to be well disposed during the examination, whose color is similar to that of the skin, and which is soft throughout, does not adhere to the nerves of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, and is not adhered to; it is necessary for you to incise it at a superficial dryness above the part from the upper part of the body down to the line of .G. between the line of .B., and peel it off entirely, extending both sides of the skin towards the armpit or even further if there is need for that, as we have said regarding abscesses on the head, and extract it little by little.
And it is more cautious that the vein or nerve not be incised.
And let the spatula not be sharp, so that it does not injure your hand during the incision, or disturb the patient, and do not incise what is unnecessary; for if you were to cut a vein or artery and prohibit yourself from continuing the operation, then apply green vitriol powder or other powders which promote blood flow, and staunch the wound, and leave that inside where the acuteness of the abscess subsides, and let the inflammation be mollified and begin to heal. When the blood has been separated.
Then return to your operation once you have completed it.
And then inquire at the time of the operation if there is any cause for other small scrofulas, so you can incise those and clean them.
And if there is a large vein in the region of the scrofula, do not incise that scrofula in its vicinity. And beware that you apply to it a very strong bandage and that you leave it in such a way that it falls by itself without harm.
And then fill the wound with old lint which you have already soaked in Egyptian onion juice, and then treat that.
And if you incise that scrofula completely, then make sure to approximate the two lips of the wound and sew them together immediately so that there is no room for any superfluity.
But if you see that there is excess superfluity due to the size of the scrofula, then you should understand that it is necessary to incise its root, and extract it and administer sutures as mentioned.
And for the scrofulas that contain humidity, make a superficial puncture where it appears to you the location of maturity, and pause in piercing until the pus from deep within the body comes out, as we have said.
And then proceed with puncturing, apply dressings and treatments appropriate for those conditions, so that it corrodes what remains of the corruption. And when you see the wound already cleaned, then treat it with a dressing of generative flesh so that it heals.
Chapter XLIII.
On the incision of the pustule of an accidental abscess in the groin.
Thus did the ancients call this section in the epitome, and I have not seen any man in our land who performed that operation.
And this is the inscription of the speech of those people.
In men who have a weak constitution, it is advisable to avoid the extraction of the epilhot, as this can be harmful because all the veins and major vessels are here.
In those who have abscesses in the mouth or throat or near the ears, when the illness is not in the bone, it is advisable to use the section of epilhot to avoid the loss which is caused by perforation.
Therefore, when we want to do this, cut the epilhot below three circles of its circles, or four if it is a small section along the latitude in that which lies between two circles by quantity, so that the section be in ciphac, not in cartilage, and this place is suitable for cutting because it lacks flesh and the blood vessels there are thin.
And if the physician is cautious, it is better to extend the throat skin by one inch and then make an incision until reaching the canal, but avoid the blood vessels if you see any reason to do so.
And then cut the ciphac as we have recommended; and it is significant for the section of the canal by the membrane which is attached to what is divided there and by the abscission of the voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Thus when the hour arrives in which the perforation is feared, join two lips of the wound, and leave it alone except for the cartilage.
And then administer medicines that cause flesh to grow until it is healed.
And Al-Bucasis says that the essence of what we have discussed is to observe that some of these abscesses have already obstructed the airway of the patient, and the patient has come close to death, and the throat has been cut open, and some will incise the epiglottis so that the patient can breathe over the site of the wound, and thus be saved from death.
And by this commandment allow the wound to remain open until the poison of the illness is consumed; and the poison remains in him for three days; and thus at that time command the wound to be closed, and care for it until it is healed.
And what I have seen myself is that a servant took a knife and cut it into their own throat, incising part of the windpipe, and I was called to treat them. When I arrived, I heard the moaning like that of one near death, uncovered the wound, and found the blood that had flowed from the wound in small amounts.
Thus I confessed that she had not cut any vein or artery, and air was coming out of the wound, and I treated her until she was fully healed.
And the servant did not suffer any harm except for hoarseness in her voice, and after some days she returned to improvement in her condition.
And here you should know that in the section on the epilation there is no fear.
Chapter XLIV.
Of the section on the abscess which occurs in the groin, and is called a hernia of the groin.
This abscess called a hernia of the groin is large according to the constitution of the body, and it occurs in women.
And it happens in two ways, either naturally or accidentally.
And in the natural case, there is no need for treatment.
And the accidental case happens in two ways, one of which is similar to fatty nodes, and the other way is similar to an abscess caused by the constriction of an artery, and in that case there is reason for concern. And it should not be attempted to treat with iron unless it is small. And you should examine it carefully as described, and if you find it similar to fatty nodes and not attached to any veins, then you cut it open as you would with nodes, and extract it with water containing oil and bursiclam; and if not, then thoroughly investigate its nature, and subsequently treat the area with what is needed for proper healing until it is healed.
Chapter XLV.
Of the section on types of nodes.
There are various types of nodes, and I have already described all their types in the division.
Here is the translation:
Thus I say that in evacuation there is heat and fever and violent pains until the ebullition of the superfluity is stilled; and putrefaction is completed when then the fever, pain, and heat cease.
But in the case there is neither heat nor fever nor pain, and one proceeds as with the lion's wound which is done on a special sac.
And it depends on the color of the skin, and its beginning is like a boil, and it is treated as such, whether major or minor, and there are two kinds: one dry and containing little moisture, and the colors of moisture are various, as I say in the division.
Thus, when you come to the treatment of wounds, express it and inquire about its origin at the beginning, which is called an intercurrent cause, as will be explained in the chapter near this one.
And this is the method of inquiry for abscesses and all wounds, which is to take this probe and insert it into the most humid part you find in the abscess, and you will move it slightly until you know that the probe has already penetrated the tissue.
And next to last, measure the size of the abscess accordingly, and then extract the probe and examine what has come out in its wake.
And if the moisture comes out freely, of whatever color it may be, then you shall incise it superficially as I have said regarding other abscesses.
And if there is no moisture in the marks left by the probe, then you know that it is dry.

For if it remains in any amount, little or much, the abscess will return according to the cause.
And if you see that some part remains, then complete the treatment with some caustic powders and apply them to the affected area, and place on it something suitable for an abscess, and treat it with another method until it is healed.
And if the abscess is large, then you should cut both sides of it and treat it with what promotes healing.
But if a pulsating or non-pulsating vein is against it, then you should cauterize and fill the place three times with vitriol and leave it open for two days or three until the inflammation subsides and the blood coagulates; then return to incise what remains of the abscess.

Chapter XLVII.
On the treatment of the breasts of men, which are similar to those of women.
Some women have swollen breasts when they begin to menstruate, and some have them swollen when they are pregnant or during menopause.
And for that which you wish to treat, cut it in a lunar section according to this form, from the line of .B. to the line of .G., and then excise all the granulation tissue, and then fill the wound with flesh-growing medicine, and then sew together the two lips of the wound, and care for it until it is healed.
If the breast inclines to the sides and is softened due to its size, as it often does in women, then divide it into two parts along its pus-filled areas, each part resembling a lunar section, such that each contains the other at their ends, with the larger line containing the smaller one according to this form from the line of .B. and the line of .G. Then excise the diseased tissue between the two lips of the wound, remove the fatty tissue, and administer what we have said about suturing and powders as necessary until it is healed.
And if you do not complete the incision of what needs to be cut due to the patient's restlessness and blood flow, then fill the wound with cloth submerged in corrosive liquid and leave it until this corrodes what remains of the fat, and then care for it until it is healed.
Chapter XLVIII.
On the Incision of Abscesses that Occur Near the Testicles.
These abscesses that occur near the testicles are of the type of scrofula, hard, and they contain a skin that is tough.
And of those few that contain moisture; and those that contain moisture must be incised in a crescent shape according to this figure: from the line G to the line B. And when all is evacuated of what is in the abscess of the sore, then fill it with boiled wool and leave it for another day.
And after the poultice is removed and it is treated with an appropriate ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess is of the type of the glands or scrofula, then you should incise it as we have said in the section on scrofula equally.
And if the inflammation has spread to where putrefaction is present, then administer cautery as we have previously described.
Chapter XLIX.
On the Incision of Abscesses That Occur Due to Artery or Vein.
When an artery is ruptured and the cicatrix has become hardened that lies over it, then abscesses often occur. The signs by which one can recognize whether inflammation and abscess are due to an artery or a vein: for when an abscess is due to an artery, it tends to be elongated in the front part of the body; and if you press on the abscess with your fingers, you will feel a sound like a hissing.
And an abscess that is due to a vein is round and appears on the surface of the body.
And the incision for this abscess is dangerous, especially when it occurs near the armpits, in the angles, and on the neck, as it can be very serious; therefore, its treatment should be avoided with care. When it occurs in the extremities or on the head, it should also be avoided.
May it be concerning him who is due to the inflation of the artery orifice, cut in the dry section along its length; and near the incision about an inch and near the artery, and remove the tunics until it is revealed.
And nearby place upon that needle and make it penetrate into the other side, and tie the artery with a double knot in two places, which cause I show you by drawing out of two arteries which are found in the temples.
And nearby apply the place which is between two ligaments with a spatula so that the blood escapes completely from there, and let the abscess dissolve.
And then administer treatment in which pus is generated so that the ligaments fall off; and subsequently treat it with appropriate bandages until it is healed.
And if it is an abscess due to the section of a vein, now you should have in your hand what is possible regarding the abscess and nearby insert the needle below the place where you had below your hand, and make it penetrate; and tie it with a double thread so that you can extract it from the other side, and then ligature the abscess with a good bandage as I have told you in the grapevine bandage on both sides of the thread's ends.
And if you fear that the thread will be extracted, now insert the knot with the needle below all the abscess beyond the initial puncture of the first needle, and tie the knots in four places.
And then dry the abscess in its middle part so that when what is inside has come out, you incise the excess of the wound, and leave what is attached to it.
Then pause and apply powder in which you have mixed with vinegar and oil, and then administer the treatment made with cauterization and herbs until it is healed.
Chapter on the abscess which occurs due to the twisting of nerves.
Just as an abscess can occur in arteries and veins, similarly, an abscess can occur in nerves when they are struck or due to excessive labor, and by analogy.
And it is done to relieve the pain in the places where assistants or the heel are involved, or in any place where the joints are accustomed to pain.
And it is a severe abscess, and its color resembles that of the surrounding tissue, and it is among the worst conditions when it is constricted by inflammation, and the patient appears in a state similar to stupor.
And it is not an abscess that forms in the front part of the body, and because of this, it is less painful, and it affects all parts equally without being more severe in front or back.
Therefore, for those who have it in the joints, it should not be wrapped in iron, as it would likely lead to contraction.
But for one who has it in the head or on the forehead, relief can be found using spatha (a type of therapeutic application). But if it is a small abscess, then treat it with poultices and incise it carefully.
But if it is large, then suspend treatment with incisions, and scrape it out; and close the wound with stitches, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter I.
On the incision of an abscess that occurs on the cheek which affects the abdomen.
Therefore, for some people there are growths in their abdomens and other parts of their bodies which are called fungi because they resemble mushrooms, whose root is fine and the head is large; and often the lips of these are smooth, and it can be small or magnificent in size.
And I have seen one such case where in his abdomen there were two growths that resembled mushrooms, and there was no difference between those white ones with fine roots.
And the lips of those were inverted and would split open, and moisture would constantly run from them.
Therefore, I incised them and found inside one weighing about eighteen uncias and the other about ten uncias.
And the procedure during the incision of such growths is that you consider whether the patient is humid, and if the color of the tumor is white and humid with a fine root; then you should incise it with a broad scalpel, and immediately in your presence cauterize it over fire, because for such growths the section should expel much blood; and if the blood flows, cauterize that area.
And if you see the patient is afraid and hesitant, refrain from incising with an iron instrument; instead, take some recent lead plaster, apply it to that tumor, following the method we have described; and leave it for two days.
And then apply a strip of lead plaster around the tumor; therefore, do not stop applying the lead continuously until the tumor is detached and falls off on its own.
If it is a wound of thick flesh, then at the time of incision there may be cause for fear and dread, especially in that which is in the belly.
It is possible that you may incise most of it, and then immediately cauterize what remains so that it does not return.
And then take care of the inside until the wound is healed.
And be careful not to presume to incise a wound that is of dark color, with little sensation, and somewhat hard, for such may be cancerous.
And this will serve as a reminder of these points.
Chapter 52.
On the treatment of a swelling of the navel.
A swelling of the navel is caused by various reasons; either by fissures in the ciphac which is on the abdomen, and then placing there linen or intestine as with other ruptures; or by a sore from which blood or pus flows, depending on what is present; or by trapped air.
But if it is due to a fissure in the abdomen and the placement of linen, then the color of the swelling is similar to the color of the skin; it is soft without pain, and soon appears in various situations. And if it is due to the protrusion of an intestine, then its appearance differs greatly from what we have described, and when you press on it with your fingers, it retreats, and soon returns; it lies lightly on the skin, and is easily reduced by bathing and hard work.
May it be that by moisture, the parts are like lions and are not hidden when you press them with your hand, nor are they constricted, nor are they hard.
And if it is due to poison, then these signs appear as the abscess turns to a declining blackness.
May it be that if it is due to dead flesh, then the abscess is severe, large and hard, and is seated in proportion to a quantity.
May it be that if it is due to gas, then the touch of it is like a lion.
And the operation in this case is that you consider whether the prominence of the navel is due to poisoning by an artery, or a vein, or gas; then it behooves you to be cautious about treating it, for there is fear and dread as I have taught you in the chapter where I have discussed abscesses caused by arteries and veins.
And if the prominence of the navel is due to the bowel or the membrane, then it behooves you to command the patient to lie on their back and stand extended; then feel around the circumference of the entire navel, and then command them to stand between your hands on their back; then surround with a spatula across the circumference of the navel at the place where you have marked with your hand; and then extend half of the abscess upwards by about an inch; and then tie around the circumference with strong wax or thread in the proper manner.
And then cut the center of the abscess extended over the ligature and insert your finger into that area to explore the bowel; and if you find it or protect it with the ligature, then you should evacuate it immediately and urge the bowel towards the inner parts of the abdomen.
And if you have found the cloth, then take one ounce of it; and incise the superfluities from it.
And if it is contrary to your artery or vein, then surround that spot, and return to your operation; take two needles and place in those two strong threads and interlace the two needles in the circuit which you have made around the abscess in a crisscross manner, which you should make penetrate; and then press the abscess into four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and let the place ooze where the flesh is extended and falls off by itself; or incise it when it has become softened and can be oozed out, and then treat it with what is needed from the medicines until it is healed.
And if the prominence of the navel is fleshly or due to moisture, then you should deal with the abscess as we have said and extract the blood or moisture that you find there, and then treat it with what is needed and which will consolidate the area.
Chapter LIII.
On the Treatment of Wounds.
We have already said in the division about the types of wounds and how to treat them with medicines, and that it is necessary for the treatment to proceed properly so that the wound does not become ulcerated; and we have discussed the nature of wounds generated by carefulness and caution in their treatment.
And the ancients said that in some cases wounds can be treated in a place where it is possible to eradicate them completely, just as with wounds in the mammary glands or on the breast and similar parts of the body allocated for removing everything, especially when they are beginning small.
When it is old and hard, do not try to treat it yourself, for I cannot heal such a thing nor have I seen anyone else do so, nor has anyone reached that.
And the operation on it is when it is prepared as we have said, you should proceed and leave the patient to purge from black bile several times, and then let it bleed if any congestion is found.
And then prepare the patient for the operation in the manner in which it is to be performed, and afterwards apply the hooked instruments that are needed for it; and then hollow out completely with a sharp knife for proper excision so that no cause of recurrence remains. And let the blood flow freely, and do not apply poultices to it hastily, and squeeze out the pus, and remove the thick blood with your hand or with an instrument that is suitable for this purpose.
But if you find thick blood congealed in a vein or artery, then cauterize it as soon as the flow of blood has stopped, and afterward treat it with other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 46.
On the Treatment of Hydrops.
We have already recommended, in the division, the types of dropsy related to water, and the signs of each type, as well as the treatment for it with medicines.
And the type that is treated with bloodletting is only the single type of anasarca, and not applied to the types of tympanites or hydropsyarchy, for these are fatal.
And when you have already treated this kind of tympanitic hydrops with medicines and do not apply your treatment anymore, then consider whether the patient has reached debilitation or is suffering from another illness with similar hydrops, just as if it were in the chest or abdomen and similar cases. At that point, be careful not to treat them with iron, for there is danger in doing so.
If you see the patient with excessive vigor and there is no other illness except for hydrops alone, neither in infants nor the elderly, then the method of the operation on them is that you lift the patient between your arms, and have an assistant press the abdomen of the patient with their hand, squeezing out the water from below the navel, and immediately take a spatum spinosum according to this form:
It should consist of two sharp parts that are long, with sharp ends like a spatum; however, there should be some slight tapering gradually, so that during the operation you do not penetrate into the bowel, and thereby harm it.
And then consider if the origin of the hydrops is from the bowels, for then it is necessary that you distance yourself from the navel by three finger breadths when making the incision, on the side below the navel.
But if the origin of the hydrops is from a kidney ailment, then your incision should be to the left of the navel by three finger breadths.
And if the origin of it is from the liver, then your incision should be on the right side.
And it is not necessary for your incision to be on the side on which the patient lies, so that the fluids do not rush to that location and cause harm.
With an instrument, perforate the entire wound, and then insert it into that section, and raise your hand with a spatula between the wound and the tissue, and you will scrape that; and if in quantity of one stripe or so.
Then pierce the tissue so that the spatula reaches the empty space, which is where the water will go. Then remove that and insert an instrument of this form: and it is similar to a thin silver cannula; it is made of silver or ivory or lion leather, at the part that is lowest on it there is a small hole, and on its sides there are three holes: two from one side and one from the other side; and the tip of it is cut in the manner of a feather, as you can see.
And at the uppermost end of it is a ring, because when the instrument reaches the water, then you should immediately apply pressure on the instrument, thus evacuating only a small amount of water at once, for if you evacuate too much at once, the patient may die due to resolution of their vital spirit, or an accident may occur that brings about death.
But evacuate that in accordance with the strength of the patient and according to what is indicated by the condition of the patient, the strength of their pulse, and their overall health.
Then remove the instrument, and close the wound with water, which is done because it is immediately retained by the wound that closes the holes on the tissue, as you are advised to pierce those in that manner.
After each operation, leave the instrument in for another day if the patient can tolerate it, and extract a small amount of water from them daily, and do this over several days with caution so that you do not exhaust their supply of water but only take a little.
If you are concerned about the patient and allow a large amount of water to be removed, then bury them in fine sand up to their waist, and pour sweat-inducing substances over them for bathing, and have them expose themselves to the sun for long periods; and treat them with drying medicines until they are healed.
And apply cautery to the stomach, and treat the chest and abdomen after the removal of the water, as I have recounted in what has been mentioned previously.
Chapter 55.
On the treatment of children who are born with unopened passages for urine.
Sometimes a child is born from the mother's womb with the head of its urination not opened (or it is blocked or not in place).
Therefore, it is necessary to perform the operation of opening it as soon as the child is born using a finely crafted instrument according to this form:
And then insert a fine lead needle into the obstruction and leave it there for three days or four.
And when he wishes to urinate, remove it and allow him to urinate; then return the instrument.
And if you do not insert the lead, it is possible that the urine, which needs to pass through that area, will not be able to flow.
May the one whose wound is tight be treated with a lead nail, as we have said, for enough days until it is enlarged.
May those in whom the wound is not in its place be treated as described, the cause of which is that one is born and the trauma is at the end of the urethra, so he cannot urinate properly; when elevating the penis with his hand above, it does not affect generation, since sperm cannot reach the major second rectitude, and this is a laghid illness.
And the method of the operation is that the patient lies on his back, and you grasp the head of the penis with your left hand forcefully, and incise the tip of the foreskin with a sharp scalpel, like cutting a feather quill, or shave off some part so that the middle of the urethra appears as if it were the tip of the penis, and the wound falls in its proper place accordingly.
And be careful during your operation to stop bleeding, as you may encounter this; pause the procedure when you cut into the blood vessel, and treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 56.
Of the condition known as balanitis, which occurs in the foreskin at the head of the penis, and of its corruption, blackening, and adhesion to the head of the penis.
Such occurrences often lead to this balanitis in the penis and it appears as a soft flesh prominence.
And one of these conditions is malignant and the other is non-malignant.

In some cases, there is a rupture in the glands as we have said, and one must examine the area.
And they do not assign any cause for the pouch to the genitalia; and even if they do assign one, it is rare; and it returns in all hours.
But if the time is prolonged with this condition, a fissure is adjusted in the sphincter where the pouch descends into the sphincter; and this happens due to the extension of the sphincter which is in the glands as we have said, and because the cause is that the sphincter is stretched out, it becomes softened, and there is an accumulation and one must examine it.
And the treatment for this is with cauterization as was preceded in the narration of it.
And in some cases, it is treated with iron according to this method: the patient lies on their back between the thighs, and then the area of the rupture of the abscesses is dried by cutting across the width according to the quantity of three fingers.
And then stretch the white sphincter that is below the groin until it is exposed.
And then take the cautery and place it on the prominent part of the sphincter, and press it against the front of the abdomen.
And then mark the two prominent points on either side of the cautery at the extremity of the sphincter, and suture one to the other.
From this extract take the extremity of the vein, and do not insert it from the suture completely, nor extend the hook, nor any other cause such as I will make you know in the treatment of an intestinal hernia.
And in this way treat it as you would with the treatment of wounds, and when you insert the sutures, clean that area and restore the wound until it is consolidated; for the suture is tight and the prominence of rupture is not adjusted.
And cautery is better in this place, for it is closer to healing.
Chapter .lxviii.
On the softening of the cord of the testicles.
In many cases, the cord of the testicles is softened in some men because the flesh that lies within it is softened, and this requires careful attention.
Then it is necessary for the one who is treating this condition to have the patient lie according to his side, and insert all of the cord that is softened over the natural cord; and then adjust both edges with the suture.
And if you wish, cut the excess of the softened cord at three or four places and with a firm suture.
And then insert the sutures between the incisions; and then treat it as you would other wounds until it is healed and the stitches fall out.
Chapter .lxix.
On Castration.
Know that castration in our law is forbidden, but because it is read to me that I should remember it in this book; and the remembrance of it is not necessary except for two ways, one of which is so that when someone is asked about it by a patient who needs treatment, the doctor knows how to treat it; and the other way is that we sometimes have need of castration for certain animals for our benefit from them, such as horses, oxen, and pigs, and similar beasts.
And therefore I say that castration is performed in two ways: either by cutting, or by making an incision.
But in the one that is performed by cutting, the way of the operation is that we should bathe the animal in warm water until the testicles become softened and descend. And then take those [testicles] with your hand until they are loose and do not appear to be attached underneath.
But in castration performed by making an incision and cutting, make sure you hold the animal and press the scrotum of the testicles with your left hand.
And then lift the suspensory tissues, and make an incision on each side so that you proceed with two cuts.
And then make an incision on those parts that you have each separated. And do not leave any part of the cord attached, but rather the fine cord that is above the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than the one performed by cutting, because cutting may leave some part of the testicles attached, causing the animal to desire mating.
About the treatment of the ulcer inside until it is healed.
Chapter LXX.
On the treatment of hermaphrodism.
Hermaphroditism in men is of two kinds: the first appears where there is a space between the testicles, and in that place where it should be male hair, there is instead a female feature.
And in some cases, that part where urine is discharged is located between the testicles.
In women, there is another kind: the female organ above the pubis appears as two testicles of a man slightly to one side and somewhat like the scrotum of a man, with two testicles.
The treatment is of three kinds: two for men and one for women. It is that you should incise those tight tissues until the pus or bad fluid is evacuated, and then treat them as you would other ulcers.
And in the second kind of men, where urine is discharged from the part between the testicles, there is no treatment or surgery for it.
Chapter XXI.
On the treatment of protrusions and elevated flesh due to causes in women.
A mild protrusion that is naturally tight should be treated until it becomes less tense and manageable; in some cases, it may become enlarged in women to such an extent that it protrudes similarly to men and interferes during intercourse.
Then cover with lint the surplus of the wound on your hand or inch and incisions.
And do not bandage an incision too tightly, especially near the root of the razor cut, so that blood does not flow out.
And properly care for that during the healing of the wounds so that it may be healed.
Matured flesh is the flesh that grows in the hollow of the wound until it fills it, and then it spreads to the surrounding areas like a tail (and thus some call this "tail illness"); therefore, we should incise it as we would lint, and care for it until it heals.
Chapter LXXII.
On the Treatment of Wound Inflammation.
Wound inflammation is when the hymen of a woman has not been penetrated, or when the opening is small. And it can be either natural or accidental.
It is accidental due to a previous illness, and this occurs either because the flesh was not properly joined or due to a thick or thin scar.
And it occurs either at the root of the wound, or on its sides, or in the upper part of it, or in the lower part, and you must prohibit cooking, intercourse, and childbirth, and rather easily also menstruation.
And the diagnosis of this condition is made by examination with a speculum, if the closure is apparent; but if it is not apparent, then you should seek it out using your fingers or a probe.
If there is an obstruction due to a small cicatrix and it is near the two lips, then you should cut it and disrupt that.
And when you place sponges over the two lips, then apply the bandages that are closest to each other.
And let the woman lie on her back, with her thighs spread apart, and then pull the two lips forcefully so that the small cicatrix is cut and the obstruction is opened.
And then take wool and soak it in oil and place that on the area, and have a man guard it for several days until the incised area is healed.
But if the cicatrix is thick and hard, then you should cut that with a scalpel or with a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is due to a piece of flesh, then suspend that for a moment, and incise it.
And have with you the medicines that stop bleeding without causing inflammation, such as acetum, dragon's blood, and hens' eggs, prepared with raw egg white.
And then administer a wide lead tube so that the wound does not heal too quickly.
And keep that for several days and administer dry lint.
And then proceed with the other treatment for healing until it is healed.
And in some cases, other tissues may need to be incised according to this method. If this is due to an abscess that causes pain, do not apply anything contrary to the abscess in any way.
Chapter LXXIII.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids and similar conditions that occur in the genital area of women.
Hemorrhoids are an inflammation of the veins' openings inside, from which blood flows continuously; and when these old hemorrhoids become hard and form lumps.
And now I have described the types of those and the signs of them, and to say which of them can be cured and which cannot be cured.
Therefore, I say that hemorrhoids and lumps when they are in the upper part of the body and do not appear to the senses, in those cases no treatment should be applied.
However, those that occur at the openings of the lower part of the body where sensation can be felt are the ones that can be treated.
And it is necessary that you place the woman in a suitable position and gently extend the affected area with forceps or a rough cloth, and then cut them with scissors.
And then sprinkle on those areas substances that promote the flow of blood from powders such as incised ones like acacia and similar ones.
And then compress the area with a pad for one hour, and afterwards soak it in cold water.
And if the flow of blood continues, then have the area soaked in a decoction of sumac and citrus peels and pomegranate rinds and similar substances.
Take flour of oats and compound it with honey and vinegar and make a plaster with that water upon the back of it.
And if the blood is clotted, it is good; but if not, apply to both sides with poultices, and on the nipples without scarification.
And take wool and soak it in the juice of coriander or the juice of lily of valley or the juice of wild garlic with vinegar, and apply it to the place; and then treat the area with ointments until it is healed.
And treat it until it is healed.
Chapter LXXIV.
On the puncturing of the bladder in cases of acute retention of urine.
Various kinds of abscesses occur in the groin, such as boils and stone-like abscesses and ulcerous ones and hemorrhoidal and erysipelatous and phlegmonous and fistulous and hemorrhoids and piles and other abscesses. And I have already mentioned these diseases and the kinds of them and the signs of them, and their treatments in the division.
And I say in this treatise about the abscess that occurs in the groin when it is one of those that need to be treated surgically, and if you want to know how the operation is performed with iron.
Then you must consider whether the pain of the abscess at its beginning is acute with percussion and phlegm and fever, and if there is redness in that area and if it feels hot when touched, then do not proceed with puncturing it, and treat it with what aids maturation until the acuteness of the symptoms subsides.
During the time when you are to perform circumcision on a woman, place her on a chair that has two seats; and recline her on her backside and adjust her feet towards the part farther down from the belly, and spread out her thighs, and position her arms above her head, and tie some of them with bandages as is appropriate.
And let the midwife follow from the right side and administer the instrument with which the opening of the birth canal is made, the form of which will be described in those cases that will be mentioned.
And when you want to administer this instrument, place it at the center of the opening of the birth canal of the woman, and what is inserted from the instrument into the birth canal should not exceed its natural orifice and lead to injury. But if the instrument is larger than the orifice of the birth canal, now it should be that you place cushions over the tissue around the orifice so that the entry of any instrument into the orifice of the birth canal is prevented.
And it should be that you place the spiral that runs along the instrument on the side below, and let the midwife hold the instrument and rotate the spiral until the opening of the birth canal is dilated.
And when the area becomes visible and is touched with the hand and feels soft, now it should be that you dry that place with a dry cloth.
And when the blood is evacuated, now it should be that you place on the wound a piece of cloth submerged in rose oil or green oil, which has some astringent properties; and place a bandage over the area around the orifice of the birth canal.
Take a piece of clean cloth and place it over the wound after soaking it in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And it should be treated properly for about two days until the patient feels well enough for this treatment, at which point it is safe to proceed.
And sometimes the wound and the swelling are washed with this, so that it is cleansed with water and honey, and then with water in which fennel seeds or long-leaved artemisia have been boiled, after which the patient returns to treatment.
And if the swelling is deep within the wound, it is now necessary to abstain from treating it with iron, instead caring for it as we have described in the division.
Chapter LXXV.
To teach midwives how to treat male embryos in a way that avoids abnormal development, according to the figure that is not natural.
It is necessary for the midwife to know at the beginning the figure of normal childbirth by signs of it; and it is that when you see a woman press herself towards the parts below and desire to breathe fresh air, and she becomes joyful about what will be born from her, and quickly senses the movement of the fetus, then you know that this birth will be according to natural form, and it is the emergence of its head first, and the second part suspended or hanging from the navel.
Therefore, when you see those signs, it is necessary to compress the abdomen gently so that the fetus descends gradually, and when its head emerges, the second part follows, and this should be cleaned of any excess with proper cleansing.
He who lies on another part is unnatural and not commendable, for when the drunkard is thrown upon his back and his body lies before his head and his feet behind, or one hand or foot forward, or his head and hand at the side and his belly exposed, and he turns lightly upon his side, and in semblance of such figures are those not commendable.
It is necessary for the midwife to be wise and subtle in all such matters and to guard against error and deception.
And there he shows all the figures of those things, and which part is injured in those cases, as it is signified by that and stands upon that.
When the act is performed upon his own head by natural effort, and it is vehement for the woman and difficult at that part, and sometimes her virtue already weakened, then let him do such a thing or women have it, and evaporate the essence of fenugreek boiled with warm oil.
And then the midwife should insert her little finger and divide it with the second one, or dry it with her nail until she draws out what is inside of moisture. And press the woman's belly so that the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then it is necessary that the woman be cleansed with powder of silk and powder of fenugreek mixed together.
And then command him, after the cleansing, to be bound and make him sneeze with certain conditions; and at his mouth and nose for one hour, because the fetus will soon be expelled.
And if both legs of the patient are fractured at the beginning, then you should gradually reduce them to ease. And if the instrument is not obeyed, then place the woman on a cushion and lift her off it, and then lower the cushion to the ground; and hold the woman so that she does not fall due to the lowering.
And if the bones are not returned to their proper place and the patient dies, then incise them and remove the contents of the body or tie the body with a cloth and extract it evenly, for it will be fractured.
When the patient is on their back, then you should push them upwards, and then gently turn the patient, and then even out their position, and then take one part of it and gradually extend it.
But when it has been introduced into the things, then you command them to sneeze, and make that person sneeze under certain conditions, for it will be fractured if God wills it.
And if it is not fractured as we have recommended, then you return the patient gradually until you place them in their natural position, for it will then be fractured easily and appropriately.
And if it is prohibited by all that we have recommended, then take musellage of hart's flesh and dissolved gum, and pound them together well in the mortar, and then apply this mixture to the woman’s groin, at the base of her abdomen. And having placed the razor in cold water, until it affects the hypochondria.
And when the upper parts of the body are inflamed, then apply the smoke of myrtle and let it act on them. And when the smoke treatment lasts for an hour, then place a razor on a comb, and afterward make the patient sneeze and open their mouth wide, and remove it, and press the lower part of the abdomen lightly, because the result will be expelled soon after.
And collect the expelled matter along the lateral side with one of their hands. Here is the translation:
And if it cannot be done, then place it on the bed and crush the substances with strong pounding, and afterwards apply plasters which I have recommended made of mucilaginous substances.
And if the time of childbirth has come, then make her sit on it, and afterwards study the reduction of the hand, and restore the fetus according to its natural figure, and make the woman sneeze and command him to compress himself inward so that he may be expelled.
Examine the fetus on its back, extending its limbs and wrap it with bandages, starting from both sides of its larger part. Let the midwife hold the hand of the fetus and then gently turn it little by little, so that its delivery is facilitated.
