The outcome of twins or multiple offspring. Know that twins are born rarely, and sometimes three or four [are born], but this is strange and happens seldom; yet these causes are due to nature, not the mother.
And among them, whether there are many or few, a second birth occurs, and they are separated by the navel string. They distinguish each of these entirely through the umbilical cord attached to each one. And all of this must be known by the midwife as it is very important for her to recognize these signs correctly, and thus she can act appropriately in what is necessary.
Chapter LXXVI.
On the Extraction of a Dead Fetus.
When you treat a dead fetus as we have said in the division of treatment and it is not expelled, and you are forced to use surgical intervention at this point, then you must consider whether the woman is of sound constitution and not subject to any of the conditions for which her safety is feared.
At this point, the woman should be placed on her back on the bed with her buttocks raised and her head lowered.
The attendant should hold both of her legs or tie her to the bed so that her body does not extend during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you should moisten the mouth of the mother with oils and honey, and with a mixture of flax, fenugreek, and linseed.
And then the midwife should anoint her hand with those oils and honey, and then gently insert her hand into the mother, and then search with her hand for the place where the hooks can be placed in the fetus.
And the appropriate places for this are as follows: then you should consider whether the fetus is positioned so that it will descend head first, and now you place the hooks in its hands, or around its neck, or in its mouth, or on its palate, or under its chin, or at the base of its throat, or in the proper areas of its back, or near its hips.
But if the descent is foot first, then in the buttocks, or in the middle of its back.
Take the needle with your right hand and place its curve at that point which is between the fingers of your left hand, and insert it into one of those places we have mentioned, until it reaches the diseased area.
And then place and insert another needle next to it, or a third if necessary, so that the traction is equal, and not an extension according to its straightness alone.
And do this to all parts, so that the extraction of it is eased just as it is done with the teeth during their extraction.
And it is necessary in the place where the extension should be softened; and if any part of it is retained, then it is necessary for the midwife to anoint some of the things mentioned with oil and insert them carefully and turn what is retained.
Therefore, if a part of the fetus is dislodged, then the needles should be moved to the place where it is easier; and do this until the entire fetus is delivered.
But if the hand is dislocated in front of the other, and it is not possible to reduce it due to its contraction, then a bandage should be wrapped around it; and afterward, extract it until it is completely out, and cut it either at the joint of the hand or at the elbow.
And do the same for the other hand and both feet.
But if the fetus's head is large, or if it causes contraction during delivery, or if it is in the tightly fitting part of the water bag, then you should insert between the fingers and cut open the head to drain the water, or use an instrument called “alundat,” the shape of which will be among the forms of other instruments in the chapter that follows this one.
And it should be done as if it were being done to a large-headed person by nature: and now it is necessary either to cut that thing which is bound, or to take away that thing which must be removed as we have said; and then extract from the bones with the forceps.
And if the head is raised and is compressed by the clavicle, then it should be split so that the moisture which is in the sinews may be effused.
But if it is not complicated and does not rise excessively, then you should cut the head immediately as much as is possible for you.
And if the lower part of the abdomen is diseased or is in a state of hydrops, then it is necessary for you to puncture that place where the moisture circulates within.
But if the condition is above the pubis, then the attraction of it is distant and the direction of it towards the mouth of the urethra is also distant.
But if it is compressed by the abdomen and the muscles, then it is necessary for you to extract that cloth which you have wrapped around your hand, and to cut open the abdomen and the muscles so that the fluid that is within can flow out.
But if the other members remain stationary and only the head turns, then place your left hand between them; but if the mouth of the urethra is open, then insert your hand into the prepuce, and search for the head there, and pull it together with those mentioned into the mouth of the urethra, and then insert one or two fingers, and pull it with these.
And if the mouth of the urethra is compressed by an abscess that occurs at it, then it should not be irritated further; instead, you should administer effusions of moist and fatty substances. Apply plasters; and follow with waters that soften and moisten.
And if the condition is on the side of the patient, then as is possible, prick there; and administer the water that we have mentioned in the case of a live wound.
And if that is not possible, then cut it into pieces and extract it.
And make sure that you do not leave any cause for the second condition.
And when you have completed your entire operation, then administer what we have mentioned about the treatment for abscesses similar to those that occurred previously.
And if bleeding occurs, then proceed with astringent waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have seen a woman in whose abdomen there was a dead fetus, and above that focus another pregnancy occurred, and above the other focus the fetus is also dead. It happened to someone who had an abscess near their navel for a long time; it became swollen and internally filled with pus, and began to emit pus.
And I was called to treat them, and treated them for a long time, but the condition did not improve.
Then I applied some causes of strong attraction to them in the form of plasters, and extracted one bone from them, and after a few days, another bone; and I was very surprised, as there were no bones in the abdomen.
Then I thought that they were the bones of the dead fetus.
Thus, when examining the wound, remove the bones that are present in its various positions, and if you have seen it in such a manner for some time, correct it with a small force.
And do not be surprised if it seems strange, for in this lies the science and assistance to what the skilled physician uses his hand for in administering treatment.
Of the forms of the instruments that are necessary to extract the fetus.
Form of a spiral in which the mother is operated:
This is the form of a clamp in which it equalizes the leaves, to this clamping there are two spirals at the ends of two arms. However, this spiral should be finer than a needle and should be made of copper or the wood of trees.
And the width of each arm should be two fingers, and the margin of each at both ends should be one finger, and the length of each is a palm and a half; and in the middle of the two arms there are two adjustments made of that same wood, which are fixed, their length being half a palm plus a bit more, and the width of each is two fingers or slightly more.
And these two attachments should be inserted into the mouth of the mother so that they are used when you rotate the two prescribed spirals.
Another similar form, finer and more lightweight:
It should be made of copper or wood, resembling the form of forceps. However, at the extremity of the bone to which it is added, make an addition as many times as necessary, and the length of each such addition is about one palm, and the width of each is two of these.
And when you want to open any of them, then you should have the woman lie on her back with her legs hanging down, because that is how the parts are positioned. And then open your hand with the instrument as you would with scissors, proportionally according to the quantity you want from the size of the opening of the womb until the sounder does what you wish.
The form of dizziness which the ancients described:
Form of an impulse with which the fetus is pushed:
Form of association in which the large head is contained:
And it is similar to forceps, at the end of which there are teeth as many times as necessary, and some versions are made long like forceps according to this form, as many times as necessary, with teeth like those of a comb in which the head is grasped and contained. Form of association for few:
Form of one having a spine:
Form of one having two spines:
Form of a spatum having two ends with which the head is divided:
Form of two spatulas:
With those instruments so exercised, and when the various kinds of them are multiplied and prepared by the artisans, it becomes easier for their operation and better for serving people according to their needs.
And you should prepare whatever is needed from those instruments, for the necessity of them cannot be excused.
Of the extraction of the second [incision or procedure].
When the second birth is retained during childbirth, you should instruct the patient to hold their breath, and then make them sneeze under certain conditions. Place your hand over the mouth and nose of the patient, and if it comes out this way, it is good.
And if not, then take a hollow object and pierce it through the cover, placing it over the mouth of the mother with herbs such as chamomile, rue, anise, camomilla, Arabian saffron, cinnamon, and sage. All of these herbs or some of them should be submerged in water in the hollow object, and place it over the fire.
And then place a reed tube in the mouth of the mother, and hold it there so that the vapor reaches the cavity of the mother.
And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the second birth will be quickly expelled.
But if this treatment does not work immediately and it is not expelled, then instruct the midwife to immerse her left hand in sesame oil or masticated elderflower, and insert it into the womb and search for the second birth with it. When you find it, take it out gradually piece by piece. If it is attached to the placenta of the mother, then insert her hand as we have recommended, and when she finds the second birth, extract it little by little according to what is appropriate so that the mother does not suffer from excessive attraction. Take care that it is turned to the right and left with ease.
And if the mouth of the mother is obstructed, we have already recommended treatment through sneezing and medication of the wound with herbs.
But if it is not expelled by all the causes we have recommended, then guard against overworking it through repeated attempts at extraction. Instead, what emerges from her to the mouth of the mother should be connected to the woman’s body part, and subsequently treated with cautery and tetrapharmaca; for that will allow it to purge itself gradually over several days and will dissolve and be expelled.
But when it purges, it raises harmful vapors to the head and stomach, and this aggravates the illness; therefore, it is necessary to administer sublimations and appropriate treatments for that.
But some of the Ancients already used sublimation of natrtle and dried figs.
Form of an instrument with which a woman is subjected to sublimation due to the retention of menstruation and during her second period:
It should be made of wood or metal.
And the narrow end should be placed in the vagina and the wider end over the fire, and the sublimation should be placed among the coals of the fire.
And keep that instrument inside until the sublimation is consumed and then repeat with another.
Of the treatment of the unopened anus of a child.
Many children are born with their anuses not open, which are small and closed. Then it is necessary for the midwife to carefully open the mentioned orifice with her finger; and if she cannot do so, she should use a sharp probe to make an opening, being careful not to injure the rectum.
And then apply a poultice made of boiled wool soaked in wine and oil; and afterwards, treat the opening until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is obstructed, then place a small tube inside for several days, and remove it when the child needs to pass gas.
And it also happens that the anus becomes obstructed due to the hardening of an abscess or swelling; then it is necessary to incise that hardness, and afterwards treat it as we have said regarding treatment and lead.
Of the treatment of fistulas that occur below.
Fistulas that occur in the lower part near the anus are often hard and thick, which appear close to the anus or in the area nearby.
And it is one fistula or multiple.
And as that hardness subsides and discharges watery or slightly purulent fluid, and in some cases these fistulas penetrate deeper.
And those that penetrate deeper are known by what is discharged from them during evacuation and by gas formation; and sometimes the discharge indicates a poor outcome, and it is easily discernible whether these conditions will be severe.
Of those fistulas, some extend into the space penetrating into the bladder, and into the shaft of the penis; and similarly, some penetrate into the joint of the thigh and into the root of the bone.
And in that which is known as a penetrating fistula into the anus and a non-penetrating one, it is that you insert your finger into the anus, and introduce a fine probe of horn or iron into the fistula when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in that area, then introduce a fine probe of lead or horsehair into it until you feel the probe or the hair with your finger.
And if you do not feel any of this nor find any cause for evacuation such as worms or flatulence, as we have said, then you know that it is non-penetrating, and at this point you can proceed with the treatment.
And when the fistula is penetrating into the bladder or into the shaft of the penis, then the treatment involves draining the urine from that area and prohibiting anything that would hinder the healing process, so that the affected site may be consolidated.
But if it is penetrating into the joint of the thigh or into the bone and causes pain in the circumference, then the indication is that the probe passes easily here if there is no tortuosity within the bone, and causes pain in the circumference of the affected area; and you will have a persistent fever; and nothing works in this treatment, nor does it heal without difficulty.
And in all those penetrating fistulas, there is no complete cure, and the treatment of these involves the labor and futility of physicians, upon which such treatments rely.
In that which is not penetrating and not ancient, healing without instruments is hoped for as I will tell you, and this is what I and my predecessors have done.
And it is that the patient lies between your hands on his back and raises the flanks up from him, the things being inclined towards his belly, and then insert a probe of lead or iron if it is in the fistula tortuosity, until you know the place where the probe passes.
And if it is around the anus that the patient feels pain, then you should insert your finger into the anus there.
And if you feel with your finger that the probe has already begun to penetrate on its own, then what you feel between your finger and the sphincter or flesh, you will certainly know that it is penetrating; and then do not work on it, for there is no healing in that, as we have said.
But some might say that it heals in some men slowly.
And for the treatment in which help is hoped for, you should ignite a cautery subtly according to the size of the fistula, as was described earlier, and insert that burning object into the fistula until it goes around the anus; and then repeat this two or three times until you know that all those inflamed tissues around the fistula are now constricted like the quill of a bird.
And then treat that with bandages wrapped in butter until all those constricted tissues heal, and then treat it with medicines that generate and consolidate the flesh. If it heals, that is good; but if not, then it will not heal at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters and does not penetrate inside, say to which part it reaches in the backside; between that and the probe there is a thin layer of flesh or cicatrix, and sometimes the fistula is situated on what appears as the surface of the buttock. At this time, the area from the beginning of the fistula dries up, and you see drying along with the probe, until the drying reaches the place where the end of the probe penetrates, and through the probe it is freed and cured.
And then cleanse those flesh areas affected by the fistula, which are similar to the tube of a bird's feather quill, without breaking any of them for lack of cause.
But if blood flows out when you are operating on those flesh areas, at that time the skillful thing to do and the proper instrument is to cauterize with fire or with sharp medicine, for cauterizing with fire adjusts two good habits: it tightens the adjusted flesh areas and stops the bleeding by drying up the moisture.
And then treat that with cloths wrapped in butter or in three layers of cloth until the place heals, and afterwards cure the wound with hard flesh remedies which generate and consolidate.
And medicines that have the property of being styptic and drying; when these are restored, it is healed and you need not fear recurrence.
But if the fistula has penetrated inside the anus and is extended from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the anus and examine it.
And therefore, if you feel the probe and between it and your finger there is a thin layer of cicatrix or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then at this point there is no operation to be performed except by faith and hope.
The cause is to treat it in one of three ways: either cauterize it as we have said; or dry it until you achieve the condition where it is possible for you to introduce some suitable remedy and proper treatment; and do not delay with drying because doing so could damage the rectum containing the bowel, and act upon the patient thusly without their will; and then cure that which we have mentioned as being light to heal; or extend that membrane and clean that pus which you dried inside towards the anus, as we have said, either by hand or by another sharp instrument, and then purify those ulcerated tissues which are visible during drying due to perforation; and then strive to restore the entire wound through drying, and if the upper pus openings do not heal, and the fistula remains in the part of the anus, it will be easier for the patient. Regarding the stitching of hemorrhoids in which pus flows during the incision of those areas.
Hemorrhoids occur in two ways: either they appear around the anus resembling coal lumps, or they are like grape clusters. And there are small and large ones among them, and the pus flows from these constantly and is one continuous flow, and they appear both inside and outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that appear outside the anus are due to less moisture, from which clear water or a little blood mixed continuously flows, and this depends on the color of the skin.
And the treatment for those that are inside the anus is to instruct the patient to sit in such a way that the anus opens and the hemorrhoids become visible to you. Then you should cover your hands and use hooks or what you have to grasp them with your nails, and then cut those [hemorrhoids] along their roots.
If the hooks do not hold in those places because of their moisture and softness, then take a rough cloth or a cork or a flaxen bandage, and extract them with your fingers, and immediately powder some powders around the incision to serve as cauterization for them; or cauterize them as was described earlier in the chapter on cauterizing. And immediately treat them with other methods until they are healed.
And if the pus does not flow out, then make an incision with a lancet in the place where there is a small bite, and wash it with the water that is in the bowel so that the pus flows out quickly when it is pressed.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then their cause is easily treated, and you should take them with your fingernail or hook, and incise them, and then treat them as we have said until they heal.
And for those who require suturing, proceed as follows: take silk wrapped around a needle and then extract the hemorrhoids from above at the parts, and make the needle penetrate to the other side at the base, and tie the two ends of the thread below the needle horizontally; tighten the hemorrhoid firmly with the structure, and then knot the silk and extract the needle.
And do this for all of them, leaving one unsewn so that the excess blood can flow from it.
And place on the wound a cloth soaked in rose oil or in cerate; and place that on the wound and order the patient to rest. And place that in a container; and when it is prepared, apply those remedies or another treatment until it is healed.
And when they are old and do not respond to that medicine, you should scrape those areas with the point of a scalpel or your fingernail until they become moist and allow the pus to drain from them, which prevents their consolidation; and then treat them until they heal.
Now you should scrape away the pus more vigorously than at first until some bleeding occurs and the area is inflamed; and then treat them until they are healed.

Of warts, it is a small raised rough fistulated round prominence on the surface of the body, and they appear in most children.
The treatment for them is to cut them open and extract what lies within, whether it be fat or hair. And if you wish, cauterize that area, as we have said, either with fire or with a sharp medicine.
Of ant bite marks, they are small raised or large fistulated prominences on the surface of the body often accompanied by pain, and when touched, they feel like an ant bite, and they occur around the body.
The treatment for them is to take a needle made of eagle feather or vulture feather, and it should be strong, and place that on the ant bite within the circle of the needle; then rotate your hand with the needle until you cut out what is around the ant bite, and press it into the flesh so that you eradicate the ant bite from its root.

Sometimes castration is performed with instruments made of silver or lead or melted and hammered metal; and sometimes they are made of small or large instruments according to those to whom they are administered, for the one who is treated in the cure of young children should be small; and for those whose testicles are tender or painful, their castrating instruments should be smaller.
The form of the large and few castration instruments:
The head of the cannula above, where the bag is tied, should be wide according to this manner; and at the place where the bag is tied it should be as you see.

And the bag in which the medicines are placed should be made of animal bladder or sheep's wool, and the length of it should be one palm.
And it should be perforated with threads as it is rolled; and the space between the threads should be as thick as a finger.
And then insert into those threads strong honey from raw honey, which should be of this amount, or around it; and adjust the bag accordingly; and when the bag is placed on it, it should be tight at the head of the instrument between two distinct tight bindings, and then castrate with that each thread: But the quality of castrating with that is that you fill the bag with medicine, which should be heated according to the quantity tolerated by the patient; and take care not to make it too hot or too cold.
Let the patient lie on their back, and raise his buttocks with something under him like a cushion or a leather pad; then anoint the tip of the cautery with oil or egg white or fenugreek mucilage or similar substances. Then gently insert it into the anus until it is fully inside, and the assistant squeezes the bag vigorously so that everything in the rectum is evacuated; then remove the instrument from the rectum and allow the patient to lower his buttocks.
And if the medicine induces vomiting, let it continue as long as possible; and if he can sleep through the entire night, that is better for recovery.
But if you treat such diseases of abdominal fluid and vomiting with the cautery, then continue using the cautery until he is healed.

On the treatment of plagues.
I have already stated in the division of the treatment of plagues what was appropriate for the book, and I will now describe the treatment of those with the plague using medicines according to the regimen.
Therefore, I say that plagues are diverse depending on what causes the plagues and depending on the location where the plague occurs.
And the causes for which plagues occur are such as stone formation, or incision of sword or knife, or striking with a lance, or stick, or arrow, or similar to those from various causes.
Here are the plagues that are in the parts of the body such as the plague which occurs on the head, the neck, the feet, the belly, the groin, and similar ones to those affecting the limbs.
And I also recommend the treatment of certain plagues, so that you pause that reasoning and rule regarding other plagues.
And I will begin with the simple plagues of the head, for the recall of the compound ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Then I say that when you encounter a simple plague on the head and it is not a fracture, you should consider: if it is due to a blow from a stone or something similar, and if only the skin has been broken but there is great swelling and you fear that an abscess might form internally, then you must proceed immediately and perform trepanation of the skull without delay; do not postpone this unless there is some prohibitive reason for not doing so, especially if little blood comes out from the wound.
And let the trepanation be on the opposite side of the affected area; and keep the patient from overeating and from eating heavy foods.
But if some reason prevents you from performing it immediately, then reduce the patient's food intake and do not allow them to consume meat or rich foods.
And be cautious about the plague, if an internal abscess occurs, and apply only rose oil or in a liquid that has been stypticized.
But if you are sure of an internal abscess, then be cautious about the wound, especially if it is fresh and the blood and air have not yet changed the powder that I recommend applying lightly.
And if the air has already changed that, pause at certain intervals until it is healed.
And if the wound is large or due to incision with a sharp object and the lips of the wound do not come together by themselves, then adjust those with sutures as I will describe concerning suture.
And if the wound is already exposed to the bone and sinew, and there is no need for it to be held together except with a small suspender, then incise it and immediately treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh in place of the suspender, and instruct the patient to eat foods that are nutritious: meat, fish, and similar items until they recover.
And if you find putrefaction in the suspender and it does not adhere to the bone, then cut away all of that rotten part and remove it, and then treat it.
And if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood flow can occur, and it does not stop with medicines, then inquire about the artery, and if you find that uncut, then cut it with a scalpel, or ligate it; and if necessity compels you to do what we have said does not suffice, then cauterize until the bleeding stops.
And if these wounds are simple and small, the cause of them is minor; sprinkle this powder on them, as it will consolidate the wound before the air changes them.
And the method for the powder is as follows: take oil of sesame one part, dragon's blood two parts, and burnt frankincense three parts; mix them thoroughly and sieve it, then fill the wound with it, and secure it with good compression so that it adheres to the wound, and let it coagulate with the blood.
And apply plasters above the poultices and leave them on without removing them while it is still such that it has not become an abscess, for medicine does not eradicate it until it is healed.
And sometimes this cure is done solely when it is not at hand the juice and blood of dragon.
And this method of operation is performed on large wounds when it is necessary to place them flat and narrow, and for fresh wounds to apply their blood, which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, especially when it is in summer, then do not apply this powder to their condition, but instead treat them as we have said, placing over them some cold applications or applying flour poultices with water and honey until they begin to rot; and then cure them with the other treatment until they are healed.
But if it happens that the wound is a fracture of the bone, and it is small, then you extract it with your hands.
And I will describe the treatment of fractures of the head in what follows.
Of wounds to the neck.
But if the wound occurs on the neck, then there is no difference in treatment, and between a simple wound of the head, it is the same.
But if the nerve of the neck is cut or an artery is severed, then if it is a nerve, there should be no immediate treatment but rather avoid any cure that strongly constricts, such as zinc ore and similar substances which harm the nerves and contract them. Do not pause on any cause of the plague, for the substance of the nerves is affected and their continuity goes up to the brain, which is the source of all members.
And if this is the case where someone is treated with mild medicines, such as being washed with warm water, sometimes infused in rose oil or non-rose oil, and then dried when washed with warm water, and other stones are applied according to this method, and moisture is found.
But if the wound is severe, then administer sutures, or close its edges, and afterward treat it with powders until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep or if there is corruption in its deepest part, then pierce it at the most affected location.
But if an artery is already cut and bleeding occurs, then ligate or cauterize it if the necessity arises for such action.
But if the vertebrae of the neck or the entire spine have already been cut, and only the veins of the neck remain intact, then you should close the edges of the wound with sutures near the bone of the neck without touching it; first equalize it and reduce it to its natural shape, and afterward strengthen it with proper support, and leave it for several days; then treat it with powders that have astringent and excitatory properties until it is healed.
But if the wound has already become infected in the bone, then probe it, and if there are fragments of bone in it, remove all that you can; and what is not suitable for removal, leave it until the wound becomes pus-filled, and the swelling subsides.
And if the wound is fresh, sprinkle over it the powder which has been recommended. And if a fistula is formed in the wound, now treat it with the liquid whose description will come shortly in its chapter.
If it is a thrust wound from a spear or knife, and you feel its depth, now consider whether there is swelling when the injured person inhales; then you will know that the wound is fatal.
And if it is not deep and the wound is fresh with its own blood, now apply powder to it during the first hour, and do not press on it so that the blood does not remain trapped in its depth and return to the heart to injure it, but apply a binding dressing, and change it twice a day.
And if the dressing is not available to you, now place over the wound opening a piece of cloth to absorb what is left of the moisture; and make the injured person lie on the wound so that it can run out whatever remains within.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound and no spasms, trembling of heart, or constriction of breath has occurred, and you see other good signs from it, then you know that the wound is safe; and then treat it with bandages and the usual care until it is healed.
But if healing of it is delayed and much time has passed, then you know that a fistula has formed; treat it therefore according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from a slash with a knife or a sword, and it is simple on the surface of the skin or the flesh, then treat it as you would with stitching if it is large, or with powder if it is small.
If there has already been inflammation in the bone, and some of the severed pieces are loose, then you should turn the wound and try to extract those pieces if they are separate. But if they are not separate, then leave them inside until the wound heals, because their removal is difficult.
And for other wounds in the other limbs, the treatment during healing is as we have described.

Of two other sutures proper communicate with other wounds, but however more in the suture of the abdomen.

Therefore I say that when the wound is small and it is caused by some reason in the bowel, and it is difficult to reduce that, then there are two ways: either due to the smallness of the rupture as we have said, or when inflammation has come to the bowel due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore when that is so, then it is necessary for you to warm that in such a way that you submerge a sponge or cloth in lukewarm water alone, or soak it in hot water with cyperus and spica, and apply it to the bowel until the inflammation is resolved.

One part of the sharp side of the bone, and the other part not sharp, and the extremity subtle, and not like the subtlety of dust, but rather slightly blunt.

But when the incision is wide and in the lower part of the abdomen, now it is necessary that you make the patient lie on their back, and place their arms closer to their head; and if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then you place the head and feet higher than the lower parts.
And similarly, if the incision is on one of the two sides of the abdomen, now you should always direct your attention and consideration so that the part where the wound is lies higher than the other.
And so you should administer this for large wounds and for smaller wounds as well.
And for small wounds, place what is at hand ready, and then have the one most skilled among you perform it, who should hold the incision entirely with their hand and close the lips of it, and then proceed to suture it appropriately.
And this is the common method of suturing: take a needle, or a needle holder according to the quantity and amplitude of the wound; then lay it from the extremity of the incision at the ring finger, and tighten the needle once, ensuring that you do not place thread at both ends of the stitch, but rather between the two edges of the wound interiorly; and when you have brought together both ends of the stitch and both edges of the wound, this makes four stitches.
Take a needle and double the thread around it, and pass two sutures from both sides upwards, then bring together the two lips of the wound and the suture by appropriate joining; then take the thickness of your finger and place another needle in that, then tie that knot as you did with the first needle; and do not stop doing this if necessary with the needles until you have completed the entire contraction of the wound.
And let the needles be medium in coarseness and subtlety, for fine needles enter the flesh quickly; and thick ones are difficult to insert into the tissue, and therefore it is appropriate that they be medium in both subtlety and thickness.
And it is not appropriate for the needle to be inserted at both ends of the wound simultaneously, because this would cause the flesh to be cut too quickly and the wound might heal improperly before consolidation.
And do not extend the sutures too much so that the wound is not hindered from proper consolidation.
And then cut the ends of the needles so that they do not bother the patient while sleeping; and place poultices spread out around the sutures, which should cover the ends of the needles; and leave them in place until you know that the wound has already consolidated.
And this type of suture with needles is most convenient for small wounds, as you only need one or two needles or around that number.
However, the second type of suture is common: it involves managing four ends of the sutures, from both sides of the wound and its two extremities, in one go, with a needle that is appropriately balanced in subtlety and thickness.
When the needle has penetrated these four extremities, withdraw the needle from that part of its division where you began, so that the suture retreats from the pus most remote part to the wound, so that the seam is a thread suture along with which the threads are tightly drawn.
And pause between each suture the quantity of the thickness of the little finger.
And this suture is known to people.
And in such a suture I sewed the wound that had been inflicted on a man's belly with a knife, and the disruption of the wound was greater than one palm, and it was situated around two palms from the midgut and the disruption was at the center of the limb; then I reduced it after the gut had been outside the wound for twenty-four hours.

And do this one side and then the other, and do this one stitch after another until you sew up everything that is outside according to that example.

Recollection of the injuries that occur in the bowel.
When a rupture occurs in the bowel and it is small, it is possible that it can be repaired in some people, because I saw one who had been injured in his abdomen by a lance wound, and the injury was on the right side of the stomach, and the injury was old and had become a fistula, with discharge and gas.
Then I bandaged the wound, although I was not confident in its healing, but I carefully examined that part which would heal and where the tissue is solidified.
And when I saw the place already consolidated, I warned about the harm that could come from this bad event in his abdomen; and no one suffered further harm due to this accident by changing their eating and drinking habits, and subsequently taking baths and exercising as advised.
And some experienced individuals say that when there is a small injury in the bowel, it should be treated as follows: take ants with large heads; then bring together the edges of the wound and place one of those ants, which has its mouth open, on the edges of the wound. When it bites onto them and tightens its mouth, cut off its head because it adheres and does not dissolve.
And then place another ant next to the first one, and do not stop placing ants one next to the other according to the quantity of the entire wound.
And then reduce that and close the wound; and let those heads remain in the bowel until the bowel is healed; and may no harm come to the patient.
It is possible that the gut suture be tied with fine thread made from animal intestine, as it is more suitable for needles; and you should take one end of this gut suture, which is bare, and tie it to one end of the fine silk thread, wrapped around it, and then insert that thread into needles and knot it with the gut, and reduce it at the point.

But if the wound is large and wide, especially if it involves one of the fine intestines, then at that time there is only a delicate treatment, and not a complete healing of it.
On the treatment of abscesses and fistulas.
Know that every wound and abscess when it becomes old and chronic, and forms an ulcer, and is not consolidated and continues to discharge for a long time without being excised, whether in any part of the body, is generally called a fistula, and we refer to it as such an abscess.
And a fistula is indeed a felted node, hard and white, at which there is no pain but to which there is a discharge, just as there is a discharge from the sore of a bird; hence it is called a cockle or sore.
And it is in some people a moist sore, which discharges continuously; and the moisture is often excised within a few hours. And at times this moisture is thick and little, and it is coarse or fine.
I have shown all those causes, the signs of them, and their cure with medicines in the division.
And it is necessary for me to tell you about the treatment of fistulas, which is done through surgical operation.
And you should know that fistulas can occur in all parts of the body; among them are fistulas that extend to large veins and arteries, or to nerves, or to the spleen, or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to any of the ribs, or to some of the vertebrae of the back, or to any of the compound joints such as those of the hand and foot; and there are also those that affect the proximity of principal members and similar parts.
And therefore, among these fistulas that occur in this manner, they are among the diseases difficult to heal, and do not readily receive treatment, and dealing with them requires hard work and ignorance.
And if any of the fistulas has a depth that does not reach the places mentioned above, and you hope for and trust in its cure, and wish to gain knowledge of it, then take a probe of iron or steel, and if the fistula runs straight ahead, explore it with this.
And if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate it with a probe of thin lead, for the lightness of its body causes it to be easily guided and curved around the tortuosities.
And if the fistula has openings that are horrible or difficult to approach, it is not within your power to signify upon them with a probe.
Then inject through one opening for the others, for the moisture you inject will go to the other openings and flow from them.
And then inquire carefully according to the method by which you can know if here there is bone or nerve, and whether the depth of the fistula is long or short, or whether it is a fistula with openings at both ends, over all this within your strength, within your thorough investigation, within which you investigate the malady, and the invention of the pain according to your compression on the spot, and similar signs and accidental causes related to an abscess.

On the treatment of hemorrhoids and the quality of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in any fleshy member and the space of the abscess is extended inward so that it forms a collection of pus, and then it bursts and perforates, and the pus is discharged from there, leaving the place empty as if it were a vessel, and the skin above it remains thin like cloth, and has not yet reached corruption, nor does it impress upon bone or nerve or tendon, it is called a sinus, and it is not named fistula.
However, if the space extends inward so that corruption impresses upon some member, now it is called a fistula and not a sinus.
And the treatment for it is to incise that skin completely as it has been formed, and especially when it is thin like cloth, and ensure that you do not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
However, if you hope that the skin will adhere and that the corruption has not yet reached its final stage, and there is pus passing through the skin, now you should treat it with enemas.

From there extract that which is held in little regard; and do this for so many days until the sinus is cleansed and the pus is evacuated.
And on some occasions it is administered as an enema with water of ashes, when this medicine is not present at your disposal; and you should take the ashes of wheat, put them over the water; and then filter that, and administer it as an enema until you are confident that the sinus is now closed.
And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the water of ashes, then administer it with water and honey mixed thoroughly three times; and administer it with vinegar and honey mixed, because the property of honey is to cleanse and wash, and the vinegar makes the sinus adhere, especially when the vinegar has an excess of astringency and dryness.
And as you do this on some occasions and become confident that the sinus is now evacuated, then administer an enema with something that promotes flesh growth.
And you should take of the decoction of dyafinicon and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent vinegar; or administer that along with some medicines and other decoctions which have the properties I have already written about in the chapter on decoctions.

Of the extraction of the leg vein.
This vein is generated in the camels in warm lands such as Al-Ategi in the land of Arabs and in the warm and dry lands of India where no grass or food grows.

And its gestation is from putrefaction that occurs below the skin, just as worms and vermin come from within the body of serpents and other creatures, generating between the skin and the flesh.

And when the extremity of it becomes apparent, then you should wrap around it a piece of lead weighing one drachma within two drachmas, and secure that area, leaving the lead suspended from the camel.
And so many times when you need something for the parts outside, wrap it in lead and attach that. And if it is elongated, then you cut a part of it and wrap the remainder; and do not cut that which is ragged until it has come out, for if you cut that which is ragged it contracts and goes into the flesh, causing an abscess, putrefaction in the place, and a bad ulceration.
It is necessary that it be treated gently and be extracted little by little until it is completely removed from the rear of it and there remains nothing of it in the body.
And this vein is found in some people extending to the length of five palms and I have been told that it has reached up to twenty palms.

Regarding the section on worms generated under the skin and it is called bovine illness.
This illness is called in some lands bovine disease because at times it appears among cattle and is a worm that is born between the skin and the flesh, and moves throughout the body up and down, and is shown through the sensation near the affected part of the member by member until it breaks the skin and comes out.

Regarding the treatment of nasal illness.
This illness is called "nafir" in our land, and it is pain that occurs in certain limbs, and then it is transferred from one limb to another.
And I have already seen that as I will now recount to you. I went to see a sick woman in a village, and uncovered her arm, where I found a small inflammation in the funis vein of the arm. After removing it for about an hour, I saw that this swelling subsided as quickly as wax melts under heat, but more rapidly than could be expected, as if it were living silver flowing from place to place.
Then the pain moved from that location to the muscle, and it remained there for about an hour.
And then it quickly moved to the other side of her body and reached the other arm.
And then she told me that it was moving throughout her body just as I had observed, and I was greatly amazed by the change and the speed at which it moved from one limb to another. And I had seen this before in my own patients in the manner described, but I have never witnessed such rapid pain moving from one limb to another. But I did not see this in my patients as I did in the woman.
And I thought this was due to the fact that the woman was elderly, with weak limbs and exposed veins, and in her, this windiness appeared as changing sensations.
And it is necessary that this does not appear in those who are delicate and have moist bodies with engorged veins.
And when you want to treat it and the patient feels that pain, immediately apply heat to that area as we have said, then press firmly on it until the confined windiness is expelled, and cauterize the location.
If you do not apply it to your wounds, then treat that with massage of the body and with what expels windiness, and anoint those with stinking pills and with Serapion pills and similar things.
Of the extraction of splinters.
Splinters are diverse according to their types and the parts of the body where they lodge, for their diversity is such that some have large points, others small, concave or blunt, solid ones as well. And there are those with three angles, and among them are those with tongues; and also slender pieces.
And the signs of the brain when struck by a splinter and it penetrates the bone, and exits at the fontanelle which is above the brain, are caused by severe heat, scotoma, vertigo, rigidity in the wounds, inflammation and redness of the tongue, spasms and confusion of understanding, vomiting of bile, and often speech is cut off and the voice destroyed. Moisture similar to flour exits from the affected area, and the appearance indicates an abundance of fluid in the flesh.
And so if I recognize these signs in you, then refrain from treatment and extraction of the arrow, unless it is outside.
Note the signs of the arrow when it hits the heart, so that it is near the left nipple, and feel how it is lodged in a hard place, not soft tissue; and the movement of the arrow is similar to the pulse movement, and there is black blood from the wound, and the extremities become cold, and sweating occurs, and fainting; therefore, you will know that death approaches very soon.
And the signs when the arrow has pierced the lung are frothy blood from the wound, and the veins near the neck become swollen, and the patient's complexion changes, and they exhale a great breath, and crave for fresh air.
And if the arrow hits the membrane that is in the legs, and the pain at this moment is near the small ribs, it causes great distress and severe pain and sighing, and there is no movement of any part of the limbs.
And if the arrow hits the stomach, then very quickly you will see that the wound expels any undigested food.
And if the arrow hits the abdomen, and it is lodged there, you will see some opening from the wound, or the membrane, or the intestine which is ruptured, and there is no hope for recovery from the treatment or extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow hits the bladder, and urine comes out from it, and some cause of infection spreads to other parts externally, and quickly there is pain over the patient, then you will know that it is fatal.
Thus the other members as well as in the face, the neck, and the groin, and the shoulders and the helpers, and the vertebrae of the back, and the collarbone, and the ribs, and similar parts of the limbs are saved according to the major dispositions when the arrow does not find a vein or artery and is not a poisonous arrow.
And I will recount this to you as I have seen regarding the nature of these arrows, so that you may understand about their treatment.
The reason for this is that an arrow was embedded in the lacrimal bone at the root of his nose, and I extracted it from the other side, leaving behind only the grey matter of the ear, and he healed without any harm coming to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which had embedded in him at the grey matter of his eye, leaving behind only the eyelid. The arrow was already concealed and could not be completely removed except for a small part to which it adhered to its shaft. It was a large sharp arrow of the Turks, with a lion-shaped iron tip that did not have barbs.
And the Jew recovered, and no harmful incident occurred to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man who had it embedded in his groin, and it was an Arabic arrow with barbs. Then I incised around it between two grooves, and it was already submerged in his groin. Then I carefully removed it, and the Christian man recovered.
And I extracted another arrow from a man who had it embedded in his abdomen, and he was thought to die because of it. However, after thirty days had passed and there was no change in his condition regarding the arrow, I incised around the arrow and removed it carefully, and he recovered without any harmful incident occurring to him.
And in one man an arrow was embedded in both sides of him, and the wound had healed over it, and after seven years the arrow emerged at the root of his spine.
And there was a woman in whom an arrow had penetrated her abdomen, and the wound had healed over it internally, and she did not change in any of her conditions regarding it, and the woman did not suffer any discomfort during any of her natural functions.
And there was another man in whom an arrow had penetrated his face and the wound had healed over it, breaking off the arrow, experiencing no pain hereafter, and similar cases are like this.
And the arrow was extracted from the man who was the prince of the king; it had penetrated just below his nose, and it had slightly shifted towards the right side, and the entire arrow was concealed beneath the skin.
And I was called to treat him regarding the embedding of the arrow within three days.
And then I found the wound of the arrow quite narrow; and then I examined it carefully with a probe, but did not feel anything with it; however, he felt pain just below his right ear, and then I suspected that this pain was due to the tip of the arrow.
And I applied a plaster to the area containing an ingredient that attracts and digests foreign bodies, hoping that it would cause the location of the arrow to become apparent so that I could operate on it.
And no significant change occurred in the area that would indicate the arrow had reached it; and I continued to apply the plaster for several days without any incident; and meanwhile, the wound healed, and he complained of pain from the tip of it after some days until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and informed me about this.
Then I applied a sharp corrosive medicine for several days until it reached the bone, and when I tested it, I found that it had penetrated to the fine extremity of the sinew, which adheres to the shaft.
Then I adjusted the incision of the wound with that sharp medicine until it became visible to me clearly where the tendon ends, and it took me some time, over a period of four months, to complete this.
And as the wound reached a certain size and it became possible for me to insert forceps into it, I did not immediately remove it but rather continued to refine and devise methods of extraction until one day I began to extract it using specially made forceps, according to the form described at the end of this chapter; and then I restored the wound.
And some physicians judged that the cartilage of the nose could not be restored, but I did restore it, and the wound healed properly, and the patient recovered completely without any further cause for concern.

About phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins for phlebotomy of which the blood is commonly drawn are as follows: xiii in number, and there are sixteen in the head. Among these are two pulsating veins close to the ears, called alhasisain; two veins that appear on each temple; and two veins near the tear ducts under each eye, which are called aspicientz; a vein located at the middle part of the forehead; a vein at the tip of the nose; two veins in the neck; two veins on the upper lip; and two veins on the lower lip; — these latter are called algelic; — and there are also two veins beneath the tongue. Here are the veins that are phlebotomized in the arms: one of these is cephalic, and it is called wild rose; and people call it "the vein of the head," and sordas, and it is a median vein, and it is composed of one branch of basilica, and another branch of cephalic; and they name that one people "vein of the whole body"; and basilica is a vein which is placed on domestic rose, and it is called "cubital," and common people call this one "vein of the belly"; and flexor of the arm, and it lies over the humerus, and it is in that one in which bloodletting is performed, and it appears above the wrist by a manifest appearance; and savattella, and it is the vein that is between the thumb and the smallest finger, and it runs along both sides.
And in the foot and the ankle there are three veins: one of these is behind the curvature of the elbow of the wild rose; and the second is sophena, and its place is behind the heel of the domestic rose; and the sciatic vein, and its place is behind the heel of the wild rose; and in the ankle there are other veins similar to those.

Of the quality of phlebotomy it is that the patient immerses his arm in warm water and makes friction thereon until the veins appear; and he places a cord above the joint of the arm where it bends, and the place of the vein to be opened is apparent below the said joints, and from this spot branches extend over the surface of the arm like little branches.
And so, phlebotomize the largest branch below the bend beneath the joint of it, for it is better and more effective.
And if you phlebotomize that on the surface of the arm, then be careful of the nerves which are below that on the surface of the arm, and pause your phlebotomy when you feel one, as if you wanted to cut across it.
And so the phlebotomy is completed.
But if the emission of blood is prohibited; then immerse the arm of the patient in warm water and allow the blood to flow there until it is evacuated.
And if the phlebotomist errs on the first attempt, then repeat the phlebotomy slightly above or below it, for the location is effective; and do not fear this error, but be cautious of the nerves as we have said.
And similarly do the phlebotomy of the other arm in the same manner.
And for the sciatic vein, the location as we have said is below the joint behind the thigh.
And the benefit of phlebotomy is to alleviate pain in the hip when it is caused by excess blood.
Of the quality of the phlebotomy is that the patient enter into bath, and hasten and tighten the skin of him at the breadth between above the heel above four fingers in a bandage thin, for it appears not save by this.

And it is in words a man weak; and if you find that, and it does not appear to the sense of all, then phlebotomize some branch of him, and it is that which appears on the back of the hand behind the finger auricular and the finger anular.
And beware of the nerves, and let the blood flow in the quantity that you wish.
And then loosen the bandage and pause over the place of the phlebotomy cotton and tighten the place, for lightly it is healed.
Of bleeding and of the qualities of using those.
Bleeding is usually from veins, or arteries, or vessels.
And bleeding is done according to two methods: one of them is by incision and extraction of blood, and the other is bleeding without incision.
And this bleeding that is without incision is according to two methods, for it can be with heat or without heat.

Of bloodletting do not extract the blood except from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and by that you do not capture the virtue through those, just as it happens with phlebotomy; nor is it necessary for bloodletting to be done near fire or without fire in any disease caused by retention until the entire body is evacuated.
If necessity prompts bloodletting due to illness or continuously, administer that at any time at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, and in the middle of it and at some times.

The benefit of bloodletting from the neck is that when it alleviates heaviness in the head and what causes sinking of the eyes; but it should be done close to when the entire body is evacuated.

Thus begins the chapter of the third part: on restoration. This chapter is repeatedly necessary in the art of medicine; it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations, accidents involving the bones.
Oh, son, be aware that those who boast about this chapter are foolish doctors and the unlearned, as well as those who for some time have not regarded the books of the Ancients or read a single letter from them.
And for this reason it is done that this method of the scientists in our land is private; and I have not forgotten it for any length of time, nor have I acquired what follows except through prolonged reading of the books of the Ancients and studying to understand them, from which I extracted their knowledge.
And then I directed myself to experimentation and study throughout the duration of my life.
And I have already described in this chapter everything that contains my knowledge and upon which I have passed my experience; now I abbreviate it for you to free it from the occupation of lengthiness; and I abbreviate it at the end of abbreviation; and I show it at the end of clarification; and form many forms of the instruments used in that, as is done by addition of explanation, just as I have done in the two preceding chapters.
First chapter.
A summary and collection of what is necessary for the treatment of fractures of bones, from which a preliminary understanding is required before we begin to recall the parts of broken limbs and their various types. Therefore, let us begin with the prologue of this chapter by summarizing the discourse; and the chapters will be such that at their beginning it is necessary for the practitioner to stand firm in the truth of them; so that you and those like you, who wish to know or learn this noble art from others, may do so promptly.
And so I say that when it happens to someone to have a fracture or dislocation or torsion or fall, at that moment it is necessary for you to decide at the beginning whether to perform venesection or to bandage them, or both simultaneously, unless some cause prohibits it, such as weakness of strength, or if it is a child who has happened to them, or an elderly person in debility, or if it is a time of extreme heat or cold.
And let them be content with food that is light, such as fresh herbs and the meat of birds and small animals; we prohibit fatty meats and foods that fill the veins with blood, until you are sure that there will be no abscess or pus formation, and if you do not fear an effusion of matter into the limb.
And now return the patient to their original regimen, which they were accustomed to before.
And so when the broken bone begins to be restored, now it is necessary that the patient be fed with nutritious foods that are moist and heavy, in which there is viscosity, such as food made from boiled wheat with water, rice, peas, beef tripe, hens, fresh pork, and similar foods, and with this regimen the healing of the fracture occurs more easily and better.
And know that the bones in children as well as in old age and in the first year of life cannot be set or consolidated by nature through the drying up of the members.
However, nature adjusts and applies them around the broken bone for all parts due to a cause similar to glue, in which there is thickness that adheres and strengthens until they are completely conjoined, until they come to an end of strength and firmness, just as fire at the beginning, without any cause prohibiting it from completing its work.
And for this reason it is necessary to avoid heavy foods and viscous foods that are thick, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the members, for the fracture of the bone of the arm is different from the fracture of the bone of the head; and the fracture of the bone of the foot is different from the fracture of the bone of the thigh; and similarly all the other members are different among themselves.
And you will find a description of every kind of fracture, explained in its own separate chapter.
There is diversity in the nature of fractures themselves. And the types of bone fractures are diverse in themselves; for one type of curved fracture is different from those that occur in the stars; and some fractures are made according to the length of the bone: and among the fractures of the shaft and splinters and non-continuous breaks, just as it is done when a pen is prepared for writing, there is a fracture due to plague and disruption in the head, and there is a small crack fracture.
And each type has its own proper treatment in the restoration, of which there will be a separate recall in their respective places.
There is a sign of a bone fracture. And by water it is known whether there is a fracture of the bone: its twisting, and that elevation and appearance which are felt by touch, and what happens when you press on it with your hand.
And therefore when there is no visible twisting or swelling, it must be done by touch: when you touch the bone with movement and the patient does not feel great pain, then there is no fracture here.
There might be twisting or a small fissure, therefore do not press that with your hand and by compression of the whole area; however, place something upon it from among the medicines which are mentioned shortly before the moment when you will treat the location; and nearby it should be narrow due to subtle constriction.
And you should know that the place where it is bent and not split into two or more of those that do not occur in its damage, as is described throughout this part of its comparison, then you should begin from your time to correct it, provided there is an abscess near it.
And if there is an abscess near it, then leave that for some days until the abscess subsides; and afterward bandage it in whatever way you can with lightness and care.
And you should know that the restoration and alignment of it are easier than for the bone in which such damage occurs.
And then strengthen that according to what will be recalled next.
But if there is swelling in the bone due to damage, then it is necessary to extend this broken limb from both parts, whether it is the hand or the foot; or by your hand if the member is small; or by two cords or one cord and your hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb in a location equal to its natural figure, while the parts so extended from the broken limb compel you to return those additions to their origin and according to each way that you can in their places.
And be careful according to your strength that no harm falls upon the patient because of your operation or injury; and compel yourself according to your ability to apply one bone to its companion bone according to their best forms.
And it is fitting that at that opportune moment you touch with your hand that which needs attention, and if you find any divergent cause, correct that and equalize it according to the measure of your virtue.
And avoid excessive extension and compression, just as some foolish surgeons often cause an abscess to occur due to their operation, or cause contraction in the limb as we have seen happen due to their careless operations.
And the quality of the constriction of the tranquilized limb is what I recommend; for you to know that broken limbs vary in their smallness and magnitude and in the form of each one.
And for those that are small like the arm and the hand and the finger and similar to these, then the wrapping should be of soft fine cloth.
And for those that are thick like the thigh or the calf, it is necessary that in these a wrapping of leather and something hard be used, because the leather wrapping connects the large and strong limb with tightness from all sides, so that nothing harmful can enter.
And immediately after you begin, after your evacuation of the wound, pause conveniently for this, on the place of that fracture with three wrappings or four as the limb requires; and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
And then bring it to the uppermost part of the place of the fracture, and tighten moderately so that your binding does not hurt the place where you have tightened the fracture. And then extend the wrapping of the place of the fracture a bit, and loosen the binding little by little, until you reach a large part of the area.
And then take another bandage and wrap that place of the fractured area again with a wrapping, and then go to the lower part of the wrappings from the fracture; and let your work be in tightening the binding and loosening it as we have said about the first wrapping above.
And then pause between the wrappings with a piece of cloth or linen on which the curvature of the fracture is equalized, if there is any curvature; and if not, do not pause for any reason.
And then pause on the other bandage, and then apply plasters properly made shortly thereafter if there is no inflammation or abscess in the area, and at that time pause using what causes the abscess to form and reduces the swelling; and leave this for several days; and then you tighten over it the plasters.
Let the splints for the middle of the reed shaft be well made skillfully; or let them be of oak wood; or of palm ribs; and let each splint be of this size and shape:
However, the splint that is placed over the fracture should be a little thicker and longer than the other splints, and the length of the splint according to how it fits the limb in size and smallness; then bind the splints together with a bandage as was described in your first dressing, and tie it above decently as we have said about dressing, and it should be that your dressing is tighter at the place of the fracture, and the farther it is from the fracture, the looser the binding.
And the threads should be of medium thickness and fineness, and they should be soft; if the threads are thick, as I have seen in some people's bandaging with cords made of twisted thread, it is a great mistake, for when you apply the dressing unevenly, it causes problems.
And similarly, very fine threads are not appropriate, for you will not achieve with the dressing what you want, and it should not be less than a finger's width between splint and splint.
And if the patient complains of pain at the ends of the splints near the dressing in those places, then place soft wool or knotted cloth underneath where it does not cause injury.
In some cases, a fracture is accompanied by bruising, and there may be disruption in the bone, and I will recall this in that chapter according to its particular nature.
And you should know that not every broken limb is dressed with splints on the first day; the reason for this is that when the limb is large, it should not be placed on splints until about five or seven days later, or more, according to your certainty of the occurrence of swelling.
And this is the method of the plasters with which the Ancients restored fractures, dislocations, and twists.
Method of a plaster made from bran for the restoration of fractures, suitable for complete bones and mostly for women and children, as it does not cause heat or irritation: take powdered bran, which is the fine flour that comes off the walls of the mill due to the movement of the millstones.
And prepare that as it is, but sieve it with egg white, and use the preparation neither too thick nor too thin.
Another method for a plaster that restores fractures, dislocations, and twists: recipe includes asafetida, lapidanum, acacia, hyssop, migraach (which is the seed of wild millet), each one drachm; myrrh and aloes each half a drachm; alochil two drachms. All should be powdered and sieved and mixed with almond water or egg white if the condition of the injury is hot; then use this plaster, as it has a good composition and restores broken bones quickly, suitable for men.
Another method for a plaster to treat fractures and twists: recipe includes migraach (seed of wild millet) and myrtle bark each one drachm; myrrh and aloes each half a drachm; acacia six drachms; Armenian bole two drachms.
All should be powdered in a mortar with a crushing tool, then sieved, and mixed with water or egg white and applied.
Method of applying a plaster to joints and bones that have come out of their places, and for pain resulting from these and for cracks and fractures: take clean wool and immerse it in vinegar and warm oil and place it over the site.
In this plaster, there is no restorative virtue, but it is useful for suppurating sores and properly expelling pains.
Another method of a plaster that restores a broken bone: take leaves of the mulberry tree and leaves of the wild poppy; mix them all and make a plaster from them.
Method of a quick plaster to be administered after the restoration of a bone fracture and when you want resolution of remaining abscess: take root of plantain, of chamomile and flowers of violets, of flour of spelt, of each one part; mix all ground and boiled in vinegar, if the member is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, now mix that with coriander water or with water and use it.
Another method of a stronger plaster for resolution than the first one, when there is a hard abscess after the restoration of the limb: take root of plantain and seeds of linseed, and buckthorn, melilot, marjoram, flowers of violets, chamomile, of each equal parts; all should be ground and boiled in sage water or in warm water or in vinegar, whichever suits the condition of the limb and the color of it.
But Hippocrates does not say in his book to apply it to the broken limb after its restoration except for wax made of wax and oil and nothing else; and recommends that it be midway between coarseness and fineness.
May is used from Gaulish wine which it is seen that they place over the broken limb after restoration due to the drying effect of its slight heat just as myrrh, and similar to those causes which we have recounted.
Method of making a plaster for injury and pain: recipe using egg white, linseed, human hair cut or bird feather, oak bark, and salt, all in equal parts; they are ground, sifted, mixed together, and then made into a plaster.
The amount according to which it is necessary that the bandage remains and should then be loosened is such that you consider whether there has been no accident of pain or swelling, nor is the bone broken or displaced; therefore, do not loosen it for several days. And if there occurs severe pain or swelling at the site, then you should be cautious or loosen that bandage immediately, and remove the plaster from it.
And take a piece of cloth and sea sponge soaked in warm water, and immerse it in cold water, with which you wash the affected area until the swelling subsides and the pain is alleviated; then let the limb rest for one hour; afterwards place on it pure wool submerged in vinegar and oil or scented oil, and bind it over it for a night until you are sure of no abscess formation, and that the swelling of the limb has subsided, and the pain from it is relieved.
And then iterate that gentle wrapping and small plaster without tightening it as much as your first constriction, and gently treat it until it is healed.
And if you find that the abscess and hardness and pain and swelling and all such occurrences have subsided completely, and you need a plaster and wrapping, then repeat that as you did at the beginning equally.
And if no cause occurs to the member of those we have mentioned, then you do not loosen it but after three or four or ten or seven days; and sometimes all this is left loose for twenty days according to what appears to you in the loosening of the member, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and it becomes accustomed to the coagulation of flesh over it.
And now adjust in the constriction more than in your first constriction entirely, and adjust according to the swelling of the patient's flesh, as was previously mentioned.
But if you see the location of the fracture already drying and wasting more than is appropriate, then you will know that nutrition is prohibited from reaching it; therefore, apply tepid water to it every time you loosen it; do this for all three days; ease the constriction a little, and through this operation direct nutrition to the member, and it will be healed quickly.
But what those foolish restorers of the fractured member do otherwise when it is not restored at the beginning as appropriate and is restored according to tortuous methods, is an error according to their operation, and a great fear, and if this were true, it would greatly enlarge what the Ancients wrote about it in their books and warned against.
And I have not seen any of them do this, so it is more reasonable to assume that they do not do this.
Chapter Second: On fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their figures are diverse, and the causes are distinct. Some fractures are caused by the impact of a hard object, which removes all the bone inward to the suture, leaving behind only that part of the bone, just as it would in wood; thus this type is called an incisional fracture. Or a hard object cuts some part of the bone and cuts through the flesh but does not penetrate beyond the surface; this type of fracture is called absolute elevation.
This is about the severity of these two fractures, either major or minor, and of a fracture that is comminuted or crushed; and the cause of it is percussion by something weak or falling on a stone, and similar circumstances.
And this fracture is penetrating, which approaches the periosteum below the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the severity of these two fractures is either major or minor.
And this is a fracture that affects the bone in its subtlety, in the small fibers, and for this reason this particular type of fracture is called capillary.
And this is a fracture that is caused either by falling or by being struck with a stone and similar circumstances, which causes some plate of the bone to enter into the soft parts inside, and it occurs in the concave location as well as when such things happen to the bones of the arm when they are struck and break inwardly.
And this is more commonly done in the bones of children's bodies, just as with the heads of infants.
And for all these types of fractures, treatments are used, which are remedies applied separately. And I will return to the recall of all this in its place.
And you can recognize all these types of fractures by detection on them and by inquiry into their symptoms and the appearance of damaged flesh that is on them.
And the capillary fracture is recognized by this: it appears on the bone, and when it is cleaned and examined with a probe, the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of fractures, consider at first the symptoms of the patient, and if you see from these symptoms that they signify by manifest indication fear, as is vomiting in anger, and stretching out, and loss of understanding, and hoarseness of voice, and fainting, and acute fever, and similar such symptoms of the intestines and hardness of them, and likewise with other major dispositions, without doubt it will not be healed.
And if you see unusual symptoms that give hope of health, then you begin the treatment of them.
The cause of which is clear if it comes to you as a complaint in the beginning as it is in winter days, then it is necessary that you study the condition of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all dispositions.
And if it is in the days of summer, then it is necessary that you wait to remove the bone before the seventh day, since what is around the bone may become corrupted by pus, and for the symptoms we have mentioned to occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the scalp covering the head and is in small pieces and scattered, then it is necessary that you incise the comminuted and scattered part as I will relate to you.
