And you should shave the head of the patient who is injured, and uncover the bone according to custom and method as best as possible according to the shape of the injury, so that it is clear on the patient.
And if it happens to you that the detection of the bone, bleeding, or an abscess requires caution, then you must be careful with what is necessary, and it is that you cover the area with cloths infused in wine and rose oil, and allow the wound to rest until the abscess subsides, and you are sure there is no further bleeding; then proceed with circumferential treatment of the bone and its removal.
In this way it should be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you cut the bone with an incisor of such and such a shape, and this is its form: And then apply another incisor to it, somewhat wider than that; and this is the form of it.
And then apply an even wider incisor than the second one.
And it is necessary to have various incisors, some wider than others and some shorter than the rest; and their tips should be sharp, and they should be made of iron or steel.
And use them with ease in percussion on the incisors so that you do not injure the head and cause pain to it.
And if the bone is strong and hard, it is necessary to drill around it before you apply the incisors with the chisels, which are called non-profound chisels.
And they are called this only because these do not penetrate to the end of the bone of the head as far as what is adjacent to it, so that the chisel has a rounded tip or margin on top of which is the part below the head like a margin or small circle, which prevents it from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take a great number of these chisels, each of which should be suitable for the sharpness of each bone, so that you are present with all the parts of the head where the tip is sharp, and the quantity in length and width should correspond to the sharpness of this part.
This is the form of three kinds of trephines: The quality of drilling in the circuit of the broken bone is that you place the trephine on the bone and rotate it with your hands until you know that the bone has been penetrated; and then move the trephine to another location, and continue this process throughout to the amount of the thickness of the rib or around there.
And then incise with the scalpel what is between each piece of bone, and make it as fine as possible at the end for lightness, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either by hand or for some other reason due to preparations made for this purpose, such as tools like probes and fine forceps.
And you must be careful that the scalpel does not touch any part of the brain.
And when you have circled the bone with perforations, it will be separated from the brain if it is continuous there, and you will extract it; you should smooth and equalize that in the roughness of what is in the bone using a tool similar to a scalpel, but it must be thinner and finer than other scalpels.
And if any small pieces of the bones remain and you are concerned about them, now take that with ease using the tools appropriate to you; and then treat the wound with lint and dressings as we have mentioned.
And the other method of operation is light, distant from fear, which Guy of Chauliac greatly praised; and this is the speech about it: It is necessary for you at the beginning to start uncovering the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe, until when you have uncovered that part and you have placed below it the extremity of this slow and cheerful scalpel, whose form is as follows:
And so the lens-shaped part is solid and does not split for any reason, and the acute part splits it into two sides along its length, as you see; that the lens-shaped part fits to the bone with the cutting surface being sharp against the bone.
And therefore strike with the saw on a small part to see if you can split all the bone with ease as it revolves, and be sure of the tissue that no accident happens in any way, and even if the physician were the most foolish of all people and the least knowledgeable among them; certainly, and even if he were asleep.
Now if any cause is attached to the bone of the tissue in some places of the bone, it will split that which is at the end of the saw that is lens-shaped, and deliver that which is with ease, for it is delivered without harm and without fear.
And if the bone fracture does not penetrate into the tissue, and what is split off or remains of the bone is its surface, and it remains rough with small irregularities, now you should file that roughness and eradicate those irregularities with fine razors for which you have prepared in various quantities so that you can apply them appropriately at any location during your operation on the broken bone according to its shape.
And you should apply in your proportion at which you file the bone at the beginning with the broader of those razors, and then use progressively finer ones, and do not stop doing this continuously until you reach applying the thinnest and finest of all.
In each of the other small, slender fractures and subtle fractures, you should provide care according to what is required for their correction.
And it is true that not all who have studied in that art are capable. And this is about what we have written concerning large fractures.
And if the fatty tissue is rectangular when you remove the bone, then take a piece of cloth according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in warm oil, and place it over the opening of the wound; then take another folded or triple-layered cloth and immerse it in wine and warm oil, and place it on top of the first cloth; and do this as lightly as possible so that you do not press on the swelling; and afterward apply a bandage to it, but tighten it only enough to cover the wound.
And keep it like this for one day or two until you are sure that no abscess will form; then loosen it and afterward administer medicines that are too drying, such as linseed poultice and flour of oats, and powder of vetches and of aristolochia, and similar ones.
And it is that you should make these medicines into a powder that is finely ground and apply them to the wound as dry powders; generally, administer any medicine whose property is to cleanse without irritating.
And be careful according to your virtue that the wound in your treatment is clean; and it should not become infected or worse; nor should you allow its virulence to increase. For when it is pressed against the fatty tissue of the brain, it corrupts that and weakens it, and thus a serious illness may occur due to this malicious condition.
And it affects the swelling of the head, as when the bone is cleaned and especially when pressure is applied to its treatment, and there is discoloration on its surface; and therefore consider whether it leads to the illness we have mentioned, knowing that it is harmful without any doubt.
And if the darkness does not come from the medicine that is placed upon it, but rather from the virtue of that medicine which causes the darkness, then you should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, and with these anoint a cloth, and then place that over the wound; and thereafter treat it with the spices suitable for healing until it is healed.
Chapter III.
On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken in more than two parts at once above, or one of those rarely, because there are two bones there, since the part below is cartilaginous and does not break, but only ulceration, distortion, and swelling occurs to it.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then you should introduce your finger into the socket of the nose, and align that fracture within with your middle finger, and press from outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this with ease, and be careful that your operation does not cause pain to the patient.
And if the fracture is in the uppermost part of the nose, and your finger cannot reach it, then it should be aligned at the end of a rod with some thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you should proceed as described.
And attempt the restoration of it on the first day of the fracture if possible; and if not, then do so around the seventh or tenth day when the swelling has subsided.
Insert a piece of linen cloth into the end of the nose if the fracture is in one part, or two pieces if the fracture is in two parts, and let each piece be large enough to fill the cavity of the nose.
One of the ancient restorers said that the pieces should be dipped in butter and changed every day, but I have not seen this done; however, it is advisable to dip the cloth in albumen from a cooked egg mixed with powder of flowers of malt; and then place it inside when the bone has set and the cartilage has hardened.
In some cases, a piece of ox horn wrapped in linen cloth may be inserted into the nose instead of pieces of cloth, especially if the fracture of the nose is very severe and the patient is not prohibited from inspiration.
And this method is not necessary for you; if you want to do it, do so, and if you prefer, use pieces of cloth.
And if another piece becomes inflamed, apply a plaster to the nose with cerate or cotton dipped in vinegar and rose oil, or with honey water.
But if it does not become inflamed, now you should apply a plaster outside with sesame flour and powder of flowers mixed with egg white; and then place it over a linen pad, and do not treat the nose with any other substance.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or comminuted, now you should dissect around them and extract them with instruments suitable for that; and then close the wound, and treat it with substances that promote healing and consolidation, and with appropriate plasters.
And if it happens that there is an abscess inside the nose, now you must treat it with poultices and administer lead tubes within so that it may be completely healed.
Chapter IV.
On the restoration of the jaw when it is dislocated.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture does not have an abscess, now observe if the fracture is only on the surface and not split into two parts and curved inward, then its diagnosis is easy.
And you should therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, insert your left thumb into the mouth of the patient; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert your right thumb; and press against the swelling of the fracture inward with ease at the outer part with your other hand; thus gently restore it.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two parts, now you should administer extension of both parts according to straightness as much as possible until they are aligned.
And if there is concussion of the teeth with disruption, now strengthen those that you expect will break with gold or silver or thread; and immediately apply cerate on the fractured jaw, and then apply a double cloth, and place a properly made bandage or a piece of sole leather equal to the length of the jaw; and then bind it above as is appropriate for your binding method and convenient application, so that it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and give him food that is easy to swallow.
And if you think that the shape has already been altered in any way, then at that time you should cut it and loosen that on the third day; and immediately correct what has already been altered by it and plaster that with powder of myrtle, egg white, or flour of cumin, once you have laid the cerate on it, and place over the plaster lion's wool.
And then as you apply and adjust that plaster over it, and the position of the bone has not been altered, leave it, do not loosen it until it is healed and the fracture is strengthened; for usually this fracture is cured in three weeks.
And if on another occasion an abscess occurs, then administer what we have mentioned often for its relief until it is healed, and let that abscess be drained.
And if the fracture is with a splinter, then consider whether it is a piece of bone separated or embedded; then you should carefully extract those splinters with what is appropriate for that in the removal of such instruments.
And if the opening of the splinter is narrow, then enlarge it with a scalpel according to the amount needed.
And then when you have placed those splinters, and none of them will be drained, close the opening of the splinter if it is wide; and if not, place over it one of the plasters that are appropriate for this purpose until it heals.
Chapter 5.
On the restoration of the collarbone when it is broken.
Most often, the clavicle is broken in front of the muscle, and that fracture is made in one of three ways.
Or it is broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without affecting the function; and it is easy to restore it. Or it happens that the fracture affects the function, and its restoration is difficult; or it is such that the fracture involves a condition.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture involves a condition, is such that you make two assistants present, so that one holds the patient's arm and supports the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck towards that part.
And then adjust the fracture with the fingers so that its shape is made according to what is necessary, and it should not have a slight prominence or concavity.
And if you need more extension, then it is necessary to place under the armpit of the injured side a pad made of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to the quantity needed; extend and elevate the clavicle and compress the pad with your hand so that you can adjust the fracture as required.
And if you cannot pull the end of the clavicle to the outer parts, so that it reaches the preon, then the patient should be reclined on their neck, and place under the muscle a pad about half as large.
And the assistant presses the muscle below so that the bone of the clavicle is elevated to reach the preon, and then you correct the fracture and adjust it with your fingers.
If you feel pain from the furcula and its joints, now is the time to cut into it and extract those irritations easily.
And if it is retained in the bone, now use your ingenuity to make an incision with one of the scalpels which you have prepared for this purpose, since you paused below the furcula with the instrument that serves as the ciphac; and this instrument is made of wood or iron, and its shape is as follows:
And it is similar to the hollow part of a bowl, whose shape it resembles; and its width should be according to what you need, depending on the size of the bone or its scarcity.
But the length of it should be according to what is possible for the operation; let there be two ends as you see fit, and one end wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound you have incised when extracting the irritation from the bone is wide, and you are sure it will form a boil or cauterization, now you should approximate the two lips of the wound with stitches.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear it will form a boil or cauterization, now you should treat the wound with plasters and powders according to the extent of the wound's incision.
And if an abscess or cauterization occurs, now apply a plaster made of vinegar and rose oil mixed with vinegar, and place it over that area.
And do not let the splint be loose over the broken bone; and therefore see the patient every day, so when the bandage is loosened or you see the splint already moved, then you must correct this and tighten the bandaging.
And place the patient's pillow on his neck, and below him a cushion to raise his sleep position slightly during the night; therefore, in this way, lift the support of his chest, and thus elevate the fracture of the collarbone by raising the muscles; or bind his arm to his neck.
And do not loosen the bandage unless you find an abscess in the area immediately or within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you see his face turned to it, and adjust around the constriction and restore it until it is set and the collarbone fracture is healed.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the measure in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some people it is done in less time.
Chapter VI.
On the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The bone is broken in the middle and not at the edges but along its sides.
And when it is broken in that place — and it is broken in the middle of it — and it is known only by touch, then according to the shape of the fracture prepare him and reduce it according to its natural figure in every way that is possible for you.
And then pause over the place where you sprinkle powdered egg white and apply a lion's head bandage; and then pause with double-layered cloth powder; and then pause over the wound table, finely sprinkled according to the quantity of the spoonful, either all or at least a little.
But if you leave the wound table slightly concave in some places of the spoonful, then you cover that concavity with a lion's head bandage, and then press the wound table evenly.
And then apply a linen strip above with moderate tension and secure the wound table so that it does not move from its place.
And consider the bandaging each day; and every time it is loosened, reapply it and tighten it evenly if the wound has moved from its place.
And place the dressing on its usual side.
And the wound will be healed in xx days or in .xx5.
And when this number is completed, then you dissolve the bandage, and you know for certain that it concerns the bones of which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the place of the bone piece and place the chain below it, then you cut above it, remove it, and do as we have said in the fracture of the clavicle, should suppuration or cauterization occur here.
Chapter VII.
On the restoration of foot fractures.
The foot sometimes is broken in the middle and remains thus; but the extremities move as cicatrization and comminution occur.
And of the accidents when the middle of it is broken, it inclines towards the internal parts and there occurs great pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, weakness, vomiting blood; and a cavity is formed in the broken bone and it is not hidden from sensation.
And the restoration of it is that the patient lies supine on their back, and a cushion is placed between their shoulders; then gently press the muscles, and adjust the ribs by two or more ribs, and delicately perform the operation and the reduction in every possible manner suitable for this, until the figure of the bone returns as required.
And then place over it plaster and cotton, and place beneath thin splints of wood or reeds or similar light materials, after which you wrap that in cloth; and then gently bind that above the broken bone without pressing too hard; and go around the binding several times along the back, and tighten that with moderate firmness; and then examine the bandage at all times, and when it has softened, strengthen it.
And if necessity compels the removal of that which has been corroded or protruded or caused pain or an abscess in the area, then you should cut it out and remove the plaster and correct what has occurred by this method of treatment; and immediately replace the plaster, if you apply it to that part; and adjust the bandaging until it is healed.
Chapter VIII.
On the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in thick parts where they join; and at their extremities, near the front part, compression does not affect them because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from the sense during the examination of the aforementioned ribs.
And the restoration should be such that it equalizes the fracture of the aforementioned ribs according to the possible method, until it is set as necessary, and then apply plaster and bind the broken bone with a splint if needed.
And if the fracture of the ribs inclines towards the inner parts, then severe pain and piercing occur, as with the pain that happens when one weeps, and because the bone pushes against the membrane; and difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting of blood result; and this is difficult to treat.
And the Ancients were skilled in treating that with powerful remedies.
And what they say is: it is necessary to pause from foods that cause inflammation and gas, so that the abdomen becomes swollen and extended, and the fracture is pushed outward.
Let us proceed with what will not be a cause of easy generation of an inflamed abscess if it does not occur; and if it has already occurred, now help is given for that.
And some of them say that it should be placed above the wound and close to where the strength is drawn; and this is similar to reasoning, but it is feared that the wound will draw the superfluities to the place due to its weakness.
And some of them say: cover the place with wool that has already been submerged in hot oil, and place poultices between the ribs until it is full, making the bandage fit according to the roundness; and treat the ailment near where the lung disease from food and medicine is cured.
And if the cause of the ailment is forcefully constraining it so that it cannot be tolerated, and if the bone is pushed against the membrane with a puncture point, we fear for the ailment; now it should be that we cut above the location, expose the broken rib, and then place over it an instrument that secures the healing process, the memory of which precedes, and that they cut the bone with ease, and extract it.
And then we adjust the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with stitching, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs internally, now you should treat it and apply poultices in rose oil to the area, and treat the ailment with what is appropriate for an internal abscess.
And let him lie supine on the side on which he sleeps until he is healed.
Chapter IX.
On the restoration of fractures of the vertebrae and the collarbone.
In some cases, there is a fracture in the bones of the collar, and treatment for them is similarly applicable to the vertebrae of the back. And when it happens to someone and you want to know if they will recover or not, at this time consider, for if you see their limbs already softened, stunned, and dead, unable to move them or extend them, and when you press on those areas with your fingers or pierce them with a needle and there is no pain felt in those areas, then you know that recovery is not possible, and in such conditions it is fatal.
And if you move those areas and feel constriction in the said places when pierced with a needle, then you know that the vertebra is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see the vertebrae of the neck showing similar symptoms and want to know if they will recover or not, then at this time consider the patient's condition.
If you see that the limbs are already softened and observe similar symptoms as those affecting the arms, and when lying on their back, they experience vomiting and involuntary diarrhea, and sometimes they lie on their back and want to urinate but cannot, then you know it is fatal, and at this point, there is no need to work on treating them.
But if none of those symptoms occur, then the cause is better and farther from being fatal.
And the treatment for them is to apply poultices to reduce the abscess until you place warm olive oil or boiled horse chestnuts on the affected vertebra; apply this repeatedly over several days until the abscess is reduced.
During the hours of pause, apply one of the soothing and restorative plasters, and bind it over that limb; and command the patient to use repose and tranquility and not to sleep on the side where there is pain until it is healed.
And if it happens that the callus of the bone has detached or for some cause it is separated from it, then proceed to scrape it over the wound and extract that piece of bone; and immediately bring together the two lips of the wound, with sutures if it is large; and afterwards treat it with one of the preparations that promote flesh growth until it is healed.
But if the end of the bone is fractured and it is the tip of the finger, then proceed to insert your thumb of the left hand into the cavity, and align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible by second-degree reduction; and afterwards apply a plaster and splint, if you require those, and bind it.
And if you feel detached fragments in it, then proceed to handle that area, remove those fragments, and treat the wound with the treatment that is prescribed until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
On the Restoration of the Wound.
The bones of the arm are often fractured, and when they are broken, then their fracture is not simply a communication at the ends but are split along their length and deviate to the sides; and it happens to the patient that there is pain in the location and puncture, and movement is restricted when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand in such a way as to bring it into alignment with the figure of the fracture until it is set.
And if the fracture is at the extremity of the limb, then adjust it as suits you by alignment so that it resembles its natural shape.
And if the fracture is in the long bone that declines toward the lower parts, then have the patient lie on their belly so that you can prepare the restoration of that fracture. And when you have aligned it, apply a plaster over it, and then place a splint of wood or cane next to it, and bind it tightly with bandages in such a way that you do not fear any piercing of the fracture or displacement of the splint.
And align the concavity so that it fills appropriately, ensuring that it takes the alignment evenly, and instruct the patient to sleep on their back or on their side.
And if an acute abscess occurs, then stop the extension of the limb and its restoration until the abscess is brought under control, and apply what is necessary to treat it as previously described; then return to the restoration of the limb, and bind it as appropriate.
And if the bones are displaced or fragmented, then you must treat them.
Chapter XI.
On the restoration of the fracture of the auxiliary bone.
The auxiliary is what is between the shoulder blade and the top of the shoulder.
And if it is fractured, then the restoration of it is in one of two ways, one of which involves taking a flat piece of wood of moderate thickness in this form:
Let there be two ends of bandages, and let it be suspended from a high place.
Let the patient lie on a bench; then place the broken arm outside onto the stick until it reaches the midpoint where the curvature of the stick is attached.
Then suspend something heavy from that, so that the patient extends it to the lower parts; and then the physician should place the fracture along with its parts above, until the fracture returns as needed.
And another method is that the patient lies on their back with their neck, and suspend the patient's hand from the neck with bandages; then command two assistants, one to hold what is above the fracture along with its parts, and the other to hold below; and each of them should pull in their respective directions.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then wrap bandages over the fracture on one side and over it on another side, and each of the two assistants should pull in their respective directions.
And if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, then it is necessary to place half of the bandage over the knot and the other part below the fracture towards the joint.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the joint, then it is necessary to place the bandage over that location and around that joint.
And do the same with ease and skill, and when it is adjusted as needed and properly applied, then wrap it tightly without causing an abscess or injury.
If an abscess occurs and is cauterized, within seven days there will be no stricture, so pause on the linen soaked in vinegar for six times and rose oil so that the abscess can rest, then proceed with strength.
And the method of strengthening that is to apply a plaster over the fracture, then wrap it with a wet cloth over the plaster; then adjust the arm on that support, and place its hand upon its own muscle.
And place the cloth and wrap it tightly around the support for the arm so that the arm remains in a fixed position, unless some cause of the broken bone prohibits this.
And now use splints.
And it is that you place on the fracture the splint that is widest and strongest of the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the break plus an addition of three fingers on all sides.
And then wrap around the splints with the bandage as I have described at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandaging over the site of the fracture be tighter there. And when the fracture is more elongated, the bandaging should be lighter.
And thus, when you arrange the splints and bandaging as we have said during the time you restore the limb to its proper position, then proceed with it.
And if you fear an abscess may occur, then leave the bandaging and splints in place for seven days as we have said.
And then consider the bandaging on all sides for three days so that it does not happen in the place properly or inflammation; or it is prohibited food from reaching the member by disposition of excess or constriction; and thus correct everything as we have said.
But if you are confident that no cause of this will happen, then do not loosen the bandage until after three days or more.
And let the patient lie on their back, and place pillows under their head so that it is raised above the stomach.
And place a pad filled with wool evenly over it, and at all times of the night or day ensure that the figure of the broken limb is not loose or dissolved, or that the bandaging is too slack; thus correct this all according to your skill.
And provide food for the patient according to the order we mentioned before, such that the food is light at first until healing begins; and then gradually increase the quantity of food.
For it is customary for the bandage and dressing to be tight for forty days; and then they should be loosened, a bath should be given, and treatment should be administered with ointments that correct this.
And if the fracture is still rough and irritated, then do not loosen the bandaging and leave the splints on for one day or even up to two months.
Chapter XII.
On the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the ulna and radius; one of which is small and is the bone that lies under the little finger, and the other is larger and is placed below the smaller one, towards the thumb side.
If only the larger focus or the smaller one is broken, and if they are broken together.
And when the smaller focus above is broken, its restoration is easy, and its healing is quicker.
And when the focus below is broken, the fracture is severe, and its healing is difficult.
And worse still is when two bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller focus above, it is appropriate for the physician, after its restoration, to allow a short period of rest and ease until it is healed.
But if the larger focus is what is broken, then a more extended period of rest is necessary.
And if both focuses are broken together, then it is necessary to allow the most rigorous period of rest.
And it is necessary to place the hand in a cast after its restoration and extension laid flat on a pillow, with the wrist above all the fingers, so that the thumb is lower than the other fingers, and the patient lies on a sloping bed.
And there should be a pillow in front of it at the height or level of it, so that the patient does not have to work to reach it.
And one attendant should extend the arm from below toward the hand or with a cloth, and another attendant should extend it from above similarly.
And the physician should set the bone so that it heals in the best position possible.
And if in the setting of the bone you stagger, then at that moment reduce all parts to their place with your skill.
And if it appears in the injury that the bone is disjointed and it is not within your hope for its restoration, then at that moment act upon it and set it as we have described above.
But if the fracture is with an injury, I have already treated this in a separate chapter, so follow the treatment described there.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then you should line a cloth with cerot made from olive oil and white wax, which is midway between thickness and subtlety, and place a bandage over it with gentle pressure so that the abscess yields; and then remove the cerot and apply a plaster prepared from powder mixed with egg whites; and afterward place strips; let the strip that is placed over that fracture be further away and tighter.
And know that the number of strips for the arm depends on the major disposition, or rather, whether the fracture is in one bone or in two bones together.
And then place your bandage over the location of the fracture more firmly and vigorously, and when it is necessary to have less tension above the fracture or below it, apply the bandage a bit looser as was mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that you wrap around the fracture should be soft and moist, not hard or rough, and the bandage with which it is wrapped should be of a medium thickness between fine and coarse, as we have recommended.
And consider the limb and the bandaging for some days. And if any cause arises for which adjustment is necessary, just as promptly the injury to the limb occurs, you should immerse the limb in hot water until that pain subsides.
And do not constrain the limb until it rests, and then return to the bandaging.
And if the bandaging has already been loosened and the bone is already set and appears to be in a similar condition, then adjust everything with your skill.
And consider if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive bandaging, for then you should loosen it slightly and leave it for some days until nourishment reaches it, and then strengthen it.
But if the patient does not suffer from any of the causes we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to loosen them for about twenty days or thereabouts.
And then the patient's arm should be suspended by their skill in making agitated movements, and let them sleep on their back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days or thirty-two days, or sometimes it is restored in twenty-eight days, and all of this depends on the condition of the tissues and the skill of the practitioner.
Chapter XIII.
On the restoration of a fracture of the wrist and the bones involved.
Know that a fracture occurs at the wrist and the bones involved, and if it happens to them, they usually require multiple treatments.
When it happens that the bone is fractured or dislocated, at that moment it is necessary for the patient to lie flat and facing upwards according to equality, and place the arm on top of it extended.
And then the minister should extend the broken bones and the physician should adjust them until they are properly set.
And now it is necessary to apply a plaster and lint, unless an abscess occurs which requires cauterization; and then place a bandage over it according to the size of the area, and you should have wrapped that in cloth.
But if the fracture is in the parts inside the palm of the hand, and at that moment make it appear like a sphere of cloth, and command the patient to hold it with their fractured palm; then secure it with cloth.
And let the bandage be applied with cloth in such a way that it is raised, so that the palm can apply to the hand; and secure it as needed.
But if the fracture is in the parts outside, then it is necessary to place one bandage above and another below on the palm, so that the hand is covered and straight; and then secure it, as when the hand is rotated at the wrist with the fingers wrapped.
And if there is a fracture in one of the bones of the fingers, then if it is a thumb, adjust it as needed and then secure it to the palm; if you wish, place a small straight splint so that the fracture is elevated and not worsened.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, index finger, ring finger, or little finger, then it should be adjusted and wrapped to that specific finger; or all should be wrapped continuously, which is better; or place a small splint over it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and approach that, the disposition of abscesses cut and opposed — both the warm operation — both with what is needed when some cause of those occurs, as it is repeated the narration of them.
Chapter XIV.
On the restoration of a fracture of the collarbone.
The collarbone, in many cases, is broken and is felt according to how it has shifted forward and upward.
And the restoration of it is done in such a way that one bandage is stretched over the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, and the patient lies face down on their belly.
And the practitioner should extend all the bandages to their proper position according to the quality when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it is closer to the shoulder joint, then the bandage should be extended toward the armpit at that point, so that the extension stops there, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is closer to the armpit, then the bandage should be extended toward the armpit, and the extension should be towards the points below.
And the physician should align the bone on both sides so that it is reduced according to its natural appearance and the bone is joined by proper conjunction; then it is necessary to apply plaster and bandaging, if an abscess does not occur in the limb. But if an abscess does occur, then you should leave it for several days until the abscess has subsided, and then return to your treatment.
The bandaging of the splint should be done as follows: wrap around the fracture a hard bandage, and fold it two or three times, and trim off any excess; then wrap the joint so that it reaches to just above the wrist and down to the base of the thigh.
And insert the bandage between the limb and the splint near the bottom of the joint, so that the ends of the bandage extend beyond both sides, and then roll over the splint and the limb any excess from the bandage.
And then place over the limb at the location of the fracture splints, and lay one strip on top of the bone of the splint.
And then fill the spaces between the splint and the limb with soft cloths, so that the bandaging is snug.
And then begin the firm bandaging starting from the middle at the location of the fracture with three or four wraps, and as you move away from the site of the fracture with the bandage, your wrapping should become looser, lighter, and softer.
And then extend both ends of the bandage downward to the ankle joint, and secure both ends of the strips from the other side so that the bandaging is not loose from its position.
And then leave the bandaging in place as it is, so that no corrosion, abscess, swelling, or similar conditions occur to the limb; and if any such cause does arise, then cut it away from the binding immediately, and correct what has occurred right away, as we have said previously.
If there is in the bone a splinter of bone which must be removed, then you should cut around it as much as possible; and if not, place something over it and massage that area, and treat with whatever remedy preceded the memory until it is healed.
And in some cases, this fracture of the skull is strengthened by these pieces being fixed to it with bandages, as we have said about the assistant and the arm.
However, in the restoration of the limb that has been treated in this manner, a bad limp does not occur; but if it is restored alone, or without the other side being similarly applied, then it is necessary for the person to have a limp permanently.
And know that the skull is strengthened in forty days or a few more days, and all of this depends on the diversity of constitutions and other circumstances.
Chapter XV.
On the restoration of the fracture of the kneecap.
Know that the fracture of the kneecap often occurs in this manner, but it also happens frequently due to trauma.
However, if it is a fracture, then it is merely a crack or bruise in its parts, and this is due to injury or several injuries, and all of this is based on the sensation experienced.
And the restoration of it is such that what is broken in its parts should be cut away, and then reunited with the said parts until they are joined together as much as possible by equalization, facilitation, and fabrication.
Apply the plaster and place it on that round bandage if you need it; and apply something above it tightly as is appropriate.
And consider all the positions that we have recommended for other fractures, as well as abscesses and similar conditions, so that whatever opposes any incident is appropriate to it until it is healed.
Chapter XVI.
On the restoration of a jaw fracture.
Know that in the jaw there are two bones, one of which is thick and is called the jawbone; the other is slender and is called the finlike bone.
And some types of fractures that occur in the jaw are similar to those that happen in the two bones of the arm, and thus its restoration is done as with the arm, and the operation for it is the same.
And if two bones are broken and come together incorrectly, the jaw is distorted in all directions.
And if the finer bone is broken, the jaw is displaced forward.
And if the thicker bone is broken, it will be below, and such cases are beyond your control.
And then administer extension, alignment, and fixation of the fragments accordingly.
However, be careful if the jaw fracture is jagged with sharp points, as the extension should be gentle and done with great caution.
And when there is any cause for an operation on the arm, and you have straightened out the bones and completed all the necessary work, at that time take pieces of wood from pine trees which are prepared from materials stored in the ground, and make them similarly from palm branches; select those with slight grooves, not too wide, and let their length be according to the length of the arm, and place one end into the skin below.
And then wrap around each piece of cloth in two coverings according to its length, and place one at the beginning of the arm on one side and the other on the opposite side, with one end inserted into the skin near the wrist.
And then bind the two bones in three places at both ends and in the middle, because with this structure the arm is prevented from tilting to either side and thus remains in proper position.
And now apply a wooden brace according to the length of the arm; and place it in such a way that the arm can maintain its necessary movements.
And especially do this in the case of a fracture, and when it is properly a bruise as well.
And then consider the arm each day and study it carefully as per previous studies, and you should guard it from abscesses, inflammation, or other similar causes; and when any such cause occurs, at that time take action on it so that it may be healed.
Chapter XVII.
Of the fracture of the heel and of the bones of the foot and of the ones mentioned.
To the scalp does not happen total fracture; but to the bones of the foot sometimes happens partial fracture; and to the said bones of the foot rarely happens fracture, and to them only happens healing in months of disposition.
And if to the bones of the foot happens fracture and times those bones be raised above themselves between them, then the patient places his foot on the ground prepared thus as mentioned.
And then places one foot on what is raised of those bones, and then steps on them, and presses, and binds them until they return to their proper places.
And then applies a plaster and stops above it, and below the arch of the foot places a small board with two flat ends.
And then strengthens that with the foot in a proper position, since you have wrapped it with cloths and other materials as needed.
And as three or four days pass, then you loosen the bandage, for you will find the bones aligned, or broken or dislocated, and the procedure with them is as you know.
But if any of them are broken, then restore those, and align them as I have recommended to you in the restoration of the bones of the hand. And then place on the broken bone a splint according to the length of the finger, and make it slightly longer; and below the foot place the board as recommended to you, and strengthen that with proper positioning.
But if what is broken of the bones are two or three or more, then you place on each finger a splint of its quantity wrapped in soft cloth, and strengthen the board at the bottom of the foot, and make it stand on one end of the board below the arch of the foot so that it remains well secured.
It is fitting for you not to forget that you should not disregard all the causes which are mentioned to you in the other fractures of the accidents we have spoken about; and prepare yourself for every circumstance with this one as well.
Chapter XVIII.
On the fracture of the coccyx in women and of the bone of the penis and of the shaft of the penis in men.
When the coccyx of a woman is fractured, at that moment make that incision in a reclined position, and shortly after incline her to one side slightly, and let her be held from behind.
And shortly after, the midwife fills the coccyx with cotton, and it should be done in the cavity as if it were intact; and then the woman should be bandaged, and her back should be raised little by little, for all of this is to ensure that before long she will be in front with the fractured part of the coccyx as if it were intact and the broken bone returns.
And then place poultices on her back, and when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton to facilitate it, and after she has urinated, reduce and return that part according to the method by which you managed it at the beginning, and then return it to its original poultice.
And do this for seven days or around that time, for it is then restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and stitch to its mouth a tube of cane; and insert the entire bladder into the cavity, and then blow through the tube until the bladder is inflated inside the cavity, for this will cause the fracture to return.
Let it be filled with cotton and placed for some days as we have recommended, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the thumb of a man or woman is broken, administer at its restoration and alignment what we have recommended for the hip bone.
And what is right is not hidden in these nearby fractures that rarely occur, especially for one who studies and uses this my book; for such a fracture signifies to you in the major positions through straightness in its restoration and alignment, and thus you understand well.
But in the penis when it is broken, take a splint of wood, and place the penis on it; then bind it with cloth wrapping, and let it be bandaged, and afterwards left for three days or about that time, until it heals.
Chapter XIX.
On the restoration of bone fractures when there is inflammation.
It is convenient for one who suffers a fracture with inflammation, and especially if the bone is large like the femur or the humerus, or similar to these, that you treat them and perform bloodletting as soon as the conditions for bloodletting indicate it to you, as we have said above.
And if the inflammation causes bleeding, then it is appropriate for you to treat it by restraining it in such a way that you powder on it finely sieved turmeric, unless another cause is present.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not delay this, unless an abscess occurs.
If an abscess is cauterized, do not resume the restoration of the inner part until the ninth day, after which the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third and fourth days either, for you may cause adverse effects.
If the broken bone protrudes above the skin, then you must act to reduce and align it with ease and in a short distance.
If the extension is not within your reach and the alignment cannot be done with your hands alone, then reduce it using this tool; this tool is made of iron, its length being seven or eight digits, and its width according to the size of the wound. Therefore, it is necessary for the surgeon to have three or four such tools in various sizes suitable for treating all kinds of fractures.
And let it be round, and let it be in slight curvature so that it does not bend during compression upon it at the time of the operation.
And let it have sharp ends with margins at its extremities; and let the upper part be thick, while the middle part be narrower.
And this is the form of it:
And it is called in Greek "λάβρα βουληντζ ἀτελέρμ", which means a slightly curved palette.
So that the extremity of it can be placed steadily with the affected part elevated above the protruding bone.
And apply it in one motion until the bone returns to its place, and is aligned with some degree of accuracy.
Therefore, try to align the ends of the broken bone between them, and if the end of the broken part is delicate and does not accept that tool as a good option, then you must insert the end of that bone into where the instrument has power over it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone entirely as we have recommended, then insert that part with one of the chisels which we mentioned; or saw that part with one of the saws in whatever manner is possible for you.
And smooth that which remains rough on the bone and small scratches.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient still feels intense pain, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and at that point do what will greatly help the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is complete, then soak a cloth in black styptic wine, and especially if it is summer; do not place cerot or any other substance with oil on the wound, so that it does not cause decay and corruption.
And then administer poultices at the appropriate time for the completion of the bone restoration, and leave the wound uncovered so that you can insert a probe with forceps and use dressings according to the size of the wound.
And be careful at all times not to touch the wound with your hands during the fracture treatment, for many times inexperienced doctors have done this, causing death or chronic illness or severe harm to their patients. And if the swelling is soft, loose, different from the swelling of other fractures.
And if the swelling is large or is a bad swelling, and you fear some of the maladies which we have recounted, and you feel pain in that place, then at this hour it does not suit to pause on those splints, but make an enveloping bandage of hard cloths instead of splints and lay them there.
And when after one day or two days the swelling begins to subside, indicating that the heat has been generated in it, then you remove the cloth which you had placed over it, and soon apply poultices, among which are those according to our custom used for treating swellings, such as tetrafarmacon and similar remedies.
And it is convenient for you to loosen the bandage and examine the swelling each morning and evening until it is consolidated and firm, and it should be such that the suppuration can run from it to the lower parts with ease.
If several days have passed and the swelling has not consolidated nor has the heat subsided from it, then you should know that here you are struggling with small issues for which you need to examine the swelling with a probe. And of those issues that are separate and removable, remove and extract them; but for those that cannot be separated and cause pain when the limb is moved, proceed to incise them as much as possible according to what is feasible for you.
And if the swelling becomes inflamed or infected, which is a sign of pus formation or some other form of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary to proceed with one of those treatments that are appropriate for the cure, as described in the preceding narrative of the relevant chapter.
And when the bone is cut, it protrudes above the limb like the bones of the chest and shoulder and similar to those of large limbs, and do not assume traction on it, nor its extraction for four movements (or times), as death often occurs because of this; and then allow that one inside which can heal, for it will easily fall out in about twenty or thirty days; and meanwhile treat the wound if it is visible, and if not, leave it alone.
Chapter XX.
On the treatment of stiffness that occurs as a consequence of some fracture.
This stiffness often occurs as a consequence of the healing of a fracture, especially near the joints, and it disfigures the shape of the limb; and quite easily prevents the limb from performing its natural function.
Then consider whether the stiffness is recent and administer medicines that have a binding effect, such as aloes, henna, myrrh, saffron, colocynth, and similar ones; take some or all of these, and combine them with either vinegar or egg white or plain water, and apply this mixture over the stiffness using a bandage and bind it tightly.
And leave the binding in place without loosening it for several days; then loosen it and apply another one until the stiffness is alleviated; or place a sheet of lead gently on it, as lead has the property of reducing anything that is swollen in limbs.
But if the stiffness has already turned to bone and is tight, and the necessity requires that it be removed immediately, then cut it at its weakest point, and excise the excess or reduce it with some instruments until it is alleviated; and treat it until it heals.
Chapter XXI.
On the restoration of fractures when they are restored and adjusted according to the natural position of the limb, gently and carefully.
When a fracture is restored and the limb is gently and carefully adjusted, it happens for several reasons: one of which is too much loosening or improper bandaging of the limb; or due to excessive tightness in the bandages preventing nourishment from reaching the limb; or due to an excess of poultices; or due to unnecessary movement at the wrong time; or due to a lack of blood in the patient's body and weakness.
The treatment for this is to feed the patient and make their body soft so that blood is multiplied in them; administer baths; and apply poultices and lettuce on them.
And placing plasters on the limb is appropriate, as the plaster draws nourishment to it, or continuing the application of warm water poultices, so that nourishment flows and returns to its natural form.
Chapter XXII.
On the treatment of broken bones when they are restored incorrectly and prevent the necessary operation from being performed on them.
When it happens that a limb, already restored near its healing, shows deformity, swelling, and knots in the broken bone, and thus appears to be recently fractured due to this condition, although the limb is not prevented from performing its natural function, you should not accept the opinion of those who believe the limb has been newly broken.
Let us know these states and conditions which the doctors and restorers have performed here; and this operation is highly reprehensible leading to great fear, the sign of which is fatal.
If lameness and limping are recent, it is now appropriate to apply embrocations with tepid water in which soft herbs are soaked, such as althea roots and linseed, and melilot; and let a poultice of softening be made properly with dough or olive oil, and it should be applied with that; or let mucilage from linseed root be taken and mixed with egg whites or olive oil, and apply with that; or take fat figs and grind them with dove's milk, and similarly for the medicines called consolidatives.
And in some cases it is dissolved through frequent light friction, which should be applied to the most humid areas, and movement of the limb should be administered to all parts at all times.
But if the tortuosity has already advanced and is firmly set and has become hardened into bone and necessitates treatment with iron, now it should be that the pus above is evacuated and the continuation of the bone be dissolved, and it should be cut away those superfluous parts of the knotting of that tissue with the bone with subtle cutting instruments, and ease in that should be administered through diligence and study.
Chapter XXIII.
On Dislocation
Dislocation is the wrongful displacement of one of the joints from its place, by which it is prohibited from movement, and the figure of the limb is distorted, causing severe pain and great injury.
Therefore, when an accident of a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to reduce it immediately so that it is set, and do not delay in reducing it, for if it is delayed, the area becomes inflamed and it then becomes difficult to perform the reduction of the dislocation.
And for this reason, it is not appropriate to move or stand during the time of inflammation, as doing so often causes spasms and pain in the teeth.
But when this happens, it is necessary for you to proceed with the incision of the abscess, and then allow it to rest until the inflammation subsides; afterwards, the limb should be bathed in hot water and oil, and then reduction can be performed with ease.
And the entire limb should be treated with whatever remedy will come to mind for its care.
And thus I have organized the chapters on dislocation as they were preceded in fractures; starting from the upper part of the body to the lower part.
Chapter XXIV.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
When both jaws are dislocated, they do not delay in being treated; and the dislocation of both can be done in one of two ways: either they are partially removed from their places by slight movement and slightly softened; or they are completely dislocated and fully separated, to the point where they are soft at the joint where saliva flows from the mouth and where it cannot be closed, and the tongue is wrapped in its place.
But when the dislocation is slight, it often returns to its normal position by itself with minimal intervention.
If the dislocation is complete and perfect, it must be reduced immediately, and not delayed at all.
And it is that the minister should take the lead in error, and insert the physician's two fingers of one hand into the mouth of the patient if the dislocation is on one side; or that he insert both hands' fingers together if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other said movements be done equally with them; and command the patient to cease their jaw movement and allow those to move from each part; and the physician should equalize the jaw and push it in until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated, then administer inhalation with hot water and oil until the reduction can be performed gently on both, and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And so when we return them and they are equalized, and the patient's mouth is closed and not relaxed, then apply pressure on those small pieces of cloth covered with wax, and let it be made of beeswax and oil softened; and afterwards it should be applied gently with ease using straps.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their head be placed between both ears, so that it does not turn to either the right or left side; and do not force them to chew for any reason; have their food cut with scissors until when the pains are alleviated and the jaws are healed, then allow them to eat as they wish.
And this should be administered gently, and do not work on the opening of the mouth for eating and drinking and speaking until the jaw is healed and cured.
And if the reduction of two dislocated jaws is difficult at any time and they are not returned to their places, fevers frequently occur and continuous sweats, and the belly of the patient is easily distended, and vomiting pure bile occurs easily.
And when you see this, then you know it is lost; and frequently such a one dies within seven days.
Chapter XXV.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and the extremity of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated from the chest within; and sometimes it is dislocated outwardly, and this is apparent by sensation.
And the restoration of it is that the patient lies on their back and extends their arms. And then press the area with your palm firmly, for it will return.
And then apply a plaster and powders and strengthen that area.
But the extremity of that which connects to the neck and is continuous with it is not dislocated on any day; therefore, it must be reduced and bandaged as we have said and as you have prepared.
And then apply a plaster, powders, and bandages, and command the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until they are healed.
And with this method of treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter XXVI.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, one of which is when it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
The second is when it is dislocated above the muscle, and this happens slowly; and it is not dislocated to the rear by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated to the front by the nerves.
It is most often dislocated outward below the tendon, and more frequently in those whose flesh is thin, it dislocates easily; but in those with thick muscles, it remains dislocated with difficulty.
And it often happens to some people due to a blow or fall that the muscle becomes swollen and inflamed, and as a result, it is likely to become dislocated.
Then it is necessary for you to understand this while you are in charge of the case, and from there proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation is known when it is below the tendon; thus, you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the normal one, as there is a clear difference between them; you find a depression at the head of the muscle below the tendon upon touching, and the injured person cannot move their arm to their ear, nor perform such movements.
If it is dislocated below the tendon or above it, you will then find a clear and obvious touch, and it is not concealed.
Of this dislocation there is made a light reduction when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the practitioner raises the hand to the upper parts; and then places gentle pressures below the joint, lifting the articulation firmly into its proper place, and the practitioner raises the hand, and extends it there; and then lets it go when it returns lightly.
But if it does not return as we have said, and the pain of the initial dislocation persists for several days, it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water and a poultice be administered to soften and relax it as if for rheumatism, using rue, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and let this be applied.
And then the patient is positioned on their back and placed below the joint while waiting for wool to be softened between levity and strictness, and then the physician places his thumb over the sphere, lifting the muscle upward with force, and pulls the patient's hand so that it is not injured below, and another assistant holds the patient’s head, so that it is not hurt below, because it will immediately return to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it in this manner: which is that you make a person present who is taller than the patient, and stand on the side of the affected limb, and insert their muscle below the joint of the patient and lift the joint upwards to suspend the patient in the air, and another assistant pulls the hand of the patient from below their abdomen. But if the patient is light, it is necessary that another person support them, so as not to strain that side, because the dislocation will return immediately.
And it is reduced in this way according to another method: and that is planted in the ground a long pole whose head is round like a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin, and then it is placed near the patient; once you place over the top of the pole lion skins, and the patient stands according to the length of the pole, and then his hand is stretched out towards the sides behind the other side; and his body is extended from the other side with strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if the reduction of it is difficult due to all the causes that we have mentioned, then use this treatment: and that is you take a pole whose length is two braccia, and its width is four digits, and its thickness is tapered at the tip according to this form:
And then over the round head place lion skins so that the pole does not hurt the patient.
And then it is placed near the patient's tip, and his hand or arm is stretched over the pole towards the sides, and the pole is wrapped around the assistant and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then place the arm on a ladder step according to its width, and the hand is stretched towards the sides, and the other body is suspended from the other side, for the joint will return quickly.
And when the instrument is completed according to each method that is possible for you, then place under the tip a pad of wool of equal quantity; and then apply a plaster prepared from powdered myrtle leaves and egg white over the entire muscle, as it has been described above, and then bandage tightly around the tip with proper tension; and wrap the bandage around the plaster, and attach the patient's hand to their neck, and leave it like this, and do not disturb the patient's hand for seven days.
When the food of the sick person has been restricted for a short time, so that the member is strengthened, it is easier to achieve healing.
And when the food is given more frequently, every seven or five hours, and movement is exercised; and thus if it is firm and not softened, then healing has already occurred.
If the joint is dislocated due to frequent exposure to moisture that occurs or for another reason, then it should be treated with cautery three times, as was said before in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after seven days the bandage is removed and the joint is not stable, and the plaster and compression are reduced several times, yet it is not firm and the hand cannot be raised to the sides, then you will know that the nerves of it, which control the muscle, have been severed or extended or softened, and you will understand that the joint will never again be fixed in its proper place.
Regarding the dislocation that occurs from the feet and the heel or the parts behind, the reduction is done with impulse and extension as much as necessary until it returns.
And the other compression should be administered to it, and care for it until it is healed.
And if heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement occur as healing progresses, then the patient should use baths several times until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its original nature.
Chapter XXVII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the collarbone.
Know that the joint of the collarbone is dislocated with difficulty and seemingly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, but mostly forward or backward. And the dislocation of it is not hidden from you when it is seen below the view or below the touch according to some figure it is dislocated, and when you adjust the dislocated one, that is clearly declared to you, for the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm nor touch its muscle.
And you should treat it, and reduce the dislocation as soon as possible before it becomes an abscess due to heat, for if it reaches that stage, its reduction is difficult, and it is not easily healed completely; especially when it affects the rear parts, for it is worse than all other types of dislocations, more painful, and many people die because of it.
And the restoration of it, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the hand be extended by two fingers, and the arm be expanded, and the physician's hand should be on the collarbone below it, and he should press the joint with his fingertips or with the palm of his hand until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is forward, then it should be returned by bending the hand with the palm, until it strikes the collarbone with the palm of his hand.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer vigorous extension and strong motion.
And it is that two assistants should extend the arm, and hold it firmly by both sides so that it is not moved during the extension.
And so the arm should be turned round on every side, because it is wrapped in a long doubled cloth or bandage.
And when the surgeon touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that this may be for those causes which help in the lubricity of the joint and ease of movement; and then press the joint forcefully inward until it returns.
And so for the reduction of the dislocated shoulder, place it on a splint which has stiffness and stability with an egg bandage, and bind it tightly in a descending manner, and hang the arm from the neck, and leave it like this for several days.
And then it should be loosened; and if the joint is formed in its proper place, now you may untie the bandage of it and leave it like that.
And if you see the joint not properly firm, then you should reapply the plaster and bandage, and leave it like that for several days until it is bound up; and then loosen it.
But if it happens that there is swelling around the reduction of the joint and its disposition is in motion, then administer hydration through bathing and gentle friction, and compression, until it is calmed; or place cold applications on the joint, as this is a condition of inflammatory movement; and then bind it up, and leave it like that day and night. And then lift that, and place it in bath water; when dry, you should rub the joint with moderate friction, and then reapply cold applications, as this is a sign of inflammatory movement, once, and the second time, and the third time, respectively. take an instrument for bathing, enter where it is clean.
And if you want, place a poultice over that moist and inflamed wound at the border and apply it; do this for several applications, since that area is moist and reduce it to its proper condition.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the wrist, which is the joint between the arm and the hand.
The dislocation of the hand sometimes occurs, and the reduction of this dislocation is easier compared to other joints. However, it is important that there be speed in reducing it at the hour when it happens, before swelling sets in or an abscess forms.
The reduction of the dislocation is done by placing the joint of the patient's hand on a table and having an assistant extend the patient’s hand. The surgeon then places his hand or palm over the vicinity of the joint and gently presses it until it returns to its proper place.
However, it must be considered whether the palm is already displaced towards the back of the hand. If so, the patient should place the backs of their hands on a table during extension and reduction; if the dislocation is towards the front of the hand, then the position of the hand should be such that it lies flat on the table, so that the surgeon's hand can apply to that vicinity of the joint.
And if it returns quickly, that is good; but if not, then apply a bandage to reduce the swelling and leave it like that; do not repeat this, for otherwise, you will neither endure nor be able to perform the reduction after a few days.
However, if the joint remains stiff according to its tortuosity; and you do not notice any other cause for pain, except that the muscle is softened and cannot grasp any cause; then you know that the nerves are cut or severed, and at this time no remedy can be applied there unless it be hardened by cauterization, which also helps remove some causes.
Then when the pain has subsided, you should pause on the bandage which we have recommended, and let it remain and harden for five days; then afterwards it should be loosened and left, and the hand should be exercised.
However, if the movement of it remains restricted and you encounter any gravity in it, then treat it gently with warm water and friction several times until it is relieved.
Chapter XXIX.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the said joint.
Sometimes the said joints are dislocated completely.
And when any of them is dislocated towards the thumb or towards the palm, then straighten the finger and apply pressure to its dislocation with your fingers until it returns.
And then hold the tip of the finger and suspend it above the part to which it is dislocated, and let it be so for two days.
And then suspend it and extend it until it is straightened on that day.
And when it is night, bandage it according to the aforementioned instructions, and do not stop loosening it during the day and exercising with movement, and bandage it at night; and do this for several days until it is strong.
If it appears thus, if it is dislocated from the palm of the hand, and join that to that part and do with this the first operation, until it be healed.
And similarly do with this when it is dislocated to the other parts.
Chapter XXX.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When it happens to any of the vertebrae of the back or neck that they are completely dislocated and the bones move out of their places, then there is no treatment for this, for death is feared for the patient.
And the sign of this is that the movement of the patient occurs without their will, and they cannot retain it; and often some parts of them or one side of them or two arms or one of these become weakened; or a vertebra is out of its place, as sometimes it moves and sometimes it returns to four places.
Then when it is moved to the rear parts, it is called kyphosis.
But the treatment for this is that you must consider whether the kyphosis occurred due to weakness, because then there is no cure and it cannot be fully healed.
But in the case where it occurs due to falling or impact or something similar to these, the Ancients have described methods of treatment at length, from which I will summarize what is essential; but I will abbreviate that part for which a little explanation suffices because of my proximity to understanding and exposition, with which I have made this clear and structured using various instruments different from those used to demonstrate and explain it.
And of those, it is not cured except for that which happens in the two properly as I mention.
And it is that the patient lie extended on his face on a bed equal to the wall, and be stretched below the operating table so that the legs not touch the ground.
And near a straight pole should be placed, fixed in the ground at the end of the bed by its head, and another pole around the feet at the other end of the bed.
And the minister holds the pole, and it should not be fixed in a pit; and another minister holds the other according to that form.
And near wrap around the head of the patient and below the shoulders tie a strong bandage; and extend the end of the bandage to the pole which is behind the head, and tie that to it; and then stretch the other bandage over the hips of the patient with the knees bent; and then adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other pole which is behind the feet.
And then the minister should extend the bandage to the side of the pole, and do not move the two poles and the bandages from their places in which they are fixed, but rather ensure they are not too tight as we have said.
And the physician should place his palms on the spine with strength until it returns to position, or place upon that a table, and then adjust the patient's feet on the table until they are in proper alignment.
If the patient does not return to this method, take a table whose width or length is about three cubits, and dig in the wall near where we said the patient lies a place into which the end of the table can fit. And place the middle of the table over the spine, and the physician places his weight at the other end, and gently strain it until the spine is compressed and returns to its proper place.
And if you wish, open a ring that is wrapped around the hand; and place the patient's head between the heels of two poles, the length of which should be one arm, and behind his head place the heels of two more poles in the same manner; and ensure there is a palm’s distance between each pole, and attach each pole to the ground so that it does not move.
And there should be a round pole in which is the ring, and it should be wrapped around the two ends of the two poles.
And at the end of it there is an end around which the pole is formed, the length of which is one palm where it is wrapped; and on the other two poles there is something similar to that.
And then strain the ropes that you tied to the patient's feet at the end behind his head, and the ropes that you tied around his shoulders at the ring behind his feet.
And then behind each ring should be a minister who turns the ring with his hand, and the physician should straighten the spine as we have said.
And after the spine is returned to its place and the area is leveled, it is necessary to apply a poultice of hot flour paste, and then cover it with wool; and afterwards place over the poultice a stick of wood whose width is three fingers or thereabouts, and whose length should be equal to the extent of the swelling, and on both sides of the spine.
And afterwards wrap it with the bandage we have mentioned, and use mild nourishment until he is healed.
And if any stiffness remains in the area at the end of the healing process, it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and lubricate along with the prolonged use of the board we have recommended; and a sheet of lead may also be applied to that location.
And at times stiffness occurs in the posterior part of the lower spine due to displacement; and that bone is such that it can lead to death if pressure is applied to it with this treatment, so one must be cautious not to do so.
Chapter XXXI.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the muscle attachment are subject only to dislocation, and do not suffer what happens to other joints with small movements and concavities.
And the joint of the hip can be dislocated in four ways: either inward, outward, forward, or backward.
And it is more seriously dislocated towards the interior parts, and slightly towards the anterior or posterior parts.
