The sign of the dislocation of the thigh towards the interior parts is that when you adjust the leg of the patient on the healthy side, it is longer and the knuckle is more prominent than on the healthy side, and the patient cannot bend their affected leg without pain, which is due to where the pus from an abscess has accumulated because the head of the bone has already reached there.
The sign of the one to whom a dislocation outward occurs is that the symptoms are opposite to those of the aforementioned condition.
The sign of the one to whom a dislocation towards the anterior occurs is that when he attempts to straighten his leg according to its full extension, it does not bend due to pain in the joint, and if he tries to walk, he cannot do so towards the front; and his urine is retained and his swallowing is affected by an abscess, and he walks on his heel.
The sign of the one to whom a dislocation towards the rear occurs is that he cannot extend the joint, nor can he bend it forward as much as when swallowing, and his leg is shorter than the other, and his throat is softened, and the head of the bone is in that location.
However, the reduction of these types of dislocation is that you consider whether the dislocation is old and ancient, in which case it has already settled in its position, so you administer reduction according to its disposition, but then it may not be entirely cured; therefore, it should not be expected to achieve full recovery at that time.
In the case of one in which the dislocation is recent, and it is one of the four species of dislocation, place your hand so that you can turn the joint and extend it to the parts within and without, and move it to the right and left, for it easily returns to its place and does not require other treatment.
If it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare someone strong and extend the arm from its side towards the rear, or place your hands under its armpits, or use a bandage which is laid over the upper part of the arm; and another person should extend their hand above and place their fingers below the elbow joints; and immediately apply a lion's ligature tightly around the shoulder joint, while having another person hold the end of the ligature.
And this extension of the arm or from the part inside to the angle of the shoulder blade, or from the rear part to the side.
And make the extension of all these in one motion while the patient is raised off the ground and remains hanging.
And this species of extension is common to all four types.
And so if the dislocation returns as we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for all types what I will say about its proper treatment.
In the reduction of the shoulder when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is towards the inside, the patient should be laid on their side; then place bandages around the shoulder joint at the location where the head of the humerus and the area below the angle meet; and then extend the bandage from the part of the angle to the farthest part of the shoulder blade.
Let another strong minister take the limbs of the patient and firmly grasp the place near the joint that is diseased and extend it forcefully to its parts outwardly through extension, for it returns to its proper place.
And this species is of the quickest cure among those to which this member is reduced compared to the others.
But if you excuse yourself and do not obey the treatment for this species of cure entirely, then it is necessary to bind both sides of the patient together with a strong, quick bandage over both heels and both toes.
And let the length of each one be the quantity of four fingers, and let the diseased side be extended more than the other by a quantity of two fingers.
And let the patient be suspended over the top of the beam, and hold it with his hand; and let the distance from him to the ground be the length of two arms.
And then command a strong youth to pull the head of the limb, and let another youth hold the patient, while this other youth pulls the limb with great strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But in the proper reduction, when it is dislocated to the outward part, let the patient lie on his back as we have recommended for lying down; and let the bandage be tightly applied to the diseased side properly and over his feet.
And then two beams should be placed, one under his feet and the other under his head; and then place beams adjusted to the middle of the body laid upon which wet cloths are wrapped, so that the patient is not harmed by the beam being between his limbs, so that during extension downward it does not pull him.
Let each attendant extend his part, and the surgeon with his art bring about the reduction.
And when the dislocation is to the front parts, then it is necessary that the patient be extended quickly, and according to this method described, lie on their back; and the surgeon places the palm of his right hand on the patient's thigh, and with one hand grasps that area firmly, and while doing so, extends the leg from the rear part towards the front.
And if the dislocation is to the rear parts, then it is not necessary for the patient to be extended towards the back, but rather raised off the ground; however, it is necessary that they lie on a hard surface, just as it is necessary when the hip joint is dislocated outwardly, as we have said about positioning the body on two planks; and it should be on their side with the limbs extended, as we have said, for there to be healing.
And it is necessary to apply compression to the table at the location of the joint.
Thus, when the reduction is complete as it should be — and the sign of hip reduction is not hidden from you, which is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see them equal and the patient extends their leg freely without pain, then you know that the limb has returned as it should — and now you adjust two things and apply a bandage to support and immobilize with a bandage so that the hip is not harmed in any part; and adhere the patient to rest for three days or four.
Then undo the bandage and the dressing, and compare one side with the other.
Now, if you see those marks on the skin, now you know that the dislocation has been fixed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any sign of inflammation, now you return and dress that area, and apply it according to your initial structure, and let it be for three days.
Then undo that [dressings/bandages], and allow the patient to delay in walking on each [affected area] for several days until they are comfortable.
Chapter XXXII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the knee.
The knee can be dislocated in three ways: it can be dislocated outward, inward, and backward toward the heel; it cannot be dislocated forward.
And the sign of knee dislocation is that you instruct the patient to apply pressure to the side; now you know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all forms of dislocation is to have the patient lie on their side, such that they can extend their leg as much as possible; place your hand against the affected area, and put the heel between your hands; then hold the knee with both your palms, and pull those firmly around the knee with strength, while another assistant extends the leg so that the knee returns to its proper position.
And the sign of successful reduction is that the leg adheres to the side with ease and without laborious effort.
Apply the plaster to it and make the cup adhere to the wound, then bind those with a bandage for three days or four, and afterward loosen those.
And do not use the limb for movement for some days until it is healed and comfortable.
But if the reduction of the thigh to the trunk is unnecessary, now administer extensive treatment with bandages, as previously described in the treatment of the hip.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle is sometimes displaced by slight movement, and at other times it is dislocated completely; the dislocation of it occurs either towards the interior parts or towards the exterior parts.
And the sign of its dislocation is that the ankle becomes swollen towards the part to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment for its displacement is easy reduction, and it should be extended with ease and gradually returned to normal once it begins to heal.
But in the treatment when it is completely dislocated, ensure that the limb is placed correctly, and let the patient have a strong and capable assistant behind him at the midpoint of the affected area.
And then place your right hand on the most superior part of it, and your left hand on the part below the foot at the location of the joint.
And then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with your left hand for two attempts, and then extend with your left; and push the toes of the foot with your right hand around the cup with effort.
In this place let two ligatures be made as we have counted.
And then press the feet of the patient to the third ligature, and you will be at the nail.
Therefore, if it returns in one ligature or in two according to this method, and if the foot becomes swollen, it is good; and otherwise, at that time repeat the operation, for it will return.
But if it is prohibited for you to reduce the swelling as we have recommended, then let the patient lie on their back on the ground, and place a roller firmly on the ground under them, so that it fits between their buttocks, and you have wrapped them in a cloth so that they do not slide.
And then the attendant should hold the patient's body, and another attendant should extend the leg with their hands or with bandages around the neck of the foot; and then each attendant should stretch it as they see fit, and the roller should be placed between the patient’s thighs to keep it in place so that the patient’s body is not pulled downward beyond its extension.
And then the surgeon should manipulate the dislocation with his hands, and another attendant should hold the healthy limb below, as the dislocation will return with ease.
And when the dislocation returns and you are assured of the successful reduction, now apply a plaster and tightly bind that with bandages, and secure the foot with bandages to the board.
And be careful of the nerves that lie behind the heel, so that the bandage over them is not too tight and does not injure them severely.
Let him stay like that for two days or three; and if the bandage is softened, remove it.
Then untie that on the third day or on the fourth, and forbid the patient from moving for forty days. But if he is forced to move before that time, there is no guarantee that the dislocation will not be dissolved and corrupted, and then it will not respond to treatment.
And if it happens that the abscess is cauterized, now you must administer according to the description in our narrative concerning the place for treatment and application, in another location for care and dressing, until it is healed.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the treatment of dislocation of the said foot joints.
It is necessary that the one who is dislocated rest with some extension without work; and what is not difficult is best done quickly.
But if the dislocation is in any of the joints of the two feet, now you must have the patient lie on a flat surface on the ground or on a table, and place them as if they were walking.
And then you stand on one foot, and place your foot on the joint that needs to be corrected, and then press it with your foot firmly until it returns to its proper position, and see that it is now in alignment and does not appear raised.
And then place the foot on a table that supports the entire foot, which has two ends, and then secure it with proper and firm bandaging for three days; and then untie it and have the patient rest from walking for ten days, until it is healed and recovery is assured.
Chapter XXXV.
Concerning the kinds of dislocations that occur from plague or fracture or any such conditions.
When any cause of those conditions occurs, and you proceed with the treatment of it and the restoration of it, often death results.
And because of this, one should not presume to treat it unless they are wise in the art, with long practice, merciful and compassionate, neither hasty nor presumptuous; and use medicines at first that only ease swellings, and leave the patient to divine judgment, but turn to him whom you hope will bring salvation from destruction or relief from illness, where some hope appears. And then strive to reduce it as soon as possible at the beginning of the cause, before it becomes a dangerous swelling.
And then if the member returns as we wish, immediately administer a regimen that reduces dangerous swelling and treat the injury with what is appropriate from among dry applications.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and occurs in the separated bones, then you should proceed with their management as we have said concerning simple injuries in those parts.
And guard and avoid, according to your virtue, the instrument in the ways of fear as my teaching has preceded; for this is better for you to serve your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more healthful for your true body, which will be in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis perfectly; may God be blessed for them. Amen.
